Having thus completed this book for you, which is the latest work on medicine with additions thereto, and having reached the end of it through his explanations and his own statements, it has seemed to me right to complete it for you with this Treatise, that is, with manual surgery.
Since manual surgery is neglected in our region and entirely private in our time, until perhaps the knowledge of it has perished and its trace has been lost; and there remain from it only a few descriptions in ancient books, which have been distorted by hand, and errors and hesitations have arisen until the intentions of his art have been closed and its sight has faded: I have thought it right to revive it with the order of this Treatise according to the way of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and so to come to the forms of cautery tools and the rest of surgical instruments; with the addition of declarations and the elaboration of what is necessary.
Moreover, the reason why a good master of this art with his own hand is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is long; and it is necessary for its author to exercise himself in anatomy before he narrated what Galen did, until he knows the members' helps, their forms, their union and separation, and has knowledge of bones, nerves, tendons, the number of them, and their exit, and the pulsations and quietness of veins and arteries, and the exits of places.
And because of this, Hippocrates said that the name is much with the name, and little with the work, especially in the art of the hand.
Indeed, we have already spoken of him at the beginning of this book.
For he who is not aware of what we have said about anatomy, will not succeed, but will fall into error, which harms men; as I have seen many among them who boast of this knowledge, yet lack understanding and experience in it.
This is because I have seen a stupid physician cut into a scrofulous swelling on a woman's neck and he injured some arteries in her neck; therefore the woman's blood flowed until she died in his hands.
And I have seen another physician who presumptiously tried to extract a stone from a man whose urethra had become blocked due to age, and the stone was large. He therefore approached him and extracted it with a urinary probe. Why the man died on the third day.
And indeed I had been called to extract it; and I saw from the size of the stone and the frailty of the patient that he would die.
And I saw another physician, who was receiving payment from a certain duke of our land for medicine. And there was a eunuch, black, who was with him; he had a fracture in his thigh near the calcaneum. The physician, through his ignorance, squeezed the fracture over the wound with lint and stitches, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
Then he released him according to his wishes. Afterwards, he released him after a few days and ordered him not to loosen the ligature. And he remained in that condition until his leg and foot were gangrened, and he came to his end.
So I was called to him, and I hurried to loosen the ligature; then peace came, and he was freed from his pains.
However, the corruption was already confirmed in the member; and I could not stop it.
So, as the corruption ceased to spread in the member, it perished.
And I saw another physician, who was probing the cancerous abscess; and after a few days, it became infected until the evil of the disease increased with its owner's condition.
This is because when the cancer is pure from a melancholy humor, then it should not be approached with iron at all unless it is in a member that can tolerate it so that the whole of it may be cut off.
And because of that, children, it is necessary for you to know that surgery, when divided with the hand, is divided into two parts: namely, the operation associated with health, and the operation where there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already discussed at length in every section of this book where the operation lies that carries fear and trepidation.
Therefore it is necessary for you to be careful and cautious so that you do not find foolish ways to speak and dishonor yourselves.
Therefore, take care with caution and protection, and tend to your patients with ease and firmness, and use the better way leading to grace from God for a commendable salvation and succession; and let go of terrible afflictions difficult to heal; and free your minds from what makes you fear, so that hesitation may enter into your faith and advantage.
For it remains more for your glory, and higher in this world, and finally for the extent of your power.
Galen indeed has already said in some of his precepts. Do not treat a bad illness; do not call yourself a bad doctor.
I have, however, divided this Treatise into three Chapters.
**First Chapter**
On cauterization with fire and acute medicine, this chapter deals with the forms and arrangement from head to foot, and the shapes of surgical instruments and cautery tools, as well as everything necessary for the procedure.
**Second Chapter**
On section and perforation, phlebotomy and venesection, and wounds: the extraction of arrows and similar foreign bodies, and all the chapter is arranged according to the forms of instruments.
**Third Chapter**
On restoration, dislocation, and treatment of torsion, and similar conditions, this chapter is arranged from head to foot according to the forms of instruments.
But before we proceed with the description of operations, it is necessary to speak of the qualities of healing and damaging factors, and in what types of wounds they manifest.
I say therefore that discourse on the quality of cauterization and its damage is lengthy and subtle, a knowledge and hidden secret.
Many learned men have also spoken on this topic and disagreed; for me, however, a brief summary of their statements is sufficient, lest I prolong the discussion.
I say therefore that cautery applies universally to every type of wound that involves matter or lacks it; excepting two types of wounds, which are the hot wound without matter and the dry wound without matter.
However, there is disagreement between them regarding the hot and dry wound with matter. Some said that cautery is beneficial for those wounds. And others said the contrary to him, namely that cautery is not suitable for a disease arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat, and it is unsuitable for healing from a hot and dry disease with heat and dryness.
And he who says the contrary to him replied: "Cautery is a remedy for every hot and dry disease that occurs in human bodies, because when you compare the human body and its humors to the fire's complexion, you find the human body cold.
And I myself say by his words: for I have already tested it several times from experience.
However, one should not approach it unless they have already been tested and practiced in the final chapter of cautery, and have understood the variety of human complexions, the disposition of diseases within themselves, their causes, the events that befall them, and the space of time for their duration.
From the other complexions, there is no need for fear, especially in cold and wet diseases. For indeed all doctors have agreed upon these things, and they do not disagree on the use of cautery for them.
And know, my children, that from the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and the goodness of it over cautery with medicated substances, is because fire is simple, whose action does not reach unless to the member to be cauterized, and it does not harm another member joined with it, unless in a slight way.
The action of medicine that fights against disease indeed reaches that which is stretched out from the body part, and perhaps causes difficulty in healing the disease in the body part, and perhaps it kills.
Fire, however, because of its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not cause it (damage) unless it becomes excessive.
Indeed, through experience, because of the length of service and study with art, and the focus on the truth of things, and because of that, I am excusing the length of this speech.
And if not, because it is not permitted to this book of mine, I will present to you a hidden secret about fire, and the quality of its operation in expelling bodies, by which it expels diseases through philosophical discourse, sharpened by your minds.
And know, children, that they disagree in the time when it comes to making cautery, and they set a better time for the wound.
And I say that cautery is appropriate in every time: because the damage caused by chance through time is submerged in the healing brought about with cauterization. And especially if cauterization is for necessary pains from strong, urgent pains that do not tolerate delay: because it is feared from them that a greater harm will occur than the slight damage caused by time's passing.
And let not that notion fall into your minds, O sons, which they do not appreciate, except for common and stupid doctors; namely, that the illness which is healed by cauterization does not always return: and they attribute its consequences, and the matter is not as they think. For cauterization is only where medicine alters the complexion and dries up the humidities which are the causes of pains.
However, cauterization exceeds medicine in the speed of its healing, and the strength of its operation, and the certainty of its mastery; and it is possible that the illness returns after a certain time according to the complexion of the patient, and the location of his illness, and its strength; and what is prepared in his body from the accumulation of superfluities in him. And it comes to itself in acquiring its causes from food and similar things, unless an illness occurs in which cauterization is administered.
A subtle illness in a member due to a few superfluities and humidities, such as cauterization on a tooth because of pain and similar things, is possible; for it may not return in that place the same pain. But that happens rarely.
In truth, the common opinion that cauterization is the last resort of medicine is a correct statement; not what they intend by it, for they believe there is no cure that can be achieved with medicine or any other means after the opportunity for cauterization has passed.
But the contrary is true; the cautery is the last resort of medicine, not unless when we apply methods of treatment to certain illnesses and they do not help, then we apply cautery at the end, which brings about relief. Thus, what is meant by the cautery being the last resort of medicine, according to popular understanding and many physicians, is false.
However, they said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron; and they did not say this unless because of the equality of gold and the subtlety of its substance; and they said that cautery does not cause pus in the wound. That is not entirely true; for I have already tested it and found that it does not work the same way in all bodies without others.
And cautery with gold itself is good, and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you use fire cautery with gold over fire according to the amount you desire because of the redness of gold, and if it is heated too much, it melts in the fire and turns into layers.
Therefore, the artisan falls from that work and because of it, cautery with iron becomes more convenient and closer to us, as a result of its suitability for practice.
And I have indeed now arranged this Chapter on Wound Care, according to the chapters: and I have ordered them from head to foot, so that he who seeks anything from it may find it.
**Head II.**
**Herein is described the cauterization that rules over moisture and coldness upon the brain: these two are causes of fever, and the great flow of humors from the head to the eyes and ears, and the abundance of sleep, and toothache, and throat pain, and in general all illness arising from coldness, such as paralysis, fever, epilepsies, apoplexies, and similar illnesses.**
**The method of cauterization is as follows: first, you must persuade the patient: to purge the head thoroughly with a cleansing medicine three or four nights in succession, according to the condition of the patient's strength, age, and habits.**
**Then instruct him to make incisions on his head with a razor.** **Then have him sit between your hands in such a way that he has already placed his hands on his chest.**
Then place the root of your palm on the root of your nose between your eyes. Whenever therefore your middle finger touches it, mark that place with an unhealed wound.
Then use a cautery with oil: which has this form.
Then place the same on the marked place with an unhealed wound, gradually lowering your hand, and you will turn it around. Then quickly withdraw your hand, and look at the place.
Which if you see has now removed from the bone the amount of the skull or the amount of the zinc, then withdraw your hand. And if not, repeat your hand with the same iron or another until you see the bones that I have told you.
Then add some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak the cloth in it, then place it on the place, and leave it for three days.
Then place the cloth soaked in butter on top of it, and leave it over the place until the scar from the fire is destroyed. Then it is treated with a four-ingredient ointment until it heals.
They also said that the deeper and more open the wound remains, the more pus it emits; therefore, it is better and more curable.
And some of them said that the skin should be cauterized from the mouth to the anus; and hold the cautery until something thick from the bone is burned.
Then it is smoothed after what is burned from the bone, then it is treated.
And others say that it is necessary to use a cautery until it leaves an impression in the bone so deep that the tooth falls out, which is done like a horn; or an instrument that women put into their vulvas when giving birth: and they think that vapors from this place are exhaled through the open wound over time.
Then it is treated until it solidifies.
However, these two types of cautery do not at all appear to me except in some people according to their fear.
And to leave it alone is better for my health.
The head becomes weakened by the dissolution of its natural continuity, as we have witnessed in the other members, and especially when the head is naturally weak and feeble, then the first species of cautery is more healthy and better for me, and I use it in my operations to preserve health.
Head III.
When a chronic pain occurs throughout the head and persists, becoming severe, it is treated with herbs, poultices, hot compresses, oils, and emollients. And especially if it has already been cauterized with one of the cauteries we have mentioned and does not benefit from them, then consider this. For if the weak head is of strong constitution by nature and is not feeble, and it suddenly feels a freezing cold, then cauterize below that point with another cautery, sparingly. Then cauterize the entire head with your cautery, without letting it penetrate the skin, and expose the amount from the bone as we have mentioned. And cauterize the center of the head with one cautery at the place called the "vas" (a term for a blood vessel or artery), which is the place where the flow is strongest, and raise your heat upon it so that you do not expose the bone. The patient indeed suffers from that constant pain, as well as various pains distinct from those of the other parts of the head.
And I will say that this cauterization should be done in its place: and it is necessary that there be a cauterizing instrument for the horns of the head, and a milder cauterizing instrument for the back of his head, through which the middle of the head is cauterized.
Head IV.
When a painful and persistent sensation occurs in a part of the head, or the pain extends to the eye, and it is drained with purifying medicines for the head, and the remaining treatment is administered as I mentioned in the divisions of ailments, but it does not alleviate the entire condition, then cauterization should be done in that area according to two methods: either cauterization with heated medicine burning it, or with iron.
However, cauterization with heated medicine burning it involves taking a tooth from one person and its own skin, and cutting off the extremities of it on both sides. Then, cut the place of pain with a phlebotomist's side, until a place beneath the skin where the tooth is caught, is formed.
Therefore, introduce it into the flesh itself until it is hidden; then tighten it with a delicate constriction and leave it for fifteen hours, then loosen it and extract another, and leave the wound uncovered for two or three days; afterwards, place a cloth soaked in butter on top of it until the pus has formed; finally, treat it with ointment until it heals.
And if you wish to do that with certain burning medicines, as I have written in the twelfth chapter of the book on drugs, then apply them.
Indeed, cauterization with iron is done in this manner. The burning cautery, whose form is as follows.
And it is called the claw cautery, because its form resembles a claw, in which there is something of curvature, and in its middle there is a small eminence.
Then place it on the spot and hold your hand, and slowly turn the iron with velocity; and the amount that is cauterized should be according to the thickness of the skin, as its halfway point. And raise your hand so as not to burn the artery below, which would cause blood flow.
Next, soak the bandage in a solution of salt water and place it over the wound, leaving it for three days. Then apply butter to the bandage, and afterwards treat with ointment until healed.
And if it be necessary, cauterize this hemicranium with the edge of a knife according to the art of cautery. And take care from the incision of the artery in this hemicranium, not too far or too shallow.
§ Concerning the method of cautery in ancient hemicranium.
When treating a hemicranium, as we mentioned in the previous treatment and in the division of ailments, and if this treatment does not help: and you see that the illness does not suffice with the first cauterization with medicine or with cauterization with fire, then it is necessary to make the cautery with a knife until it turns white after marking on the site of pain with a line, whose length is half a finger, or something like that; and place your hand once with pressure, and tighten it until you cut the artery and reach the jaw.
However, it is necessary for you to be cautious about the continuation of the jawbone, which moves during chewing, so that it does not burn the side or the nerve that moves it, from which spasm may occur.
And this should be above all a guard and caution regarding the flow of blood from an artery that has been cut. For in cutting, there is fear, and above all to him who does not know what he is doing, and is neither studious nor expert; and he leaves the operation with that first mistake.
And I come to the remembrance of the regulation of the flow of blood from an artery according to its own manner and in the place of this book.
If you see from the severity of the wound that this cautery is not sufficient for it, and you see the body tolerating cautery in the middle of the head, as we have described, treat the wound until it heals.
And if you see, administer cautery which we said in the chapter on extracting an artery with a cautery having two knives. For cauterization is indeed better and more effective with this method.
# On the method of cauterizing in the case of ear pain.
When ear pain occurs due to cold, and it is cured with warm compresses, and the remaining cure as we said in division, and the pain does not go away, then apply fire cautery; and that is called 'Punctum', which has this form.
Then, after the ignition of the point, it is applied around the entire ear, as it has been shown; or around both ears if pain is felt in both simultaneously. Then, using the cauterizing iron, stretch it from the root of the ear, shortly after you have marked the site with wax; and the amount of caustic should be ten points on each ear, approximately.
Then, take care of the wounds until they heal.
§ On the method of cauterization in surgery.
Chapter VII.
The surgery which is cured by cauterization is only of certain types that occur from phlegm, according to what we have mentioned in the division of diseases.
And for the species of cauterization that occurs from desiccation and spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this species of surgery with fire, and hot irons, and gargles, and it does not confer a cure, then you should cauterize the patient three times: first at the root of the ear, second slightly below its natural position, and third at the connection of the two lips.
And place your cauterizing instrument from the opposite side of the weaker part. For softening does not occur unless it is in the part that appears healthy.
And the shape of cauterization is such that you cauterize the very tip of the upper ear lobe just beneath the forehead's horn, and at another time; its length should be according to the length of the thumb. And place your hand with the cauterizing instrument until the amount that burns through half of the skin's thickness is consumed.
And this is the shape of the cautery, and it's a kind of knife, whose form I've described: however, it isn't too thin as you can see; and it should be that there's a little extra thickness to the knife.
Then care for the wound with what came before; until it heals.
# Regarding the ancient method of cauterization for apoplexy.
When apoplexy becomes ancient (or when, after following the aforementioned treatments and care, your condition doesn't improve and the patient does not have a fever), then cauterize them with four cauteries over all the horns of their head, and with a cautery in the middle of the head as I've said before, and another cautery at the nape of the head, according to what came before; and the manner of cauterization should be as previously described.
It is also cauterized with a cauterizer on the stomach's opening: and this is more ultimate.
Then it is treated with what came before.
Chapter IX.
It is necessary that in the early stages of a great disease of the hip, washes that cleanse the brain be given.
Then wash the entire head of the infected person, and place a plaster of cooked glue described in the treatise on plasters on their forehead, and apply it several times.
The same method is cauterization itself: and it should be done according to the same order that I mentioned there.
If therefore it is cured by that, it is good. If not: then perform three cauterizations on the back part of their head, which are descending from the upper part of the head to the lower part of the eye, and put between each cauterization a thickness of a finger.
Then care for cauterization, together with what preceded it.
If, however, you wish to add to it, and the patient can endure it: then perform cauterization in the middle of it. If you wish to add it: then cauterize the very top over the two horns.
Then care for the wound until it heals: and let cauterization be oily according to the form that has been described before.
§ Regarding the method of cauterization in paralysis and its soothing.
Chapter X.
It is necessary that you proceed with the purification of the head with hair, and with what we have said.
Then carefully clean the head of the patient: afterwards, cauterize the very top with hot iron in the middle of the head, and cauterize over every horn of the head, and cauterize over the back part of it, and apply three times over the spinal bones.
If, however, you need to soothe the body from many other ailments, and the patient can endure it, and the ailment is strong: then cauterize the very top with four hot irons over the spinal bones, and finish with the last iron cauterization, until much of it is burned from the skin, and draw your hand away. Then care for the wound itself until it is healed, and thus apply oil as a cauterizing agent.
**On cauterization in epilepsy.**
An epileptic should not be cauterized except for that whose epilepsy arises from phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary for the surgeon to clean the brain first with cauterization and with the rest of the care that we have mentioned in division; when the patient's condition is severe or he is tolerant of accepting medications.
However, if it is a child who does not tolerate medications, then gargles and brain-cleansing chews are administered for many days prior, with the aid of nutritious foods.
Then he should shave the patient's head, after which cauterize the wound with a single cautery instrument in the middle of the head, according to what was mentioned before; and another cautery on the back of his head, and over every horn of his head with cautery. If the patient is strong and tolerant, then he should be cauterized with the cautery instruments that I have mentioned while having paralysis, and softening of the body over the spine of the neck and the spine of the back; and the cautery should be done with oil as before.
However, if the patient is a young boy, then apply a fine cautery in this manner.
**On the method of cauterization in melancholy.**
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize them with cauteries, as we mentioned regarding paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess of sad thoughts and the body is weakened by moisture, then give it something to purify its brain, as mentioned before in separation; then shave the head of the weak person, and make afterwards a proper poultice from flax, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head: and the patient, sitting square, should be held from all sides.
Then apply the linen compress. And it is better from an ancient sheep's tallow. And heat it gently over a moderate fire, to the extent that the finger can tolerate when introduced into it. Next, apply a cool compress in the middle of the head in a circular motion and continue until it becomes cold.
Apply this treatment to the patient once a week, along with the rest of his regimen, until he is cured.
If it is yourself, cauterize it by making small punctures in most of it, apart from holding the hand with a cauterizing instrument; deep down, it smells bad.
This type of cauterization moistens the brain with its quality.
Next, place on it a cloth soaked in butter, or chicken fat.
**Regarding the method of cauterization with descending water.**
**Head XIII.**
When you have determined the beginning of water in the eye by the signs I mentioned in division, then begin and wash the patient, with that which will cleanse his head.
And keep it free from all moisture, and make it sit in a bath, for several days of fasting.
Then command her to shave her head and burn a scar in the middle of her forehead. Then burn them deeply with two irons if there is an accumulation of water in both eyes at the same time; or from one side, if there is an accumulation of water in one eye; and cut all the veins and arteries that are under the skin, and the burns should be long in width across the temples, and cauterize to stop the flow of blood.
If you see anything from this, then cut it off immediately with whatever medicine is possible, and I come with wisdom, which is in the extraction of arteries, cutting them off, and guarding against the flow of blood.
And when it is burned into the skin, under two bones, with the last two irons produced.
**On cauterizing in ancient tears.**
When the tears of the eyes are ancient, continuous, and flow over the veins and arteries that are clearly visible on the outer part of the head, it is because of the excesses of cold phlegm; then burn with the same iron used at the beginning when water descends, burn in the middle of the forehead, and use two irons on each temple, and two irons on the skin under two bones. And if you need to add to it, then cauterize it carefully on every side with a small cautery instrument above the end of the eyebrow.
On the method of cauterizing a nose ulcer.
When you treat it with what we mentioned in the previous section, and it does not respond to treatment; then apply a poultice of three nights, then shave the head and cauterize it with a medium cautery instrument and an olive cautery. Then cauterize it with two-sided cautery instrument above the two eyebrows slightly below the hairline.
And be careful not to injure the artery.
And this is the form of the clauis cautery.
On the method of cauterizing palpebral softening.
When the eyelid softens due to inflammation or moisture; then cauterize the eyelid with a single cautery instrument, this cautery should be in the shape of a crescent moon.
If you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows with just two cauterizing instruments on every part, and they will extend from two time periods; and the length of each cautery should be according to the length of the eyebrow, and do not use your whole hand with the cautery, but rather according to the amount that is burnt off of the third eyelid.
§ Regarding the cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs turn sharp towards the eye.
The cautery on it is according to two methods: either cautery with fire, or cautery with burning medicine.
However, in cauterization with fire, especially the sick person should be prepared beforehand to remove their hairs, if there are any that need to be cut, until they lengthen and equalize. If, however, they are pricked near their origin: then close the patient's eyes with a bandage, so that they do not move until they heal.
When they have healed and equalized, then place the head of the sick person in your lap: then make signs on the eyelids of the eye with wax, marking it according to the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the sign be near the hairs.
Then place a cloth soaked in albumen or in the mucus of psilia over the eye.
Then use a hot iron whose shape is like this.
Then cauterize over the shape that you have marked gradually on many wounds until the surface of the skin is burned, which appears like the shape of a myrtle leaf entirely own.
And the sign of health of your work is: if you notice that the eyelid of the eye is contracting again and the hairs rising up near the point of the eye.
So raise your hand then, and leave it alone for three days.
Then place a cloth with butter on it over it until the scar is gone. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
If any of the hairs return after an hour and the eyelid softens, then repeat the cauterization over that place as you did at first.
However, if the hairs are below: then cauterize it until it goes back to its natural place, and ensure that the hairs do not bother the eye.
It is necessary to urge the patient to pull out the hairs until they are extended and equalized. Make a shape of a myrtle leaf from a piece of cloth.
Then take a known amount of soap and some dry lime, about an ounce or something like it, and rub them together vigorously while applying pressure to ensure they mix well, being careful not to let it freeze. Then take the prepared piece of cloth in the shape of a myrtle leaf and place it over one or both eyes. And place under the eye a cold compress soaked in egg white, holding the head of the patient in your lap.
And place your index finger on the medicine and gently press it. And you should move it whenever the patient feels a stinging sensation from the medicine, for its sting is like fire.
While he persists in this bite that he has discovered, then abandon the medicine and move it with your finger. When the bite has ceased, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And then consider that if you see the eyelid lifting up as it does when it contracts with heat or a cut, it is good; but if not, then repeat the application of medicine to the place where it has left no mark or stain until your operation is complete and the eye contracted.
Then place a poultice of butter on it until the burnt skin is eradicated. Then care for it with a diaphoretic ointment or any other until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during the operation, to take care as the last step so that nothing from the medicine falls into the eye.
If after several days the eyelid becomes soft and something from the hair falls into the eye, then repeat the medicine on that place which is becoming soft from the eyelid, as you did at first. Then care for it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of humans differ in size and shape. Therefore, according to that, your consideration should be such that the way of righteousness is not hidden from him who studies this art.
§ On the method of cauterizing the lacrimal fistula.
When you treat a fistula, as we have said in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment does not bring relief, then you should cauterize it according to this method.
Tell the patient to place his head in your lap and hold his head with both hands in such a way that it does not move or be agitated while he performs this act.
Then place the heated cloth or a piece of mucilage over his eye: then apply the cauterizing iron, which has this shape.
It should be shaped like the curve of a reed's quill from one end, as is done with the cauterizing iron; and if it goes through to the other end or tries to go up, then it is inadequate.
However, this cut is better suited for your purpose.
Then express the fistula, if it is open, and drain the blood from it, and clean it; and if it is not open, then open it, and drain the pus from it.
Then place a hot iron on it, which is made to burn very much.
And hold it with your hand for a moment near the iron by the eye, to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not wander or disturb the patient; and place the hot iron on the fat of the eye, and destroy it.
If therefore you have reached the end of your hot iron up to the mouth, it will be good. And if not: then repeat the hot iron once or twice, as you need; and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cloth with butter, and treat it with an antiseptic ointment until it is healed.
If, after forty days, it does not heal: then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine to it until the bone is exposed; and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some have called the first. Intend to the location of the fistula and find it; then place a fine iron (i.e., a thin cannula) into that fissure. And pour into it the quantity of a drachma of melted lead, and hold your hand firmly with good retention of the cannula, so that it does not move at all, or flow, or reach the eye with the melted lead.
And it is necessary that you place over the eye of the patient a cloth dipped in either albumen or water.
Lead burns the site of the fistula and heals it with a wonderful cure.
Which, when the fistula is healed through cauterization and treatment as we have said, is good. And if that does not happen, then it is necessary to create a nasal opening and bring the fistula back to flow with the nose, according to where it naturally goes.
# On the Method of Cauterization in Lip Fissures.
Many times, cracks occur in the lip folds, which are called pilings, especially on the lips of children.
When therefore you treat these cracks, as we said regarding the division of the disease, and if treatment does not help, then use a small iron cautery in this manner.
And the depth of it should be according to the thinness of the knife.
Then place the hot iron quickly into those cracks until it reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then care for it with a poultice until it is healed.
**On cauterizing oral fistulas.**
When an abscess occurs at the root of a gum, or on the palate, or among the roots of teeth; it forms pus and breaks out; then it becomes gangrened and pus runs, and a fistula is formed; if treatment does not help in this case, then hot cauterization should be applied according to its size. Then introduce the burning steel into the fistula's opening and hold your hand until the burning iron reaches its deepest point and its end: do this once or twice with two strokes.
Then take care of it afterward, as we said about the treatment until it is cured.
If therefore the healing separates and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to remove the place and take away the corrupt bone as it comes to its head.
§ Regarding the method of cauterization in the gingiva and the softening of teeth.
When the gingiva softens due to moisture and the teeth become loose, and treating it with medicines does not help, then put the sick head in your lap.
Then apply the cauterizing fire, whose form comes after these steps, so that you place a tube over the tooth and quickly insert the burning cauterizing iron into it; hold your hand for a moment until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then raise your hand; afterwards repeat the cautery several times, according to need.
Then the patient should fill their mouth with salty water and hold it for one hour and then spit it out.
For the tooth's motion becomes fixed, the gums, having softened, tighten and thus the corrupted moisture dries up.
§ On the method of cauterization for tooth pain.
When tooth pain is due to cold, or there is actual cold within the tooth itself, or decay, and treatment with medicines does not help, then cautery should be applied in one of two ways: either cautery with butter or cautery with fire.
The cautery with butter is to take the raw butter and boil it in a copper kettle or in vessels. Then take a cloth, and apply it over the affected extremity. Then immerse the affected part in the boiled butter and place it eagerly over the painful tooth, holding it until it becomes numb, and repeat this several times until the strength of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
If you wish, immerse the cloth or bandage in cold butter, and place it on the aching tooth; then place a hot iron on top of it, until the fire reaches the depth of the tooth's cavity.
Its cauterization with fire is to aim for the air canal or the iron channel. And it should be in the body so that the point of the fire does not reach the lower jaw.
Then apply the cautery, whose shape will come together, and place it on that tooth; and hold your hand until the cautery becomes cold.
Do that several times, for the pain will be moved, or on the same day, or on another day.
And it is necessary that the impression of that cautery fills the patient's mouth with good butter, and holds it for a certain time, then spits it out.
And this is the form of the cautery.
Cauterize with any available point of the instrument according to what is possible, and this is the form of the cannula.
On the method of cauterization for styes.
When styes are affected with phlegm and cold humours, and do not yield to the operation of medicines, nor permit their dissolution by heat, then apply a hot cautery of this form.
It should have two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end as the cautery is applied to the other; then place the glowing tip on the wound once, and a second time if necessary, until it reaches the depth of the wound.
If the wound is shallow, then apply the cautery according to its depth.
Afterward, leave it for three days, and apply a poultice dipped in butter on top until the charred part is removed by the heat.
Then care for it with a dental ointment until the shape of the canal is healed.
On the method of cauterization for hoarseness and difficulty in breathing.
When moisture is collected over the lung channel, and especially when it is accompanied by the coldness of the skin, then it is necessary for the patient to be evacuated with the first use of laxative medicines.
Then, he should cauterize the same at the base of the throat in a depressed place, and be careful not to come across the cautery with it to the throat or skin, unless something of its own.
Then, he should cauterize the same with another cautery at the joint of the neck in the last spine of it finishing: and let the cautery be made into a clasp according to the manner that preceded.
Then he should care for it with what preceded until it is healed.
§ Regarding the method of cauterization in lung diseases and coughing.
When coughing and lung disease are caused by cold moisture, and the patient has neither fever nor phthisis, nor is the illness old: then he should cauterize the same with two cauteries above the furca in depressed places, gentle, smooth. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples: and let the cautery be made into a tube according to the form that preceded.
And if your cauterization by puncturing with a cauterizing instrument, called the *Punctum*, precedes the pain of the ear in puncturing; and there are punctures from thirty punctures as if to another.
Then care for the wound itself with what preceded it, until it is healed.
And now it has three branches according to this form. Why should cauterization be done with it when you make three cuts with it at once?
Chapter XXVII.
When the head of the helper is dislocated due to moisture and does not form itself back to its original position within the allotted time, until it has become accustomed to returning, then it is dislocated by a slight movement, which happens as we have already testified, at that time you must reduce the dislocation in the first instance. Then the patient should be placed back on his back or side, over the healthy side. Then lift the skin, which is inside the *Titillico* towards the upper parts with your left hand's fingers; and if the joint dislocates to the interior side.
Then, having a fire-brand, he holds two vessels. What is the form of this?
Then he cauterizes the skin, until it penetrates to another side, and the figure of the cauterization joins with four fire-brands.
And sometimes the cauterization is made with a fire-brand that has three vessels, why then the figure of the cauterization becomes six fire-brands; and the vessels are according to the thinness of what is attempted.
And this is the form of the fire-brand that has three vessels.
And sometimes one is added beyond this manner, why then there are eight fire-brands.
Then he places crushed salt on the fire-brand. Then he makes the injured part adhere to tranquility, so that the member does not move until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is to higher parts and it happens rarely, then he cauterizes itself on the shoulder with a fire-brand of a key, or with many points of fire-brands.
When the union is strengthened and moisture is removed, the sick person is healed.
**On the method of cauterization of the stomach.**
When cold and great moisture happens in the stomach, until they extract it from its condition, and phlegm is multiplied towards it, and persistent treatment is required, and no relief is afforded, then the patient should lie down on his back, and extend his legs, and his hands. Then cauterize with three cauteries, a cautery under the shape of a scallop in quantity equal to one finger, and two cauteries below the two sides of the single cautery, until the figure of the cauteries becomes triangular. And stretch between each other so that they do not join when pus is formed.
And the depth of the cautery should be the amount of two-thirds of the thickness of the skin.
And the figure of the cauteries should be according to this form without addition, and if you wish to cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form: and let the cautery that is made for this cautery be one according to the figure of a circle according to this form.
And the stomach is cauterized by puncturing it which is troubled by this cautery; and it is as if signs are made on the stomach with punctures according to the quantity, as when wax is incised with encaustic; then it is cauterized itself with the cautery of the puncture; then it is cared for with the cure that preceded until it is healed.
On the manner of cauterizing with cold.
When pain occurs in the liver due to cold and moisture, or because of a thick excess of humor, until it is freed from its natural excessive discharge, and the patient is cured as we have said, and it does not benefit him; then he should lie back on his own head, and make signs with encaustic in three cauterizations according to this figure, and the same quantity below the epigastrium from the hypocondria where the elbow of the man reaches: and let there be a space between every cautery according to the thickness of a finger; and let the cautery be straight according to the length of the body; and do not press your hand much with the cautery.
And let the quantity of that which is to be cauterized be according to the thickness of the skin, half of its quantity not added; and let the patient stand on his feet.
If, however, he is not standing, then let him lie down, who has already extended his legs and raised his arms.
This is the form of cauterization.
And it is indeed possible for you to cauterize these wounds with the cautery knife when subtlety and caution reside within your art.
And take care not to proceed too far with cauterization, so that the thickness of the skin throughout is not too severe, which could affect the whole body and reach the intestines: since the skin there is thin, therefore be aware of this.
§ Concerning the method of cauterization, proceed with caution regarding the liver.
When it happens, and you need to know if that cautery mark is on the liver or its membrane, then if it's on the liver, a severe pain arises without any acuity, and gravity takes over; but if it's on the membrane of the liver, intense pain accompanies it, and you now see that their treatment has exhausted them.
Therefore, the patient must take back his weight on his head. Then, the shape of the cautery mark with wax is made; then, ignite the cautery tool that resembles a flame. And this is his form. And it should be burned with one cauterization until the whole skin is consumed by fire, and it reaches the siphon, until all the pus flows out; then care should be taken for the cure of the ulcers, until it is healed.
And this method of cauterization should not be used unless one is skilled in the art of medicine and has dealt with these illnesses many times before; then, and only then, should he dare to perform such an operation.
To release it, I prefer to do it myself.
## On Cauterization for Pleurisy.
The ancients said that cauterization with the roots of aristolochi should be used for cold pleurisy according to this method. And it is, as you receive the roots of dried aristolochi, one root longer, which is found from that, and it should be like the thickness of a finger. Then dip it into oil, and light it on fire. Next, he cauterizes with one cautery the space between the fork's continuation and the neck, and with two small cauteries beneath the guides at a slight decline towards the part beneath the beard.
And with two cauteries also in the space between the fifth and sixth ribs towards the rear a little, and another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from the rear: one in the space between the two blades, and two cauteries from the two sides of the chest, below the cautery that is between the two blades.
And it is not necessary to press your hand with the cautery, but rather it should be placed openly on the skin so that it smells.
And certain ancient people have said that there are men who use cauteries made of similar iron radii: they ignite them and insert them into the space between the ribs until they reach the same point as the cautery, and then blood flows out, as we said about the hepatic apostema.
But in this cauterization, there is a risk that either the patient dies immediately or an ulcer occurs at the site, which does not heal.
**On Cauterization: The Splen (Spleen) Head 32.**
When you treat an illness of the spleen, because of what we said about dividing the illness from the treatment, and your treatment does not benefit, then there is cauterization in it according to three ways, all of which are correct.
One of these is to make three or four incisions along the length of the spleen according to the shape of the smaller livers (the ones that precede in order), whose shape should be followed.
And for every incision, the thickness should be that of a finger, or more or less.
And the manner of incision, which we said in the liver incision, should be equal.
And do not press your hand down with the incision; let the patient lie on their back, weak and relaxed.
And another mode of cauterization is: to have the fire for the incision with two veins, as we said in the chapter on dislocation of the humerus; and lift the skin that is directly on the spleen where the lower left cubit arrives, and your lifting should be according to the width of the body so that the incisions fall according to the length of the body.
Then insert two hot instruments deeply until they penetrate the skin on the other side; then extract the cautery; so the four incisions are made.
And if you use cautery with another instrument holding three veins.
Then, after you have released the pus for many days, care for the cautery; for it is more beneficial than any of the treatments that preceded it.
§ On the method of cauterization in hydrops.
The cautery should not be applied to hydrops unless the swelling is properly drained.
When you treat a hydrops using the methods we have described in division, and the treatment does not improve, then you should apply four cauterizations around the navel, one cautery on the stomach, another on the epigastrium, one on the spleen, two behind the back between the vertebrae, and one directly on the chest, and another directly on the stomach; and let the depth of the cauterization be close to the thickness of the skin.
Then leave the cautery open so that it can release pus over time.
And do not withdraw the weak from treatment after cauterization with it, for it should have two intentions; and it will quickly be healed with God's help.
The shape of the cautery instrument with which the stomach is cauterized should be curved; it should follow the shape that preceded it. And the shape of the one with which the back is cauterized should be oily; it should follow the shape that preceded it.
**On the method of cauterizing feet and crural areas.**
When they are soaked in hydropic (a medicinal solution) and replenished with citrine water, one should cauterize over the back of the foot at the instep, which is between the ankle bone and the little toe. Then raise your hand holding the cautery, without twisting it, and afterward raise your hand again, without touching it at all.
The citrine water will then revive, and the cautery should be applied in this form.
Then make two cauteries on both crural areas, one at the top of each leg, and let the edge of the cautery be a knife-like point; make another cautery along the length of the leg, one below the knee and the other lower down, roughly in the middle of the thigh; and make two cauteries over the entire hip. Leave the open cautery without treatment for a long time, so that some water can heal from it; then care for the rest as we have mentioned.
**On the method of cauterizing in the abdominal solution.**
Chapter XXX5.
When the resolution of the entrails from cold and humors is delayed until the vital forces of digestion, which are in the stomach and intestines, are weakened; and it is treated with methods of treatment, but does not heal, you will see the patient enduring the heat of a strong vitality, then cauterize itself with a great cautery on its own stomach, as was done previously in the cauterization of the stomach with a circular cautery, and four small cauteries around the navel, with a fine cautery; and a cautery over the liver above the ribs, or two cauteries.

Indeed, healing is successful whose aid does not waver.
**On the method of cauterizing hemorrhoids.**
Chapter XXXVI.
When there are many old hemorrhoids, or one large one, arising from thick, cold, or corrupt humors, and they are treated as we have said in division, but do not benefit from the treatment; then cauterize with three cauteries over the lower spine below the navel slightly triangular, and a cautery under the navel like two fingers.
If you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face turn pale, then cauterize him over the stomach with a large cautery as has been previously described; and with another cautery over his epigastrium, and another over his spleen with a claw cautery; and leave the open cautery for some time. Then take care of him until he is healed.
