**On the treatment of water accumulated in the heads of children.**
**Chapter I.**
Know that this ailment often occurs in children at the time of birth, or when the midwife tightly squeezes the child's head without gentleness.
And whenever something happens, even due to an unseen cause that is unknown.
And indeed, I have not seen this disease in others as much as in children, and to every one of them death has followed: therefore it seemed to me necessary to give up the operation on it.
And indeed, I have seen from them a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was magnified every day until he could not sit on himself because of the size of his head; and the humidity increased until he died.
And this humidity, either it accumulates between the skin and the bone, or it accumulates beneath the bone on the surface.
And the operation on it, if there is humidity between the skin and the bone and an abscess is present, then you should make one crack in the middle of the head in width; and the length of the crack should be like two knots, until the humidity runs out.
And this is the form of the abscess.
If therefore the humidity has increased more and the abscess is larger, then put these two cracks according to this form.
And if the humidity is beneath the bone and its sign is that you see the openings of the commissures of the head from every side; and water sinks down when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and it does not hide itself from you, then you should make three cracks in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after the cut, remove all the moisture. Then tighten the wounds with cloth and lint. Then sew it up from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the ligature, care for the wound with bandages and ointments.
And do not release the tension from the head equally. And feed the weak with dry food containing little moisture, until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of cutting as you consider where the magnitude of the abscess appears and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is in the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right side, or on the left.
So rise up with the cut where you see the abscess and the collection of water, and split it according to what is possible for you.
And beware not to cut the artery, and let there be no flow of blood, and the weak may die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
On the incision of arteries that are behind the ears.
**Chapter II.**
When someone suffers from a catarrh affecting the eyes or chest, and it becomes chronic and does not respond to medical treatment, then the final remedy lies in the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when one decides to incise them, they should ensure that the diseased head is removed. Then, using a rough cloth, one should create a space so that the arteries become visible. Then, the diseased neck should be tightened with any cloth available. Then, consider where the pulse of the vein is located. And the locations of both [arteries] are the areas behind the depressed ears; they rarely become hidden, except in certain people.
Then, mark them with wax on both sides; and incise each one with a sharp scalpel on both sides accurately down to the bone; and it should be done according to the width of the head.
And if you wish, introduce a spatula beneath the artery and cut it towards the upper parts with an incision.
And the length of the incision should be like two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood flows out with pulsating force, rising into the interiors with frequent jumps.
However, if the artery is not visible to the eye, then it is necessary that the distance from gold should be the quantity of space equal to three fingers. Then mark with wax and follow until it reaches the bone.
And what needs to be released from blood is six ounces and perhaps you release more or less from it. All of this according to the amount that appears to you from the condition of the patient and the fullness of the artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if there remains anything near the bone from healing, cut it so that there is no risk of a hot abscess; then tighten the wound with linen bands.
Then care for the wound with ointment until it heals.
On the extraction of arteries, which are found in certain parts of the body.
Head III.
When an old headache or sudden acute catarrh occurs due to the harsh humidities and heat in the face, or because of a sharp object such as a splinter, and similar causes, and treatment with the usual medical methods does not help, then we have to resort to the extraction of arteries from these parts, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of operation for extracting them is to remove the weak hair from the part, and pull towards the visible artery in that time. It is indeed shown to you from its pulse, and it is rarely hidden unless in a few people or due to coldness.
If it is hidden from you, then press the weak head with any cloth you have; afterward, use fear to apply a rough cloth. Or use steam with hot water until it appears manifestly to you. Then take a knife that I formed a little while ago. Then use it to scrape the skin easily until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it and pull it towards the upper parts until you extract it from the skin, and make it pass through the tunics that are under it from all sides.
If, however, the artery is thin: then pierce it with the tip of the hook. Then make an incision from that part according to the length of its ends, and tighten it, so that there is no flow of blood. For if it is not incised and not severed, the blood will not flow completely.
Then take six ounces of blood from the vein until three ounces remain.
If, however, the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong double thread, either from silk or from some other string, so that it does not flow into putrefaction before the wound heals and the blood flow occurs.
Then make an incision on the surplus, which is between the two ligatures. Perform that operation at that time, or after another hour.
And if you see it, cauterize it with a hot iron from the mouth to the end, with a cautery knife; until its ends are cut off and it becomes firm in that place through the same operation.
And it is better work, unless the patient has fever or is of heated complexion. Because the cautery is from those things that dry up humors. Therefore it is better drainage.
And after the arteries are extracted, the place must be filled with cooked fat; and small pieces of well-made cloth should be placed on top of it.
And after the wound is dressed with dried medicines that promote the growth of flesh, and with bandages, until it heals.
If, however, while you are operating, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then quickly proceed to cut it off with a cautery, or fill the place with a plug; and hold your hand tightly until the blood stops. If none of those things is present to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood clots. And it embrows the site with a steady application of cold water until the acute pain subsides and it tightens as necessary.
And because it is easier to extract arteries from a softer and more pliable part, it is done by cauterizing the vein with these two instruments, and the sharpness of both knives should be similar. However, they have much less sharpness than the two knives. Because if they are as sharp as the two knives, both will apply coldness, and they will not cut into the flesh swiftly. Therefore, since they have a little less thickness, heat from fire is retained in them and flesh is cut swiftly; so with this, the work needs to be done better, easier, and lighter of all works.
And this is the form of cautery.
And there should be a space between the two knives equal to the thickness of a finger, after you mark with encaustics two places; and apply fire vigorously to both until they reach the wound, and cut the vein at both places: so as to elongate what is between its extremities. For they do not solidify completely with this cautery.
**Head IV.**
On the treatment of continuous acute tears from the eyes.
When tears are continuous and flow from the eyes, which are situated on top of the skull, and no medical treatment brings relief, while you see the face of the patient turning red and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead like that of ants walking, and his eyes become strained, wet, to the point that their hairs are damaged by the constant flow, and the eyelids are inflamed from the intensity of the tears, then the ancients employed a special method for treating them, calling it 'the operation with the knife'.
And it is as if you instruct the patient to shave the hairs on his forehead. Then make three incisions evenly spaced along the length of the forehead, and the length of each incision should be about two fingers, with one incision placed in the middle of the forehead at the level of the nose, the second a little below the movement of the eyebrows, and the third on the other side. Be careful to avoid the arteries located on both sides. Extend your hand from the continuation of the lower jaw, and the distance between the incisions should be about three fingers. Prepare a piece of sponge or a dry cloth, with which you will absorb the blood.
Then insert the blade of a sharp instrument at both ends, after which comes the shape. From the incision which is from the temporal part, to the second incision: and the scar, together with it, all the skin which is between the two incisions, with the heal, which is over the mouth. Then do that from the median incision to the second incision as well. Then extract this scalpel and insert another instrument into the first incision again, which is called a sharp knife, with one side blunt, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side against the flesh attached to the skin, and its blunt side towards the mouth; and push it until it reaches the median fissure, and cut along with it all the tendons that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until what appears of the skin.
Then do that in the median fissure until another fissure.
And when it flows from blood in an equal amount, express the places from which the coagulated blood has formed a clot.
Then put in every fissure lint made from old cloth, and place over them rags soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And observe that there is no risk of a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligature and administer a large amount of the infusion with warm water. Then apply that care with the ointment of basilicon to the rest of the wounds, until they are healed.
Head V.
§ On the care of acute tears and cataracts on the eyes from internal head injuries.
When a person suffers from acute, intense, and persistent cataracts, you will notice their eyes are exhausted, weak, and their appearance is already debilitated; the eyelids are ulcerated, hair falls out of them, and there is a deep pain in the head, painful and causing subsequent sneezing. Then, from these circumstances, one must understand that these materials do not come out of the eyes unless through many channels.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is, that you should advise the sick person to scrape their forehead. Then make one cut in that fissure in the middle of the forehead, or slightly beyond its width, and begin with the cut from the left side towards the right temple; continue the incision until it reaches the bone; then extend your hand away from the temporals, which move during chewing, until you reach the jawbone; remove all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate it between the two lips of the fissure with a hot iron or with linen lint. Then tighten it from above with small threads and moisten the threads with wine and oil so that there is no risk of a hot abscess.
And when you dissolve it and see that the hot abscess is already approaching, then you must grind your teeth until the flesh begins to grow there.
Then take care of it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh, as if you were to take two parts of flour from wheat and four parts of colophony, prepare a paste from them and apply it to these wounds to promote the birth of flesh.
Head VI.
§ On the care of that which falls into the gold.
Everything that falls into the gold belongs to one of four categories: either a mineral stone or something similar to stone, like iron and glass; or from a plant, such as wheat and beans; or a fluid substance, like water, vinegar, and others similar; or some kind of animal.
When something that resembles a stone or belongs to the stone family falls into the gold and does not add to or grow in it, expose the gold to the sun alone. If you then see the stone, distill some of the molten oil or essence into the gold. Then he should perform the extraction of the foreign body, and make the patient sneeze when ready, and press on the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you place a piece of cloth or wool around the ear area and stretch the ear towards the upper parts.
Indeed, this treatment should be repeated many times.
However, if it does not come out, then perform the extraction with the use of poultices, that is, subtle compresses made from pizigari (a traditional medicinal herb), whose form is as follows...
If therefore it comes out with the poultice, it is good.
And if not, then perform the extraction with a delicate, slightly curved hook.
If therefore it still does not come out through that (method), then make a tube from metal and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal deeply, and press the material around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so as to block any passage except for the tube; then extract it with your strong pulling force.
Indeed, it often comes out with what we have described. If however it does not come out in that way, then take some helkalimb (a type of herbal preparation) or from a viscose (prepared glue), with which birds' feathers are caught, a small amount, and place it on the tip of a radius after you have carefully wrapped a clean cloth around it; then insert it into the ear canal easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have mentioned, then proceed to divide it, before a hot apoplexy or spasm occurs.
And the manner of the incision is, as the phlebotomist makes an incision in the weakest part of the head; and he draws out blood from it according to the measure of its strength. Then place the weak part between your hands, and turn its ear towards the upper parts, and find a small incision at the root of the ear, near the fat, in the depressed place; and make the incision in the shape of a crescent until you reach the stone; then remove it with what is possible for you among the instruments.
Then use that incision with speed from your hourglass, and take care of it until it heals.
If the matter falling into the ear is from one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If, however, it does not obey you for its exit, then take a thin, delicate forceps in this shape.
And administer with it the cutting off of that kind from the grains that fall into the ear, and do not do that when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place many turns on it; then remove them with hooks, or with a delicate gesture, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction to be relieved.
When water is introduced into the ear, it is necessary for the patient to use sneezing with the initial conditioning; and he has already filled his ear with some cooked wax. And he lies over that ear where the water is. If it therefore exits through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many small stones, according to the length of your finger, thin and soft, and heat them with fire briefly, and let the patient insert one of them into his ear opening; and he should lift up from that side a little on his foot, and strike with another stone the stone that is in his ear. He should therefore continue doing that with the stone and the stone until all the water has exited.
And the water will exit in such a way that you take one from the hot water or the steam and insert its end into his ear; and light the other end with fire until it burns most of it. Then he should repeat the process with another cloth, and do that many times until all the water has exited; and then extract it with a tube as mentioned earlier in the stone.
When extracting the animal that has entered it, if it is a small body, like a flea or something similar, treat it as I said regarding division.
But if there is a large body that appears to the senses, then perform its extraction with gestures and hooks, for it is easier than all those held in gold.
However, when treating a spine generated in gold, if you take care of it as we said in division and distillation, and it does not respond to your treatment, then you should look at the gold in the sun. If something appears there from the nerves, then extract it with gestures or with delicate hooks.
However, if nothing appears there from the nerves, then take a tube whose form is tight below and wide above, and insert its thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate; then suck with it using strong suction. Do that many times until all the nerves come out.
However, if they do not obey you to come out, then wrap the catheter around with wax, as I said on the stone.
If they do not come out with what I said, then perform distillations that the ancients are experienced in for the intersection of nerves, and you will find it in the treatise on distillation.
And let your discharge be as you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, the form of which is depicted here. As you can see, make this one out of silver or bronze, tightly folded below, with a small opening on the inside and a larger one on top.
It should therefore be made such that it can push what is in the cavity of the tube; and it should be made neatly out of bronze. And if you wish, take a radius and insert its coated tip into the tube with proper lubrication; then pour oil or syrup or any of these medicines into the tube, and place it in the ear. Then insert the radius with the coating above, and express it gently with your hand until the oil is forced into the cavity of the ear and feels warm inside; but be careful not to allow anything cold to drip into it, for medicines do not tolerate that.
**Head VII.**
**On the Care of Treating an Injury to the Ear.**
Sometimes certain infants emerge from their mothers' wombs that have not been circumcised.
And indeed, it has also happened to some people that their ear tissue is damaged at birth from a wound or flesh growth within it.
And this operation is sometimes performed in a deep hole in the ear, which does not follow the usual method; and sometimes it happens on the outer part of the opening, and then it follows the usual method.
Therefore, what happens in the deep ear, and which does not fit within the scope of vision, is difficult to cure in many cases.
And in those that follow the usual method, it is necessary to place a warm compress over the ear and examine it; then, if you see the operation appearing, open it with a fine probe, whose shape follows this, with its end where there is some small width, and one part of it acute; and the rest of the probe should be smooth on both sides so as not to hurt the ear.
If therefore the operation is caused by flesh grown in the ear canal, then hold it with a fine hook and cut it away easily until all the flesh is removed.
If the operation is in the deep ear, then take a thin, flexible probe and heat it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel the operation with what the patient feels with the weight of hearing, then try to open it easily; and be careful not to wound the ear's nerves.
Then place in the ear a quantity of lint according to the size of the wound you have cleaned with Egyptian ointment: do this for several days until you are free from the healing of the wound.
Or take the lint, and soak it in rosewater, then sprinkle over it powdered zinc, and use it.
If you see that the ear has already formed a crust, then it is necessary to remove the lint and allow it with the new lint you apply in the paste made with rose oil, until the crust is warm, then take care of it until it heals completely.
If, however, there is bleeding: then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it over the ear, and use the remaining cure that stops the flow of blood as described in the treatise on distillation.
**On the care of the eyelids.**
**Head VIII.**
These are the conditions that occur in the eyelids of the eye when they are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore, it is necessary to hold them with tweezers or with a hook, and cut them with a scalpel from their roots. Then place a tri-cut veil over the wound.
However, if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a thin lens-shaped cauterizing iron.
And cauterize them, it is better after the incision. Indeed, they often return when something remains from its roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
§ On the care of the eyes' grand muscles.
Chapter IX.
Often it happens in the eyelids that something resembles the grand muscle in its strength and hardness, and because of that it is called Grandinus: and there is a thick aggregation of moisture in the upper eyelid and lower eyelid.
The operation on them is therefore to consider at that time, if the grand muscle proceeds from it, which appears from the eyelid, and moves to every part, then its condition is easy.
Therefore, find the crack over it in width. Then he should scrape it from all parts with ease until it is healed. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
However, if you cannot prepare to cut it off except after you have pierced the eyelid with an incision slightly, then it will harm the weak patient somehow.
If the cut is deep: then sew it up and care for it until it heals.
And if it is minor, it will not cause you any harm from that. Blood will restore it and make it flesh again.
And if the decline is great towards the inner part of the ear away from the surface, then turn the eyelid inside out and hang the swelling with a hook; besides needing an incision, press it from all parts.
If the eyelid is elongated by an incision, it will not harm anything.
Then wash the eye after cutting the swelling with mixed water and care for the place so that it becomes flesh again until the sick person is healed.
On the care of the axirna (eyelid) palpebrae (tissues).
**Chapter X.**
The axirna (eyelid fold) is a thickening that occurs in the upper eyelid skin. This most often happens to children, and it makes their eyes water in the morning, preventing them from seeing sunlight due to the tears, so they constantly rest their eyelids on their faces and sleep with them open, and the axirna becomes prominent and visible to the eye, and when you press on the area with your fingers, you feel the axirna between them.
And the method of its operation is to place the patient's head in your lap.
Next, take a linen cloth and prepare it with care, making a circle from it according to the amount that contains the axirna from all sides. Then place it over the axirna and press with your fingers from all sides so that the axirna is gathered together in the center of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that humidity with a spatula, opening the cracks across the width, and let there not be a deeper crack than that made in phlebotomy.
You must find the skin entirely, until you reach the axira, for indeed the axira proceeds from the fissure according to the shape of the white, swollen fragment.
Therefore, extract it with the cloth that you have inserted between your fingers towards the exterior, and turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed. Be careful not to add to the fissure, so that you can reach with the instrument to the eye, and do not injure it.
However, if the axira does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, add a little to the fissure with ease until the axira proceeds. Then extract it as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the site, and bind it with small strings.
And there are people who rub salt on it, and they place it into the depth of the fissure so that whatever remains from it dissolves because of that moisture.
Then take care of it until it heals.
If however, there is an abscess at the site, then take care of it with soothing plasters until it heals.
On the causes of eye atrophy, that is, when the eyelid softens the eye, causing it to bulge abnormally.
**Chapter XI.**
When additional hairs grow in the eyelids beyond their natural course beneath the skin, and they become old, they harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as frequent tears, eyelid softening, and clouding of vision, until that becomes the cause of eye destruction.
And eye atrophy is treated according to four methods. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, as mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. Or eye atrophy is treated by cutting and stitching. Or by tubes, as I will mention.
You must place the head of the patient in your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it turns out well, so much the better. And if not, insert a needle into it, where the thread is located at the lower part of the eyelid, and make it penetrate upwards with the thread, so that it comes close to the individual hairs themselves, that is, close to the margin of the eyelid, where the hairs are born; then pull the thread towards the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with the radius.
Then, find the inner part of the eyelid, beneath the added hairs, with a spatula-like instrument, from the larger tear gland to the smaller one.
Then extract the thread and place a small piece of cotton or cloth beneath the eyelid; then mark on the eyelids with wax, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid, since it differs in humans.
Indeed, there are those who need to have the eyelid incised with a large quantity, according to the amount that becomes soft from the eyelid.
Then, with the spatula, find the two lines you marked and begin from the larger tear gland to the smaller one, and let there be a single fissure near the natural hairs, according to the thickness of the radius.
Next, introduce a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then, scrape off the entire skin from that area. Then, sew together the two edges with a needle and fine thread, to stop the bleeding and allow the excess threads to adhere above the two eyebrows, for some things may puncture if you do that; and if not, take no concern.
Then, leave the suture and thread until three or four days; after that, take care of it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitching and care for it so that it dries and contracts. For the eyelid is lifted at the sealing of the wound and its aggregation, but it will improve in time.
The ancients taught this method of healing.
However, there is a concern about the patient's condition, and it comes from good practice, and there is no fear in that.
And there is another method in Ataxmir as well; it is like signs on the eyelids in the form of a figure that resembles a myrtle leaf, as we have said.
Then elevate the eyelids with three hooks separated or joined, according to this form which follows, and then cut off the excess with small scissors, according to this form, with equality in the incision.
However, if it is not possible for you to hold the hook or if it does not align, then take a sharp instrument with which there is a thread, and insert it into the middle of the figure, and insert another thread near the larger tear, and a second thread near the smaller tear, and join the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then elevate your hand equally, and raise the skin above which the mark is made, as we have described.
Then join the two lips of the wound with stitching, and take care of it until it heals.
If indeed there is an abscess near your incision or fracture, fill it with gauze and similar materials, using healing ointments.
And when the eyelids also turn, as if their hairs are converting.
Therefore, it is necessary to use what we have said about the wound: but the stitching of each (side) is more artificial. **Concerning the care of the eye with dogs.**
And thus, as you turn the eyelids and cut the gap that is inside, according to what I have described. Then, with the artifice, make two tubes or two thin pieces of wood: whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of the space: and you have already cut at both ends on each side where you hold the thread.
Then, join each with delicate threads over the excess of the eyelid and tighten the two tubes on both sides with a strong twist, and leave it for several days. For the skin, when tightened, will die, and turn black, and rot until it falls off on its own.
If, however, it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then take care of it until it is healed.
When it is healed, raise the eyelid; and the hairs will no longer hurt.
And this is the form of the two tubes that you will make according to this shape and size in length and width.
**Chapter XII**
On the elevation of sharp pilons in the eye with a needle.
The operation consists of taking a thin needle and introducing it into the eye with a delicate, flexible thread. Then gather its ends together and tie each one with a very thin knot; the length of the threads should be one palm.
Then take another thin thread and compose it beneath the first in a small knot, and tie the ends of it if visible.
Then the patient should lay his head in your lap, close to the light, so that the operation may be shown, for it is a delicate and minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural pilus.
Then pull the hair up to above the thread and introduce the hair into the thread; if it is one, or two, or three, and not more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out of the eyelid together with the thread and natural pilus.
If, however, the thread is withdrawn from the wound without the hair coming out with it: draw the hair to the lower part, using the thread which you have woven into it, until the hair emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with your eye; then draw both the hair and the thread, and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a longer thread with it from its natural hairs, and it will be lifted along with it; or leave it until it lengthens after some space, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this alxatrati (lidding) occurs in the eye, it is called leporina.
And this alxatrati (lidding) is either natural or accidental.
The accidental alxatrati is caused by the healing of a wound, or its cutting or burning, and similar things.
And the method of treatment is that you should find that healing, and separate the lips, and place something between them, a kind of gap from linen, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not necessary to use in the treatment of this things that burn and contract, for if you do so, it will return to Alxatrati (Albucasis) in a worse condition than before; rather, use things that soften, such as fenugreek; mix with water in which cooked aloe is soaked, or linseed oil; and apply it with a poultice or use it for treatment.
And the treasure of your treatment is to preserve every intellect so that it does not harden according to its original form.
And this very operation that we have mentioned is nothing but a correction of Albucasis, so that it does not return to the original form of the place at all.
§ On the treatment of Albucasis (for) the lower eyelid, which is a swelling or contraction upward towards the right and left.
Chapter XIV
This treatment of Albucasis (for) what occurs below is what is truly called Albucasis. And it is both natural and accidental.
And accidental injuries occur from wounds, cracks, burning, and similar things.
And the way of operating is to take hold of a sharp instrument, where there is a double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it penetrate from the left lacrimal to the right lacrimal, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh towards the upper parts with the knife, and cut it with a broad scalpel. If it then returns to the shape of the eyelid as it should be, it is good.
And if it does not, take a radium (a pointed instrument) and insert it into the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it. Find two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of these fissures meet at the two angles of the incision, until they form an angle. Until this is achieved, when they are joined, the shape of these will be like a Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh along the length of its sharp lower side, from where it follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts, using a thread of linen.
Then care for them with the medicines that we have mentioned, and bandages, until they are healed.
And if an injury to the eyelid occurs from a fracture, and stitching or cauterization is required, then it is necessary to find the flat surface of the fracture beneath the eyelash, even as it was previously. Then separate between the two eyelids with a sharp instrument, as we said.
And in summary, regarding the care of the eyelid, whether it is above or below, the treatment should proceed as prepared according to Albucasis's form.
Indeed, it (the treatment) varies greatly in form: the skilled artisan prepares the tool in any way possible until they restore the figure to a natural or nearly natural form, and the patient themselves, as appropriate from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
§ On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This hardening occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid hardens with the whiteness of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, it is necessary that you insert the tip of the instrument beneath the eyelid and lift it upwards, or extend it with a hook, then cut the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharp but slightly blunt, so as not to disturb the patient during the operation, and remove the spatula, and harm the eye with its sharpness, and make the incision such that it frees that adhesion easily until, when the eyelid returns to its natural form, the whole adhesion is healed.
Then apply a salt water wash to the eye, and rinse it with water; or let it drip onto the eye from a sieve made of silk, dissolved in water.
Next, separate the eyelid and eye with a lint-free linen; place a compress soaked in albutylin on the eye, and use a sieve and compresses, strengthening them daily, until it heals.
§ On the cutting of the fingernail and the prominence of the flesh of the tears.
Head XVI.
Know that the fingernail is of two types. It is either nerve-like, similar to hard, yet delicate silk. Or it is not nerve-like, similar to the white frozen moisture; which when touched by hot iron or attempts to grasp it with a hook, it separates and does not adhere to the hook.
And neither of these types begins, unless from the tear gland, gradually increasing until it obstructs vision and blocks light, and movement of the eye.
And the method of cutting it is: place the weak head in your palm. Then he opens his eyes and lifts his eyelids with your hand. Then gather a finger with a hook having made it slightly thin, and extend it towards the upper part. Then take some thread and insert a needle into it from the equine hair, or wool, or silk; make sure the thread is strong, and bend both ends of the needle slightly, then fix it in the middle of the finger: and pierce through with the needle, and tie the thread to the finger, and extend it towards the upper part: and scrape along the side of the finger nail that follows the pupil, as if you were grasping it with the thread until its very end.
Then cut the remaining part at the base of the tear duct with a fine scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that there is no continuous flow of tears from the cut.
And when the finger is extended with just a hook, or with thread, as we have said. Then it will be scraped with a soft scalpel whose shape is as follows.
And be careful not to let the scalpel touch the corneal tunic, and cause it to tear or puncture the pupil.
Then, after the incision, insert into the eye some crushed salt or a liquid made from zinc, and leave it until the next day. Then, care for the wound with what is necessary until it heals.
If the nail is not nerve-related and you cannot insert a sharp instrument into it, nor is there a curved hook on it, then this type of nail often heals on its own with medicine, and it recovers as if divided.
If you must cut it, then open the wound with a gentle, delicate, and weak edge according to this form. And shave the nail from above with precision and delicacy. If you notice that it thins and contracts, and the shape is impressed on it, then from this moment onwards, pour into the eye a liquid made from zinc, or red lead, or crushed salt; and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on that part until it is completely removed. Unless there is a hot spot in the eye, then leave it alone and care for the affected area until it heals.
Then perform a repeat operation on that part, using reason, until it is healed.
However, if the eminence of the tear gland causes great harm to the eye, it should be suspended with a hook and cut from that part, but not the final cut, so as not to disrupt the flow of tears.
Then apply a stop to the tears, either from a red eye or that caused by a foreign body, until it is healed.
§ On the incision of Albucasis, and the flesh grown in the eye.
Head XVII.
In some human eyes, a red flesh grows together or is overlaid, until it covers the pupil, draws close to it, or squeezes it against the eyelids, and perhaps even turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a rose bud.
And the operation on it is also such that it places the weak head in your lap. Then open the eye and pull that flesh completely with suitable hooks towards it: and hold it with a forceps or tweezers.
Then cut into the flesh at the first point, until it is entirely finished with the scalpel. And be careful with the eye, so as not to hurt it during the operation; and let your cut be made where you are cutting it, or with a spatula, which we have described in the section on the nail cut, or with small shears, with which the lint is gathered, according to its form after this chapter.
When your cut is complete and the entire wound has been cleaned, then apply to the eye an external compress with crushed salt mixed with red dust from medicinal powders, or similar substances, and place it over the eye to protect it from heat and inflammation.
If there remains some of the wound and you have a hot blood flow, and you fear a hot inflammation; then leave the eye and treat it with what soothes the inflammation.
Then repeat the operation on it until it heals.
And likewise do with the added flesh that occurs in the eye from this cause.
However, it is necessary that the cure for every weak eye, whether from natural or accidental causes, should be operated on with a tool and not with bare hands; and you should not attempt any of these operations unless you first understand that this member can endure such an operation.
On the collection of fluid from the eye.
**Chapter XVIII.**
The sebel (or veins) are red and swollen, lying over the eye, preventing the flow of tears and weakening the eye with the passage of days.
Therefore, you must first consider whether the eye containing the sebel is strong and free from any illness other than that related to the sebel, and then collect the sebel from it.
And it is advisable to instruct the patient to place his head in your lap.
Then, suspend those veins with a hook or two, according to your judgment: the hook should be thin and flexible, shaped as follows, or there can be two hooks joined together in one body, also shaped as shown.
Then, carefully collect those veins with fine forceps and clean them hour by hour until you see the veins moving away from the eye and are resolved; be careful not to harm the eye with the tips of the forceps.
And perform the operation in the middle of the day, facing the sun, and be firm in your operation so as not to cut except those veins.
And when you have washed it, then let it trickle into the eye either with a green or red substance; so that its acuteness may corrode what remains of the grease.
However, if it is not possible for you to collect all of that in that moment, then apply a poultice to the eye with something that alleviates the pain of the eye, and leave it for several days until the pain subsides; and protect it from further harm by heat. Then repeat over it in the same manner until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
§ On the reduction of an abscess to the nose.
Head 19.
The surgeons of abscesses make a fistula, which they treat with cautery or with sharp medicine, according to what has been said before.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in that; unless you find a fissure near its origin and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the wound is closed.
When the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackening in it, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is as follows.
It is called the Hardness of the Head.
Go in with the iron, and make the head round like an apple, marked by a fine cut of the knife or a subtle point of the chisel, and place it over the corrupted bone.
Then take it between your fingers and gently press with your hand until you know that the corruption is completely smooth, and do this several times.
Then restore the place with dry aromatic medicines.
If therefore the place hardens and flesh grows, and the flow of pus ceases, and it remains for forty days without renewal or relapse, then know that it is healed.
And if this does not happen, then there is no skill in it, except to reduce the fistula to the nasal opening, according to this form.
And it is necessary to remove from the bone with iron or sharp medicine, and once the bone is exposed, then take a drill according to this form, let the iron end be triangular, and the wood of its pyramid be thinned towards the end, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself, and let it be near the tear duct, and stretch your hand slightly from the eye. Then resolve to move your hand with the knife, until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it; through this, he will find the strength to exit from it, when he holds his own hand over his nose.
Therefore, at that point, restore the place with styptic medicines that dry up, as I taught you.
When the flesh is formed and the place hardens, then the matter that was exuded to the outside returns to the nose; thus, it becomes softer over the patient.
## On Reducing Eyelid Protrusion
**Head XX.**
When the eye protrudes with its entire mass, and there is no pain or loss of vision, then you should relieve the patient; then use phlebotomies; afterwards, apply a flow of air to the eyelid without scarring but with suction.
Then place on the eye a liniment made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then close the eye with a bandage over it, using many small threads.
And the method for these threads is as follows: take many soft cloths, and make each cloth double over four times; then make many from them; then place them one on top of the other according to the amount needed for the eye; finally, bind them tightly with a strong, hard cloth.
And they also operate on flowing blood without scarification.
Then remove the blood flow and allow the eye to rest day and night.
However, if the ligature becomes softer again, it should be bound tightly; then, after a day and night, remove the ligature as we have said.
If therefore you see that the eye has regained its new skin, it is good; and if not, repeat the plaster, threads, binding, and bleeding until it heals.
## On the Incision of the Skin's Layer
**Chapter 21.**
When there is a tear in the skin's layer, it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grain, causing disfigurement due to that, and one should incise it; then the operation on it is as I describe: and it is as if you insert a sharp point into the root of the lid from below upwards.
Next, introduce another knife where the thread is double from the tearful side, and cause it to penetrate. And leave the first knife according to its arrangement.
Next, incise the location of the double thread, and tie with it the edge of the needle in a circular motion: and tighten it greatly.
Next, extract the knife, and place on the eye a linen cloth soaked in alum: and leave it until the thread falls with the eye.
Then care for the eye with it, so that it is strengthened, until the wound is healed.
And when the thread is tightened from all sides in a circular motion with the cloth, until the eye gains great prominence.
Next, it is pierced with a fine instrument that reaches the depth of the eye, and the humidity of the alum runs out, and the eye descends and enters immediately: then tighten it until it heals.
**On the cure of alum (or eye disease)**
Head XXII.
This disease called alum is not, but a health condition that is aggregated in the eye, similar to water running down, and it is not itself.
And the method of operation is such that you make the invalid sit on a prepared or straight seat.
Then take the head in both hands and move it until you think the blood has reached the lower part of your eyes. Then it is secured so that it does not drop, and the light is released; and the invalid sees things as he saw them before: and if it does not come down below, then know that it is water.
However, if the descent of the blood is not prepared as we said, then make the invalid sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula and find it with the above skin fold just above the connection of the joint with the corneal tunic in the crown, until the blood flows out.
When the blood has thus flowed out, then pour warm water into the eye, in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then it is treated with the rest of the cure until it is healed.
§ On the care of water that flows into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have previously described the types of water in division, and what is suitable for piercing, as explained and expounded in the discourse, then take that same one according to health.
Therefore, you should make the sick person sit with their face square between your hands, opposite to the light, towards the sun, and bind their healthy eye tightly; then, with your right hand, if the eye with water is on the left, hold the left eye, or with your left hand, if the eye is on the right.
Then, take your right hand with the alum, if the eye is on the left, or your left hand, if the eye is on the right.
Then, place the end of the alum near the edge, by the thickness of the radius, on the same white eye appearing lacrimal smaller; then push the alum with strength; and you return with it in your hand, until it penetrates into the eyeball: and you will feel that the alum has already reached the vacuum.
And it is necessary that the journey of the alum to the depth should be measured, as much space there is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and this is the crown of the eye. You will indeed see that in the same pupil vision of the eye, due to the clarity of the cornea tunic.
Then place the alum until it reaches the place where water is. Then press the same below with one hand after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the patient will see it over which he opens his wound immediately, and alum in his eye.
Then rest for a little while. If therefore the water descends, then lay it down again the second time, besides what alum comes out.
When it is thus fixed, and does not rise up: then draw out the alum with ease: and you return your hand little by little.
Then dissolve in water some of the clear salt of the stone, and wash with it the inside of the eye. Then put on the eye entirely the poultice, or wool steeped in rose oil, and alum too: and bind it with a healthy eye.
But we, and those of our time, put on it ground cumin with alum.
If the surgeon does not obey you and enters the eye without softening it because of its hardness, which comes from human flesh, whose eye is very hard, then he should take a spatula called "Alberid," which has this form.
Therefore, when handling it, connect only the joint itself and not your entire hand into the wound, and there should be only a narrow space for the spatula to enter.
Then insert the spatula as we said.
And for the completion of your work, prepare a dark room for the patient, in which he sleeps on his back; forbid all movements and coughing; give him that food by which his nature is soothed; and do not allow him to move his head to the right or left at all.
And the bandage should be left according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then remove it in that dark room and examine his sight; and make him see things. Then reduce the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary for him to do that at the time of healing or immediately after incision; rather, he should wait until a foreign body is introduced, because water rises quickly through an open wound.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs: then it is necessary to drain the eye before the seventh day; and straighten it with that which calms the abscess until it is calm; then release the sight of that eye, and place over its surface a poultice to regulate its sight for several days while it remains in that dark house.
Then it should gradually leave the house and return to its case.
And know that in zeal the student is not excused from sight for some time; then indeed he should dare to engage in surgery.
And indeed, some of those from Al-Andalus have now reached us, who say that a certain pierced magdan, through which water is drawn, has been done in Al-Andalus.
Therefore, no one has ever been seen in our land to have done that; nor is it written about in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from magda, so that one who ignores them may stand on them.
Let them be made of their own air; and let the ends of them be triangular with the same subtle angle, and the end acute.
Head XXIV.
On the care of flesh grown in the nose.
Sometimes various kinds of flesh are produced in the nose, from which some is like a scorpion with many feet; and from this there arises cancerous, hard flesh, of a dark colour; and from this there arises soft flesh, not of a dark colour.
Therefore, with regard to that flesh which is soft, neither putrid nor cancerous, you should make it lie between your hands facing upwards and open the nostrils, then insert a hook into those meats; after that, pull them out towards the exterior, and cut what you have seized from them with a thin and sharp blade on one side, until you know that all the flesh has already been removed.
If anything remains of that which cannot be separated, then cut it with a thin instrument easily until nothing remains from it. If, however, blood dominates you or a feverish abscess occurs: then oppose it with what is necessary; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then apply cauterization to it until the blood is drained and all flesh is removed.
Next, after making an incision, pour vinegar and water or wine into the nose. However, if the nose opens up and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is already healed.
If indeed moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that there is flesh grown within the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Therefore, you should insert a line of thread that has some thickness and knots on it; place the space between each knot equal to a finger, or less, and twist the weak end of the thread into the nose with a hook, or something similar, and pull until it reaches the cavity, and comes out from its opening.
And indeed, many young children do this like atoms in schools; and it is an easy thing for whoever wants to.
Then, place both ends of the thread; one end that comes out from the nose and the other end that remains inside the nose. Then, using the help of the knots on the thread, close the wound with the flesh. Do this until you know that the flesh is sealed with the knots on the thread.
Then extract the thread and place it in the nose after diverting the blood flow with the ointment you applied from Egypt. Do this for three days or longer until the ointment has completely dissolved what remains of the flesh.
Then, after some days, insert a lead pipe into the nose until it heals.
However, if further treatment is needed to stop the bleeding, use it.
To make the head purification, with which oils are distilled and other medicines into the nose, make it from silver or brass, in the form of a small lamp, opened like a conch shell, and its passageway similarly.
And if you want to make the pipe closed like a reed.
And the conch shell of the head purification instrument should be full and open; it should have a handle at the end with which to hold it when heating oil or whatever you want to insert, or substances that can flow.
Chapter XXV.
Many times a lump is born at the end of the nose, and it swells and grows with the passing days, until an agreement is reached about its appearance.
And because of that, it is necessary to cut off that lump at the beginning of its appearance and remove all of it. Then apply a poultice, or a burn dressing, or a burning medicine that takes its place.
If the incision is delayed until the lump has swelled and its appearance has changed, then consider whether it is hard, firm, dark in colour, and has little sensation; if so, do not touch it with the blade, for it is a malignant tumour.
Many times I have seen those who have cut off a malignant lump, and great evil has come from it to him who had that one.
And if the lump is soft to touch, not dark in colour, and you see that an incision can be made throughout it, then encourage the sick person, and cut off the lump without fear or hesitation; and treat the place with something that draws out and constricts it until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.
§ On the stitching of the nose, lip, and ear.
You should know that when there is a separation of the connection in one of these cartilage tissues, little surgical treatment is required for it, except in certain cases.
Therefore, when something like this happens to anyone, you should consider if it is a recent wound still oozing its own blood. Then, stitch together the wounds with cautery and afterwards take care of it until it heals.
And if the connection of the lips has already separated and one part becomes healthy, then it is necessary to clean all the parts appearing from the skin until they bleed. Then stitch together the two lips with sutures and tighten them, and apply over it dragon's blood and powdered frankincense; place above the wound a fragment of an ointment pad, and use another from consolidant plasters. And leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, change the medicine, and leave it until the threads come out by themselves. Then take care of it with ointment until it heals.
The method of stitching is to gather the connection of the cartilage, or with a pad, as we mentioned about the stitches of the stomach; or to gather the separation with threads, as you know.
**Chapter XXVII**
On the extraction of lip nodules.
Many men suffer from hard protrusions within their lips, often resembling small or large grains of herb, and some are more painful than others.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn the lip and find the top of each nodule, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it off from every side.
Then, after the cut, apply pressure with a clean cloth until the bleeding stops.
Then, it should be washed with vinegar, and the place treated with it where there is stiffness, until the wounds heal.
**Chapter XXVIII**
On the incision of flesh grown over the gum.
Flesh often grows over the gum, which the ancients call an abulsion.
Therefore, it is necessary to suspend it with a hook, or hold it with tweezers, and cut it near its root, allowing the discharge of pus or blood.
Then place a third covering over the wound, or other sticky poultices, dry and excoriating.
If the flesh returns to its former state after treatment, as it often does, then cut it away a second time and cauterize it; for indeed, it will not return after cauterization.
Head 29.
§ On the reason for teeth with iron.
Sometimes teeth become attached to the surface from both the inner and outer parts, and between the gums there are rough and painful cortices, which sometimes turn black and yellow, and become bright, until finally corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through that.
Therefore, you must make the patient sit between your hands, and rest his head in your lap, and scrape away the roughness and tooth, where you can see the cortices, like sand; until nothing remains of them, and do the same with blackness, whiteness, yellowing, and others until they are cleansed.
If therefore what is in them from the beginning of the reason can be removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on them another day, a second time, and a third, until the ultimate study in what you wish has been reached.
And you should know that the tooth needs to be cut with various forms of instruments, in accordance with what is prepared for your operation; for the instrument that cuts through the tooth from below is different from the one that cuts between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And these forms of instruments should be prepared for you as you see.
**Chapter XXX**
**On the Extraction of Teeth Pain**
You should cure a tooth from its pain with all skill, and take your time in extracting it. For indeed, I have seen that its pain does not subside when it is extracted, since it is a noble substance; until there is no excuse for extracting it, then when the patient is compelled to extract it, as it should be strengthened and verified by you, the painful tooth.
Many times the patient is deceived by its pain, and thinks that it is relieved in the tooth and extracts it; then the pain does not cease until the infected tooth is extracted.
We have already seen that from the work of bloodletting instruments.
If a toothache is confirmed within your gums with pain, then you must perform incisions around the affected tooth with a scalpel, which has something of sharp quality, until the gum is completely dissolved from all sides; then gently move it with your fingers or with subtle pincers in the beginning gradually, until it detaches.
Then locate large pincers and grasp the lower jaw between your fingers; hold it firmly so that it does not move. Then pull on the tooth straightwards, avoiding to break it.
If it does not come out, then take some of those instruments and insert them beneath it from all sides easily, and try to move it as you did before.
However, if the tooth is perforated or corroded, then you must fill that hole with cloth and tie it with a thin end of a wire so as not to break it during your squeezing with pincers on top.
And it is necessary to be careful with incisions around the gums from all sides much.
And preserve your strength so as not to break it and let a part of it remain, and return from that malady which causes the greatest pain for it initially.
And beware, do not do what uninformed bloodletters do in their haste and boldness about its removal, except that they administer what we have mentioned.
Many times they cause great infirmities in men to develop, which are more difficult to break and leave all or some of their roots intact, or so that they extract it along with certain bones of the mandible, as we have often witnessed.
Then it should be washed after the removal with wine, or vinegar and salt.
If bleeding occurs from the wounds (which often happens), press something against it and fill the space with that, or if not, cauterize it if it does not stop bleeding.
The pliers should have long tips, a short handle, and be made of sturdy metal so that they do not double over when pressed against a tooth; they should resemble the handles of large pliers until, when you squeeze them, they do not bend or double over themselves. They should have sharp points at their ends, some of which enter certain spaces and grip firmly with constriction.
And when the tips take on the shape of the curved beak called a forceps, they become strong tools for grasping.
Chapter 31.
§ On the removal of teeth and the extraction of fractured jawbone.
When there remains, near the extraction of a tooth that breaks something, then it is necessary to place over the site a custom bandage with butter for one or two days, until the place becomes soft; then insert the proper forceps or pincers, whose ends are similar to the beak of what is called a forcep. The shape of these forceps is as follows:
The ends of these forceps are made like a chisel or a knife blade.
If, therefore, it does not yield to you when using these forceps, then you must apply leverage over the root and remove all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose shape is as follows.
It should have a short end, slightly thicker; it must not be soaked, lest it break.
If, therefore, the root comes out through it, it is good; if not, leverage must be applied with other instruments of this shape.
The shape of the first end is triangular, with some thickness.
The triangular end is delicate.
And with other instruments and tools that preceded it in terms of teeth.
It is also a saw with this instrument that resembles a large hook, with curved ends of a triangular shape, which has some thickness to it so as not to break, and it should not be oiled.
And you should know that there are many instruments for teeth: and similarly, the other tools are not counted, and a skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, found this very instrument himself, according to which he understands the operation and illnesses themselves.
Because from the illnesses to which they (teeth) are subject, there were no instruments mentioned by the ancients, due to the diversity of those species.
However, if the bone breaks from the jaw or from any other bones of the mouth, or it decays: then examine that place with him (the surgeon) using the instruments and pincers suitable for extracting roots, and treat with gestures, whose form is as follows.
In that, put some thickness gradually, so as to tighten or hold the bone, and not let it escape or waste away, until the bone is extracted, and restore the place with suitable medicines for that.
If there is a small putrid bone, then cut it away from its putrefaction and treat it until it heals.
Head XXXII.
On the projection of the prominent teeth above others.
When the teeth project not according to their natural course, an unsightly form results from it: and especially when this happens in women and girls, that is, in those with thin jaws.
Therefore, it is necessary that you should consider. For if a tooth grows after another tooth, and does not move or become loose, then extract it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, which has the following shape.
It has a similar appearance to a small face, and is made of iron bent, with very acute ends.
And your cut should be such that you cut it for many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so as not to disturb any other teeth.
However, if it is prominently exposed and its edge can be filed: then file it with a file made of iron, whose shape is as follows.
Sit entirely from the indus, and its handle of small punctured points like a liming tool with which pins are made; with it, the tooth is gently filed day after day until it smooths out without disturbing the tooth or causing it to fall out; then make it soft, finally polish it with certain rasors.
If there is a tooth, from which a part breaks off and injures the tongue, then it must be filed further until its roughness due to the fracture is removed and it becomes smooth; it should be smoothed without injuring the tongue or corrupting speech.
**Head XXXIII.**
**On the Relationship of Teeth with Golden Threads**
When the front teeth suffer a shock or displacement due to impact or accident, and the weak patient cannot hold onto what he chews to prevent it from falling out, and treatment with astringent medicines does not help: then there is a trick to make them cling together with golden thread. Since silver turns green and rots after a few days, but gold never changes.
And the thread should be of medium thickness, according to the amount that can fit between the teeth.
As for the method of threading, take the thread and insert its double length between two healthy teeth; then weave the ends of the thread between the moving teeth, one or more, until you reach the meshwork up to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the texture up to the part from which you started, and tightly hold your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved at all, and let your compression at the point where you are tying the thread, near the roots of the teeth, be such that it does not slip.
Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, and gather them together, and twist them with your fingers, and insert them between the healthy tooth and the moving tooth, so as not to harm the tongue, then release it so that it remains tightly held.
If it is however loosened or cut, tie them again with another thread, and let it remain thus for a long time.
And this is the form of teeth, and the arrangement of two healthy teeth and two moving teeth, as you can see.
And whenever a tooth or two return to their original position after falling out in their places, and remain as we have described, it is done only by an artist, prudent and skilled.
And whenever some suitable bone is obtained from a corpse, it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in the position from which the tooth is supported and tightened, as we have said, and remains, and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes the ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation is to open the lower jaw at its base in your palm, and lift up his tongue; then cut through that nerve ligament across its width until the tongue is released from its attachment.
If there is something of hardness and knotting in it, resulting from the healing of a wound, then insert an hook into it and find its width, until you cut through the ligament and the knot is released.
And be careful, so that there is no tear in the deep flesh, and cut through the artery there, and let there not be a flow of blood.
Then the wound should be washed with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a piece of linen that will hold it open at night so that it does not heal again.
If, however, there is a flow of blood, then place over the wound a torn cloth. If indeed blood clings to you, then cauterize the place with a lentiginous cautery, which is appropriate for it; then treat it as you would any other wound, until it heals.
Head XXXIV.
Sometimes a swelling occurs under the tongue that resembles a small ranula, preventing the tongue from its natural movement, and it may even become so severe that it blocks the mouth.
And the treatment for it is to open the lower jaw before the sun, and consider the swelling.
If therefore you see that it is of a pale or black colour, hard, and lacks feeling, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it has dipped to whiteness with humidity, then throw a hook at it and cut it off with a thin instrument, freeing it from every part. If indeed blood clings to it during your operation, then place a crushed, third-degree bandage over it until the blood lets go; afterwards, return to your operation until you have completely extracted it along with its complement.
Then it should be washed with vinegar and salt, and the rest of the cure should correspond to that treatment until it is healed.
§ On the treatment of an aptham (edema) of both amygdales.
Head XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur in the throat, glands similar to cotton, which appear externally, and are called two amygdales. If these do not heal when treated as we have said, then consider them.
If therefore the aptham is of a dark color, firm to the touch, and has little sensation, then do not attempt to treat it with iron.
And if it is of a red color, and the root is thick: then do not touch it with iron either, fearing blood loss, but instead let it stand until it heals properly. And then either pierce it or it will break on its own.
And if it is of a white color, round, and the root is thin: then you must cut into it.
And the operation on it is such that you should consider before your operation if the aptham's heat has completely subsided or diminished in any way, then afterwards make the patient sit before the sun, and place his head in your lap, open his mouth, and with a tool of this form, press his tongue from below.
Make it from silver or brass, which should be as thin as a knife blade.
So, when you have placed it (the appendage) in the wound and it sinks into it, your finger should then be removed using a hook and fixed in a groove, and pulled out as far as possible without pulling on the skin.
Then cut it with the instrument, whose shape follows this one. It is cut with shears; however, its ends should be protected so that the hole of each one is in direct contact with the sharp point of the other, thereby ensuring it is made of iron-wrought or soaked alum.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a knife, whose shape follows this one. It should be sharp on one side and blunt on the other.
And after you have cut one groove, cut the other according to this same pattern of cutting.
After the cutting, then wash the patient with cold water or with vinegar and water.
If, however, there is bleeding, wash with water in which bark of the hemlock or similar astringent leaves have been boiled until the flow of blood stops; then take care of it until it heals.
And sometimes other abscesses are born in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut those according to what I have said about the cutting of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I have already treated a woman from an abscess that had been born between her throat, which was drawing towards blackness, with little feeling, which had almost closed her throat; and the woman was breathing through a narrow opening, and had forbidden herself food and drinking water; and she had already reached the point of death, if she had not recovered within one or two days.
And the abscess will only rise as far as it has emitted two branches, until they have emerged above the two nasal openings.
I therefore proceeded with greater haste, and fixed one of the hooks into one of them. Then I pulled it straight, and the portion that was torn out from it is a large one.
Then I severed it where it was caught in the nasal opening.
Then I did the same thing to the other one that had emerged from the second nasal opening.
Then I opened her mouth, and pressed her tongue; then I fixed the hook into the abscess itself; then I severed from it that part of it, and nothing more came out of it but a little blood: thus the throat of the woman is healed, and she began to drink water immediately, and then accepted food.
Therefore I did not cease to cut away from that gap many times over a long period of time, and the gap was succeeded by the place where I had cut, until it became too prolonged and as a result, that gap.
I therefore devised a way and cauterized the gap between the throat.
It stopped bleeding; then I walked away from it, and I did not know what God had done with it after me.
§ On the incision of the skin gap.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the phlegm descends to the skin and forms a gap, which is long, then it is called "perpendicular"; and if it is in the lower part thick and round, then it is called "uua".
When it is cured with what we have said about the division, and treatment does not help; and you see that the gap has become calm and thin, then it is necessary to cut it.
And what is aggregated from it in a round shape, and does not have length, and is of a bloody or dark or black colour, and has no sensation, then you must make an incision into it. There is fear in it concerning the sick.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described, by its whiteness and length, make the sick sit before the sun, and press his tongue with the instrument of which mention has previously been made; then attach a hook to it and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments I have mentioned in cutting the two glands.
And it is necessary that you do not cut from it unless that which is added to the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
After the cutting, use what we have mentioned in cutting the two glands, and take care of it until it heals.
If, however, the sick is disturbed by the cut: then you must use skill in sealing it without fear and trepidation.
The manner of cauterization in it is not otherwise than with sharp water; and it is as if you place the head of the sick in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument I mentioned. Then take water as I have told you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with a paste of unextinguished lime, not too thick or too thin, and fill up this cavity of the instrument, which has this form.
Place the tip of it at the place where the medicine is to be applied, as the cavity of the radius bone.
And place the instrument with the medicine on that very same wound: and let the patient lie on his side so that the salt from his mouth flows out due to the medicine's inherent action, lest any of it descend into his throat and harm himself.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And from the first, place it over the wound with a quantity sufficient for half an hour, until you perceive that it has already turned black and the bite of the medicine is subdued.
And if you wish to take off the poultice and cover it again over the end of the radius, and insert the rayon with the poultice into a tube above, until the poultice adheres to the wound.
Do that many times until you achieve what you wish from the cauterization of the new (wound). Then release her; for she will heal and fall off after three or four days.
If you need to apply further medicine, apply the same to it; and after cauterization, clean around the new wound with butter-soaked cotton and purify it with what is left from the medicine; then wash it with cold water.
And it will heal from the outside with bandages, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This, therefore, is the kind of healing that is more salutary and less fearful.
And sometimes the wound heals even better than with burning or cutting; for it heals with fumigation, in this way. Take calament, hyssop, oregano, rue, fennel, chamomile, and tansy, and other similar herbs; mix them all together, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them in vinegar, allowing them to bubble: the pot should be sealed with decent clay; and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which a concave instrument is placed in this way.
Let it be made of silver or brass; and the end with the grenade should be inserted into the mouth until the vapor rises to the upper part through the tube, blocking the wound a lot.
Then repeat this process many times over the same area until it heals.
And moreover, do not perform this cure at the onset of the apoplexy. For indeed, it adds pleasure to the apoplexy: and you should not perform that unless near the decline of the apoplexy's heat.
If, however, this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane, and fasten a piece of wood at its end, so that it does not burn the weak bone, for the wood prevents the vapor from burning the bone; and this is from good cure with health.
§ On the extraction of spines and bones from the throat.
Head XXXVIII.
Often, a bone, or a fish spine, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, you must extract what is apparent from them, after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the sun, and it will be declared to you whether something is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food digests in the stomach. Perhaps the condition will be expelled through vomiting.
If the patient swallows a fragment of raw naph (a type of plant), or a root of lettuce, or chews but does not swallow a dry crumb of bread, he may take instead a piece of soft, dry seaweed and wrap it in thread, then swallow it. When, therefore, he reaches the place where there are spines or bones, and extracts them, then he is cured.
If, therefore, it does not come out as we said, then use an instrument of lead in this form.
It should be larger than the radius by a little, and its end should be sharpened so that it is easily inserted into the patient's throat; and he himself should keep his head raised upward.
And he should be careful with the touch of his epiglottis, so that he does not experience coughing, and pushes out with it a bone or a spine, or the doctor himself inserts it by hand.
Indeed, it is better for the patient to insert it himself, because of his knowledge of the place where the condition is attached, and he pushes it down or pulls his hand up with the instrument until it comes out completely according to the amount that was prepared for him.
On the extraction of the bloodsucker.
Chapter XXXIX.
When the bloodsucker is treated with what I have mentioned regarding division from cure with medicines, and it does not improve, then consider the drop of the weak tongue before the sun, or near the sun after you have placed your instrument on it as I told you.
