If it is not possible with these [instruments], then take a hollow pipe and insert it into the weak tongue up to near the throat: then insert into the hollow of the pipe a heated iron tip with fire. Do that many times, and the weak person should abstain from water all day.
Then take a full cup of cold water and open the person's mouth with it: and have them suck their own tongue in it, and not swallow anything from it: and move the water every hour from one to another with their hand. The bloodsucker indeed falls immediately when it feels the water.
If, however, he does not leave with what we have described, then smother the sick with lice or with wings and an instrument, as I described for the suffocation of a fly.
Do that many times, because it will fall itself.
And the mode of operation in suffocation is to take a pot, in which there are live coals with fire; and let the pot be covered with a cover, in whose middle there is an opening; and arrange the end of the instrument in that opening.
Then release the suffocation, and have the sick person place their mouth at the end of the tube, and close their mouth so that the suffocation does not escape, until you know that the suffocation has reached the blood vessel. For the blood vessel will immediately fall.
If it does not fall, then repeat the suffocation many times.
And the sick person will endure thirst, and eat spicy foods, and others, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary to leave with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the blood vessel is extracted from the throat when it falls on that surface.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that there is a certain curvature which extends to the throat. And its end is similar to the beak of what is called a forceps, in which there is a rough edge; when it grips something, it does not let go of it completely.
§ On the opening of wounds (apopthema), their incision, and general care.
Head XL.
There are many kinds of wounds (apopthema), varying according to the manner in which they present themselves, namely, one kind and one alone is discussed in this book; and they differ in their punctures and incisions in two ways.
One of the kinds of wounds is from a specific type of aperture (apopthema) itself, and because it absorbs moisture.
The second manner (of incision) is according to the places where it happens on the body.
Since the wound (apopthema) occurring in the head is different from that occurring in the anus; and a wound occurring in a fleshy part is different from one occurring in joints; and each has its own judgment according to its operation.
And there are those which should not be pierced unless after the purification of the discharge within them and its completion; and these are those which must be pierced while they are still raw and do not complete their maturation according to their due course.
As for the abscesses that are close to the joints.
For when an abscess occurs close to a joint and persists until the pus within it putrefies, the surrounding ligaments or nerves of that joint might become corrupted; hence, it is a cause for closer contraction of that member.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member; for if you delay piercing it until it matures, it will harm either that one or the principal member through it.
Or it is near a bowel; therefore, pierce the raw abscess that has not yet completed its digestion, only if it is near a bowel to prevent deep putrefaction and to extend towards the interior of the anus, thereby creating a fistula or ending in a condition that does not heal.
You must know the time for piercing the abscess that has matured according to its course; and it is indicated by the relief of pain, the decrease in fever, and the diminution of redness, pulse, and sharpness of the abscessed head; and other signs.
And you must make the same perforation in the lower part of the apostema and of the more prominent eminence; and it should be a piercing cut along the length of the body, if the apostemata are in the parts of the hands and feet, and in the places of the tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
However, in those that reflect, pierce according to that member.
When the apostema is in fleshy places: then it is better to leave off the piercing until its healing is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
Because if you pierce it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there is much pain and swelling, and perhaps its lips become hardened, and its depth increases.
And certain apostemata are pierced according to the width of the body where it is necessary, or according to that which the member needs.
And it is necessary to administer in small apostemata a small piercing, and in large apostemata a large piercing: or many sections according to the similarity of the size of the apostema.
And there are apostemata where it is necessary to make the skin hollow: and it is cut when it has already formed as a cloth, and has become in the end that it is dead, such as happens in many cases, as it shrinks, and in gonorrhoea.
And from them are those that are cut with a section having three angles. And from them is that from which it is incised as the figure of a myrtle leaf.
As the scrotal hernia.
And from them are those in which a round section is administered, and a lunar section; and similar sections to these.
That, however, which has no head, as are the soft hernias on the surface, should be treated with a single or merely shallow perforation.
And when the hernia is large and has already absorbed much blood, and you make a single perforation, so as not to tear it and drain all the pus at that time; on the contrary, remove part of it from that very place. Then, bandage the hernia until another day. Then, remove something from the pus again.
Do this many times according to the degree, until it protrudes; especially if the patient is weak of will, or a pregnant woman, or a very young child, or an old person who has declined: for the animal spirit often leaves the body with the discharge of pus, and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, this chapter demands great caution.
And after your perforation, through which you make these incisions, it is necessary to cleanse the wound; and consider this: If therefore the wound of the appendage is small or there is a flat cut, then apply a poultice of lint or an old cloth. And if it is a large fistula and there are many cracks from the perforation, then you should introduce lint into each crack until they heal completely.
And if you have cut off a part of the skin in the fistula or torn it in the surrounding area, then you should fill it with an old cloth or as when a cloth is woven, or cloth lint from dry linen and tighten it until the third day.
Then remove the bandage and care for it as necessary with ointment until it is healed.
If, however, there is bleeding during your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after which a cloth should be soaked in them and placed on the place of the bleeding numerous times.
If, however, the bleeding persists, then it is necessary to administer treatments and care, and use the poultices and substances I have mentioned in many places in this book, and according to division.
However, if it is in the winter season and the site of the abscess involves many nerves, then you should insert small threads with wine and hot oil and place them on the affected area.
But if it is summer and the sites are fleshy, then you should apply threads soaked in water and oil, or wine and oil to them all, while they are cold. Until the third day, as we have said, then you should open up the abscess, wash it, and apply whatever is suitable for its cure until it heals.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the cure for abscesses universally.
According to the way of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess, how the path to its cure is described individually.
Head 41.

§ On the section of strophules (lumps) on the neck.
Head 42.
These apostemas (deformities) often occur in the neck, and under armpits, and groins: and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.

And let the instrument be not too sharp: so as not to give you a hand with the cut or disturb the patient, and make an unnecessary cut.

Then fill the wound with more of the same Egyptian ointment that you had already applied. Then take care of yourself.
If the strophula is completely cut off, then it is necessary to gather the two lips of the wound and close them immediately as soon as you see that there is no excess left.
However, if you see that there is excess left due to the size of the strophula, then it is necessary to prepare to cut its root and extract it; and administer the stitch as we have said.

Then after the perforation, apply a linen cloth with ointment and similar things to corrode what remains from the corruption, until you see that the wound is already clean; then take care of yourself with a generation-type ointment for the flesh until it heals.
**Chapter XLIII**
On the incision of the epiglottis through an opening within the throat by accident.
The ancients spoke of this incision into the epiglottis, and they knew of no one in our land who had performed it; and this is the writing of their words.
In cases of strangulation, the incision of the epiglottis must be made with care; if it is not done carefully, then because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those who have a hot aperture in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, without any obstruction in the channels, it is necessary to use the incision of the epiglottis to avoid the destruction that occurs due to inflammation.
Therefore, when we wish to make the incision of the epiglottis, we must cut it under three or four circles of the epiglottis with a small section in width, at the place that is between the two circles, in such a way that the incision be on the side (since it is enclosed by meat, and the vessels of blood are far from there).
If the physician is timid, then he must extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then it finds the wound, until it reaches the canal: it should open the vessel of blood if it sees anything coming out from them.
Then it cuts through the part we have described: and this is indicated above the section of the canal from which phlegm comes out, when it breaks and separates from it.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When, therefore, the time comes when it is feared that the wound will close, gather together the two edges of the skin of the wound, and leave it alone without cartilage.
Then administer the medicines that cause the flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. In conclusion, as we have described, the exposition is not complete unless they see that one of these conditions has already saturated the throat of the patient; and the sick person is lifted up above death, and desires to be cut open, so that the patient breathes through the wound in some way, and is saved from death. And for this reason, they hastened to leave the wound open until the malady is consumed, and it lasts for three days, and as if it were itself; then they hastened to close the wound and care for themselves until they are healed.
And what I have seen myself is that a nurse takes up the knife, and sends it into her throat, and cuts with it part of the pulmonary artery.
And I have been called upon for the treatment of this condition, and I find her moaning, as is the moan of a dying person.
I therefore detected a wound, and I found blood that had emerged from the wound of the lung. I am therefore confused that she did not cut the vein, nor tie it off; and air was coming out from the wound.
I therefore prepared, and treated my own wound, until it was healed; and the nurse suffered nothing, except a hoarse voice: and after several days she returned for better arrangements.
From this, therefore, I say that there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
## On the Incision of the Gut Hernia
Chapter 44
This hernia, called the gut hernia, is a large aperture, according to the color of the body; and it is common in women.

Accidental, indeed, it is according to two modes: of which one is similar to thick knots. And the other mode is similar to an aperture that is made from the knot of an artery. Chapter XLV.
§ On the Section Regarding Knots' Species.
There are many kinds of nodules, and I have already described all their types in a division; and it is necessary that I tell the difference between a nodule and an exit here, as it creates doubt.
Therefore, I say that when the exitum (exit) presents heat, fever, and severe pain until the ebullition of excess fluid is calmed and putrefaction completes, then indeed the fever and heat subside.
When the nodule is not characterized by heat, fever, or pain and it contains a cystic sac that is its own bag and is according to the color of the body, then it is captured like a chick in an egg and becomes like a blister, with some larger and others smaller.
And they are according to two types: either plump or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, as I said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the treatment of a nodule, it is necessary that you experience it and examine it with the first instrument called Intromissum, according to its shape as it comes in the chapter that follows this one.
And the method of examining the abscesses and nodules is to take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place that you find in the abscess; and you gradually push your fingers with it until you know that the instrument has penetrated the skin. Then, with your last hand, conform it according to the measure of the aperture's magnitude.
Then extract the intromission and consider what emerges from its track.
If therefore moisture emerges flowing, whatever its nature: then dry it by a shallow section, according to what I have said in the remaining apertures.
And if moisture does not emerge from the intromission's track, then know that it is fatty: so dry it by a section above it in the form of a cross, according to this form as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and strip the skin from all sides easily: and guard the wound if you can over it, so that you extract the same healthy with the knot.
However, if the cyst ruptures during the operation and it is not possible to extract it healthy, this often happens, then extract it fragmentarily until nothing remains from it.
For if something remains from it, whether little or much, the knot returns according to its greatest extent.
Which if it catches you, and something remains from it, even little: then fill the wound at your completion with some sharp, corroding powder: and tighten the wound, and place over it what sits on the aperture a hot compress, and care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If there is a large nodule, then its two lips and treatment of itself with what it encloses.
However, if the vein is pulsating or not pulsating: then approach it and fill the space with a third-degree wound dressing, and leave it tightened for two days, or three, until the ulcer putrefies and the bleeding ceases; then return to incising what remains of the nodule.
Head XLVI.
§ On external instruments for cutting and piercing.
From these are the forms of instruments for insertion, and there are three kinds.
Indeed, there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They should be made of iron, square at both ends, so that they hold the opening in the wound firmly.
And these are the shapes of instruments for piercing, and yes, they are called punctors, that is, piercing ones; and there are three kinds.
Indeed, there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They need to be probed and incised, and fistulas and buboes drained from what lies between them, which is bone.
And others are made round and smooth and soft, like a large needle that is used to sew, of citron or brass, or iron, or steel, or silver.
And the best of them are made from citron.
And experience is also made with black lead, which is used to probe fistulas, the cavities of which have reflections, so that they reflect with their gentle nature those same reflections.
And there are again three kinds. Because they are long, medium, or short from them, according to the quantity of the width and tightness of the fistula.
The shapes of stitches, and they are of many kinds.
Because they are simple, that is to say, having one thread: and they are of three kinds as you see, large, medium, and small.
And from them are blind stitches: and they are of three kinds.
And from these are hooks having two wounds, and they are of three kinds; and from these are hooks having three wounds, and they are also of three kinds; and all these are necessary in their respective places.
And these are the forms of scalpels by which knots and incisions are cut and made; and they are of three kinds, because there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
The ends by which the cutting is done are acute, and the other ends are not; and they are not used thus except to provide help in the incision of knots at the site of a vein or nerve's fear, or to cure the sick through them, and gain some rest from the heat as they did during the excoriation of an apostema.
They are made of iron, and are similar to a knife with which alcohol is made; and at the tip there is a small hidden point of the scalpel, through which it flows inside and outside when you wish, as you see.
The form of the scalpels which are hidden among the fingers at the piercing of an apostema, and which are not perceived by the sick; and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The forms of the wind instruments by which the flow of blood is divided; and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They should be made round and somewhat long from air or citron, as you see, and they should be thin.
And you should have these two kinds of wind instruments with you, the large and the small ones, so that you can drain blood quickly from them when necessary, and to use them when no other medicine is available.
However, do not use them for the removal of blood from every part of the body, and only use them on fleshy parts, such as the thigh, hip, arm, breast, abdomen, groin, and similar places, where they are applied to damp flesh.
And when they are made into smaller instruments similar to the shape of almond shells, they can be used to drain blood when it flows from a phlebotomy site or near a vein or artery incision; and sometimes they are made round according to this form.

Chapter XLIX. Head.
**On the section of the apoplexy that occurs due to an artery and vein.**
When an artery ruptures and the skin above it hardens, a deep ulcer often results from it; and the same happens to veins, leading to ulcers and swelling.
And the signs by which it is recognized that the ulcer and swelling are due to an artery or vein are: since, when the ulcer is due to an artery, it is long, deep, and localized in the body; and when you press the ulcer with your fingers, you feel a kind of cracking sensation.
And that which is due to a vein is round, clearly visible on the body.
And cutting into the ulcer in this area is a mistake, especially when it is located under the armpits, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body; and it is a great risk. Therefore, it is necessary to take care of it with caution when using a knife, and the same should be done for those at the extremities, or at the head.
Therefore, over that area where an artery's orifice becomes inflamed, make an incision in the skin along its length. Then open the incision with hooks, afterward locate the artery; and free it from the tunics until it is exposed.
Then insert under it a sharp knife, and cause it to penetrate until it reaches the other side, and tie the artery with a double thread at two places, as I taught you in extracting the two arteries that are in the temples.
Then punch the space between the two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood inside it has drained out and the hemorrhage is stopped.
Then apply treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off; afterwards, care for it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If it is an abscess due to the severing of a vein, then you must use your hand as much as possible to squeeze out the abscess along with the skin. Then insert a knife under the place that you have held with your hand, and cause it to penetrate until you extract it from the other side. And put a double thread in it until you remove it from the other side. Then bind the abscess tightly with a good ligament; as I told you about the young ligament, from both parts with both ends of the thread.
However, if you find that the thread cannot be extracted, then insert another knife with a different thread under the entire abscess near the wound of the needle; press and tie your threads at four places.
Next, dry the abscess in the middle of it until the purulent matter is discharged; then cut away the excess skin and release what is tied up in it.
Next, place on it the small piece of cloth that you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply the treatment using poultices and ointments until it heals.
Head (L.)
§ On the abscess that occurs due to the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, so too does it occur in nerves; this happens either from a blow or from excessive strain, and similar conditions: and it is most common in the areas of assistance [i.e., the hands] or the foot, and at any location where there are joints, and it is severe.
It resembles the colour of the rest of the body, and is present in many configurations without pain; and when it contracts with spasms, the patient feels a weakness similar to stupor.
And the abscess is not formed deeply within the body, but it is beneath the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not extend forward or backward, nor to the sides.
Therefore, what arises from them at the joints should not be attacked with a sword. Perhaps it happens that contraction occurs.
However, for what arises from them in the head or forehead, find the skin with a scalpel. If therefore it is an apostema of the bones, then hold it with pincers and cut it off at the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook and scrape it; then remove it and dress the wound with sutures; and take care of it until it is healed.
Head (Chapter) LI.
§ On the cutting out of an apostema, which happens in the belly.
Certain men suffer from repeated growths in their veins and other parts of their bodies, which are called Fungalia, due to their resemblance to fungi.
Whose root is thin and whose head is large, and their edges (or lips) are turned inward. And there are a few among them that are rare, and there are some that are very impressive.
And indeed I have seen a man whose belly had two hernias, more similar to other things like fungi; there was no difference between them, white and thin roots, and their edges turned inwards, they closed up, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore, cut them and you will find that one weighs as much as one ounce and eight pennies, and the other as much as six pennies. And the operation is to make an incision in them, as you will consider then, if the patient is weak and moist, and the color is white and thin like a root: then cut it with a broad scalpel, and there will be cauterization before your eyes over fire.
For often much blood is expelled during the operation; therefore, if it should lacerate you, then cauterize it.
If you see that the patient is weak and timid from the operation with iron, then take a thread made well of lead and tie it around it: in this manner as I have said, and release it after two days.
Then add to the tightness of the lead. Do not therefore cease to tighten the lead whenever it presses on the edges, until it is completely cut off and falls by itself without effort.
However, if the root were very thick, then there would be something to fear and be afraid of in making its incision, especially when it comes to the abdomen.
However, it is possible to make an incision through its middle. Then cauterize it thoroughly so that it does not reopen.
Then care for it until the wound is healed.
And do not assume that you should make an incision into any part that is dark in color, has little sensation, and appears foul; for it is indeed a cancerous ulcer, and I will speak of this with remembrance of cancer thereafter.
Chapter II.
§ On the care of the hernia (umbilical).
The care of the hernia (umbilical) should be given due attention, as it arises from many causes. Or from tears (ruptures) in the skin above the abdomen, through which it exits, be it fat, or intestine, according to what happens in other breaks. Or from blood flowing from a vein, or artery, according to what has been mentioned before. He was constrained by the urgency of the case.
However, if it is due to a crack and the exit is clean, then the colour resembles that of the body's flesh: it is soft, without pain, and appears in a different place. And if it is due to the exit of the intestine, then its position is with the condition that we have described, of a greater diversity. Therefore, when you press it with your fingers and it disappears, then later it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it, and it becomes very prominent at the bath entrance, causing great effort.
If indeed it is due to moisture, it is soft, then it does not disappear when you press it with your own hand, nor is there any constriction, nor is anything added.
Which if it is due to blood, then with these signs appears the abscess with blackening of the skin.
However, if it is due to new flesh: then the abscess is grey, thick, hard, and fixed according to one part's quantity.
If indeed it is due to heat, then its touch is soft.
And the operation in that case is to consider, if there is a protuberance of the umbilical region due to blood from an artery or vein or circulation, then it is necessary to prevent treatment of that condition. For in that case there is fear and trembling, as I taught you in the chapter where I spoke of the apoptoes due to the artery and vein.

Then tie him tightly around the abdomen with a strong thread or a firm silk string; and let the knot of the ligament be loose.
Then open the middle of the abscess that is extended above the ligament. If therefore you find the same now captured by the ligament: then extract the tongue, and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the abdomen; and if you find the liver, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If, however, it is against you an artery or vein: then surround it with much care. Then prepare for your operation. And take two needles, and insert in them two strong threads: and insert two needles around the circumference that you made around the abscess in the form of a cross, which you make to penetrate.
Then tighten the abscess at four places above the needles: and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the flesh becomes putrid: and it will fall by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and putrid.
Then take care of it with the necessary ointments until it is healed.
If there is an protrusion from the flesh of the navel, either from bone or moisture: then it is necessary to make a hollow abscess as we said before. And extract blood, or moisture, as you find it within.
Then care for the wound itself to solidify the place.
Head (Part) III.
§ On the care of cancer.
We have described the division of the kinds of cancer and how the way to its cure is with medicines: and the need to be wary from it in healing, so as not to ulcerate; and we have described cancer generated in the matrix, and the precaution in its healing.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where it is possible to eradicate it completely, such as cancer that is in the breasts or thigh, and those similar ones, from the parts located to be extracted all of it; especially when it is beginning in a small way.
When it is truly ancient and great, one should not approach it too closely.
For I have not been able to heal anyone from it; nor have I seen another before me who has succeeded in that regard.
And the operation on it, when located as we have described, is to precede, and thus relieve the patient from the black cholera several times. Then, if you find the phlebotomy itself obvious with a manifest fullness.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is spoken of when performing the operation.
Then insert into the cancer hooks that are suitable for it. If, however, the flow of thick blood from an artery or vein causes you trouble; then cauterize the vein until the blood is stopped. Then take care of it with the rest of the cure until it is healed.
**Of the cure for hydrops.**
Head (IV) We have discussed in detail the division of hydrops and how the accumulation of water occurs; as well as the signs of each type and its cure with medications.
And those that are treated with iron are nothing but the sole species of ascletis, and they do not approach the species of tympanitis with iron; nor do they penetrate completely to the species of hyposarcae, for that is deadly.
When, after having treated this species of tympanitis with medicines, the treatment does not improve it, then consider this: For if the weakness has already taken hold, or there is another illness with it apart from hydropisis, such as cough, dissolution of the chest, and others like them, then be wary not to treat it with iron, for that is a danger.
But if you see a patient with abundant strength and no illness other than hydropisis alone, neither young nor old, then the mode of operation is that you raise the patient standing in your hands, and have the assistant afterwards press his belly with his hand, driving water down to the lower parts of the ribs.
Then take a spatule with a sharp edge, according to this form. It has two sharp points, long, with a sharp end like a spatule. However, it should have some degree of thickness gradually, so that it does not penetrate with you during the operation up to the intestine and injure it.
Then consider. If there is a swelling due to fluid accumulation in part of the intestines, then it is necessary to lengthen with a cut from the navel with the quantity of three fingers, towards the lower parts in a straight line above the breastbone.
If the swelling is indeed due to liver disease: then your cut should be from the navel with three fingers on the left side.
And if the swelling is due to the spleen, then the cut should be on the right side: and it is not necessary for the cut to also be on the left side where the patient wants to lie, so as not to cause excess fluid to flow to that weak place.
Then pierce the skin with an instrument throughout its whole thickness. Then insert into that cut, and lift your hand with the scalpel between the skin, and make an incision, as if you were pricking it yourself: and the amount to be cut off should be about the size of a fingernail, or somewhat less.
Then make an incision through the scar, until the scalpel reaches the empty space: and that is the place of water. Then remove the scar tissue, and insert an instrument into the opening, which has this form. And it is similar to the cannula: it is made of silver, or copper, or brass, smooth: it has a small opening at its lower part, and on its sides there are three openings, two on one side and one on the other; and its end is cut, according to the manner of cutting the calamus, as you will see: at the top of its end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches water, then it immediately descends upon the instrument, thus drawing the quantity of water equal to that of the hour at once.
Because if you draw too much water from it, perhaps the patient dies in that hour through the resolution of his animal spirit; or he suffers a fainting spell that brings him close to death.
Indeed, the drawing of water from it is according to its strength, and according to what the weaknesses of the patient tell you, from the pulse of his strength and from his goodness.
Then remove the instrument and retain the water.

Chapter LVII.

Then extract it and make the wound flow through the urethra; and repeat the syringe, and do not stop doing this until the vesica is empty and the patient finds relief.

Chapter 60.
§ On the extraction of stones.
We have described the division of stone species and their treatment with medicines; I have explained the difference between stones formed in the kidneys, bladders, and signs thereof; I have spoken of the stone in which the operation takes place with the hand and the incision; and it is a stone that forms naturally in the bladder; and I am narrating this with clear exposition and abridgment.
I therefore say that stones formed in the bladder most often occur in children.

**Chapter LXIII**
On the care of flesh wounds.
