Chapter LXXXIV.
I have spoken about the treatment of wounds in the previous section of this book, and I will now describe here how to treat them with the hand and various medicines as required.
I say, therefore, that wounds differ according to the type of wound and the location on which the wound occurs.
The condition of the wound often resembles a blow from a stone; or a cut with a sword or knife; or a thrust with a lance, wood, or arrow; and many other similar things.
Wounds that occur according to the locations in the body are also diverse, such as a wound on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, or any other part of the body.
And I am describing the treatments for certain wounds in order to set forth this reasoning and rule for the rest of the wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, since the recall of the composers will come at the beginning of Chapter III.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs in the head, and there is no fracture of the bones, consider whether it was caused by a stone or something similar, and has already damaged only the skin: then it is a large wound, and you should apply cold compresses to ease the flow of blood from the affected part. Act quickly and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately, without delay. Remove the blood according to the amount of force required, unless there is something preventing phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is in small amounts. And the phlebotomy should be performed on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And the phlebotomy should be performed on the opposite side of the wounded part. And the patient should avoid overindulging in alcohol, heavy meals, and excessive wine consumption to prevent further complications.
If, however, something prevents abstinence from food, then reduce the amount of food and avoid meat and wine.
And if a hot abscess occurs, apply a warm compress soaked in rose oil or wine with astringent properties to the wound.
However, if you are free from the hot abscess, then apply a compress to the fresh wound still oozing with its own blood and not yet gangrenous; I will describe the kind of poultice to be used later. And if it has already turned gangrenous, then apply some ointment until it heals.
If, however, the wound is large, or it results from the incision of a knife, or similar, and its edges are not brought together with stitches, then these edges should be joined together with stitches, as I will describe regarding the wounds of the abdomen concerning their stitching.
However, if the wound is already exposed to the bone and hanging down, without any tension except for a slight suspension, then it should be incised. Then, care for the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh to it in place of the skin, and especially instruct the patient to take foods that are substantial, such as grain and cooked porridge, until it becomes firm to the touch.
If you notice putrefaction in the skin that does not adhere to the bone, then incise and remove the putrefied part entirely and treat it afterwards.
However, if there is an artery or vein in these wounds from which blood flows and it does not disconnect through medicines, then seek help regarding the artery. If therefore you find it uncut, then dry it with a sponge or bind it. If, however, there is a need to do so when it does not respond to what we said, then cauterize it until the blood stops flowing.
If these wounds are small and simple, then their treatment is easy and mild; so that they heal over this wound as if no wound were there, because it hardens before it changes.
**Mode of the Wound's Formation** Take one part of myrrh, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unburned lime; mix them well together, and sieve it; then use it to fill the wound, and tighten it with a good ligature, until it adheres well above, and coagulates with the blood.
Then tighten it from above with small threads and leave it untied, so that it does not loosen while it heals. For this medicine is not removed from it until it heals.
And whenever it (the wound) becomes a hard callus and you do not have myrrh or dragon's blood at hand, these substances still perform the same function in many large wounds when they are placed over them and tightened; and they are recent wounds filled with their own blood, which has not changed.
If, however, it has already changed with some alteration of air, especially in summer time, then the intention of this wound is not (to be) achieved; so take care of it using what we have said, so as to place over it certain ointments made from meal, or put on poultices made from barley flour mixed with water and honey, until it emits pus. Then care for the remaining part of the treatment until they are healed.
If indeed a fracture occurs in the bone of the wound and it is small, then extract it with the finger: and I will now speak of the treatment of fractures of the skull, as will be said in what follows.
§ On wounds of the collar (or neck)
If, however, a wound occurs in the collar, then there is no distinction between it and the treatment of other wounds, and between the wound of the head when it is simple.
If, however, a nerve or artery of the collar has already been damaged: then, if it is a nerve, there is no skill involved in treating it, except to take care of the healing with something that causes constriction, such as a bandage or a splint. Since nerves cause pain and constrict them.
And do not place anything cold on the wound at all: for the substance of nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is higher among the members; and it should be treated gently with medicines, as a boil washed with sweet water several times mixed with oil, or with rose oil, and turpentine, when it is also washed with sweet water; and all other mineral stones, according to this manner, and moist ointments.
If, however, the wound is large, then apply a suture; otherwise, close its lips with small threads. Then care for it until it heals.
If indeed the wound is deep, or there is a cavity in its lower part where pus has already collected, then perforate it in a more depressed place within that.
If, however, an artery has already been cut open in the wound and its blood flow is occurring: then separate or dry it, and tie that very spot; or cauterize it if need be.
If, however, the muscles of the throat have already been cut and are healing, then close the edges of the skin with stitches above the canal of the throat, and do not touch the throat itself, but rather equalize it, and reduce it to its natural shape. Then tighten the stitches gently, and leave it for a few days. Then care for it with ointments that have a constricting and drying effect until it heals.
If, however, the wound has already left an impression on the bone, then examine it; and if there are fragments of bone within it, remove all or as many as you can, and leave what cannot be removed, until the wound heals, and the discharge from it is separated and drained.
If the wound is fresh, then sprinkle powder over it as I have described. However, if there appears to be a bone in the wound and it forms a fistula, then treat that particular part as described in its own chapter.
**On Wounds in the Forearm**
If there has been a thrust with a lance or a cut with a knife, and you see the depth of the wound, then consider the following: If therefore a gust of wind issues forth from the wound and the patient grows weak from it, then know that this is a mortal wound.
If it is not deep and fresh with its own blood, then do not apply powder to it or press on it immediately, so that the blood does not get trapped in its depth and return to the heart, causing harm to the patient. But rather apply a strong ointment to it and loosen it twice a day.
If you do not have any ointment at hand, then place a piece of old cloth over the wound to absorb what seeps from it due to moisture; and make the patient rest upon the wound so that whatever is accumulated in it can flow out.
If indeed three days or more have passed since the wound, and no spasms occur in the patient, nor bad heart tremor, nor tightness in breathing, and you see that the rest of its dispositions are good, then know that the wound is healthy. Therefore, care for it yourself with caution and with the appropriate treatment, until it is healed.
If healing indeed excuses itself, and it is always opening, then know that a fistula has already formed. Therefore, care for it from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut with a sword or knife, and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back: then care for it as its previous suture, if it was large; or with dust, if it was small.
However, if it has already made an impression in the bone and a fragment has been severed from it: then examine the wound, and strive to extract those fragments, if they are separated. If indeed they are not separated, then leave them, until the wound putrefies, for extraction of them would only worsen the situation.
The judgment on the rest of the wounds' accidents in the remaining members is the judgment we have mentioned regarding their cure.
On abdominal wounds, the exit of the intestines, and their extraction.
The disruption that occurs in the intestine can be major, medium, or minor.
From the major, therefore, the intestine sometimes emerges, or the number of intestines.
Why this intrusion occurs, and why their sewing becomes more severe and difficult.
And the minor disruption is also difficult in another way. Therefore, it follows that their reduction becomes more difficult if you do not proceed properly to their place immediately; for they are inflated, opened, and enlarged, which makes the entry difficult.
For this reason, the medium disruption becomes better: because when it does not make the reduction of the intestine difficult, as it is delicate in these two types.
Know that the stitching of wounds is according to four modes.
The two common stitches meet in the stitching of the abdomen and in the stitching of the rest of the body's wounds; and the other two stitches properly communicate with the rest of the wounds. However, they are more proper when closing the abdomen: and I will tell you each one with its exposure and declaration.
I say therefore, because when a wound is fresh and something from the intestine protrudes from it, making it difficult to reduce, then that is why it is one of two ways. Or because of the fragility of the wound, as we said; or because there is an inflation in the intestine due to cold air.
When therefore it is thus, then you must warm it up so as to immerse a sponge or a damp cloth in warm water alone. Or dissolve it in hot water with squinantum, cypress, and spices, and wrap the intestine in it until the inflation is resolved.
It makes wine within it, which has a constricting effect; and it is better than water alone in resolving inflammation.
When therefore you see the inflammation resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which other herbs have been boiled. For it will also facilitate the entry of its humors with little difficulty.
However, if his return after this treatment is delayed, then find in the rupture a small amount, with the instrument used to cut hemorrhoids.
And the initial treatment is better when it is prepared, rather than cutting; and there should be no compulsion to cut after necessity has passed and the instrument is defective.
That part of it which is bent, acute, and the other part not acute, and its tip is not as fine as that of a knife, but rather more obtuse; and this one resembles chalk, as you will see.
When the wound has healed and the intestines have been restored, then it is necessary that its reduction be made according to its natural form in its own place, if possible; for this is better.
When the rupture is extensive in the lower part of the intestines: then you should make the patient lie on his back and place his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the intestines, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the intestines, then direct your attention and always consider that the part with the wound should be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you should apply it to large wounds and to medium wounds.
But in small wounds, apply it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make it so that there is a faithful servant between your hands who holds the wound entirely with his hand and closes its edges. Then remove from it something that follows the stitching at some point, and this is the common way of stitching one.
And it is necessary that you take a sharp point or a number of sharp points according to the quantity and width of the wound. Then leave a quantity from the edge of the wound equal to the ring finger, that is, of the doctor, and fix the point outside in both ends of the skin with two threads, with two edges facing what is under the skin from within, until you make it penetrate from another part, and now you have joined together both ends of the skin and both facing edges, and there are four parts.
Then tie with a double thread around the wound several times on both sides at the same time, until the two edges of the wound are joined together decently by the stitching.
Then release the thickness of the finger and fix another point in them; afterwards, pass it with a thread as you did with the first point. Do not therefore cease to perform that with it which you need from the bed, until you complete the closure of the wound entirely.
And there should be needles of intermediate size between thickness and thinness, because the thin ones penetrate flesh very quickly, and the thick ones have a difficult entry into the skin; therefore, they should be of an intermediate size in terms of both thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary for the needle to be positioned very close to both extremities of the skin, so as not to penetrate flesh quickly, and to open the wound before its healing, and not to elongate even with suturing, so as not to prevent the wound from healing.
Then cut the end of the needle so that it does not injure the patient during sleep, and put small pieces of cloth on all sides to hold the ends of the needle; then let them go until you know that the wound has already healed.
And this type of stitching is indeed more appropriate for small wounds with needles. Since it is enough in stitching them with one, or two, or approximately those needles.
The second type of stitching is common, as it joins with stitching four ends together; two skin ends and two sides in one action, with a needle in which the thread is equally entangled in terms of thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, bring the needle back from the part where you began, so that the threaded thread falls from the upper part of the wound, in order to make a stitch, just like a stitch made with cloth to bind cloths together, and put between every stitch the thickness of a smaller finger.
However, people know this stitch.
And with this stitch, I treated the wound that had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a larger disruption of the wound, about one palm wide, and it had protruded from the intestines about two palms from the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the belly.
I then reduced it after the intestine had stopped moving outside the wound after twenty-four hours.
So the wound was consolidated in space, having taken about fifteen days, and I took care of it, and it healed, and after that it lived for many years, changing into its original state.
And the doctors judged that it would not heal.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where I could not find ointment, nor anything from medicine. I therefore placed two layers of old cloth over the wound every day and insisted on washing it with honey water until it was healed.
I will now describe two types of suturing according to the description in this text.
And it is thus that one begins with the acute suture: introducing the blade from the outside towards the inside.
When the needle has penetrated the skin and moved along the wound, following the length and width of the entire body: leave one end facing sideways where you introduced the needle, and allow the other end to penetrate from the inside towards the outside at the opposite extreme.
Having thus made the first penetration, then make it penetrate in this way along the same extreme from the outside towards the inside: and leave the other end facing sideways, allowing the needle to penetrate the other end from the inside outward; and as you make this second penetration, follow the same path that you used for the first penetration on the opposite side, until it penetrates into the entire area.
Then begin again from this same side, and with the same end that was previously inserted, and extract the needle from the skin at the point where it entered.
Then insert it back into the same skin, and with the other end that was previously facing outwards, make a new penetration; extract it from the skin on the opposite side of its entry point, and repeat this process until all sides are completed according to this example.
And the essence of this suture is that it should envelop the edges of the wound as if they were a skin; so that you remove one part with one movement from one edge of the wound, and another part with another movement from the other edge, until you complete it.
A second species of suture, as described by Galen.
He narrates that this method is employed by certain surgeons in such a way that they combine all parts to suit their natural disposition, applying one end of the suture to the other and the other end to its corresponding extreme. And that is done according to what I have told you.
You must now fix your attention on the end of the suture closest to you from the outer part and cause it to penetrate towards the inside, leaving the two ends of the suture free. Then, taking hold of the suture again, cause it to penetrate from the outer part towards the inside on both ends of the suture.
Then, taking hold of each one again, cause both to penetrate from the inside out, reaching the other two ends of the suture mirach that are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture whose remembrance has come before.
This is therefore the discourse of Galen as well.
And some have said. The suture of the abdomen is corrected with two hooks. Which is, as you introduce into each one thread by the other: and begin with the introduction of the needle, from that which is with you, and make it penetrate until it reaches the other side, according to the stitching of their skins equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than those of the other parts of the abdomen.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound, is the judgment of the other wounds.
Which is because when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood, before it is altered by air: and reduce the intestines, and its own self, and make it suitable: then place over it the poultice of the flesh.
If the air has already altered the wound: then place on it some ointments which you have at hand, until pus forms: and the threads fall off, and it is healed completely, and miraculously. Then, care for it as is done for the healing of the other wounds until it heals.
However, if you do not have these medicines at hand, then apply them immediately to it as soon as it begins to emit pus, and change the dressing twice a day, as I taught you, until it heals.
For indeed, you do not need other medicines in most cases when the wounds are simple.
However, if there are instances where the wound communicates with the main parts of the body in the injury, then it is necessary to immerse a soft cloth in oil of a moderate temperature, or in rose oil: and place it around the areas that are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, it feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in their intestines (for this often happens), then cleanse it with a warm enema of vinegar: especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and becomes an exit that penetrates to its depth.
And know that what is from the thick intestine is easier to heal, and what is from the thin is more difficult to heal.
However, the intestine called the jejunum does not admit healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its abundance, being made up of thin vessels, and its own natural nearness to the nerves.
If, however, what proceeds from a wound is pus, and you cure it fresh: bring it back according to your method of reducing internal organs equally.
If a space has indeed been left, and it has already formed as a bruise or blackness: then you must bind it with a thread above the place that has become discolored from it, so that there is no blood flow. In pus, there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place both ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it: so that its attraction is avoided, and the extraction of that piece near the case of the pus wound, and the release of the pus from the wound.
**Reminder of the wound that occurs in the Intestine.**
When there is a rupture in the intestine, and it is small: then it is possible for it to be restored in some people: therefore, because I have seen those who were wounded in their abdomen with a lance, and the wound was from the right side of the stomach, and the old wound: and a fistula formed, and excretion came out from it, and gas.
I therefore applied treatment, until I was not confused about its healing: nor did I stop subtilely massaging it, until it healed and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the wound had healed, I feared for the patient, that some misfortune might befall him and cause a harm within his belly; but no such harm occurred to him from that misfortune. And he remained in good health, eating and drinking, engaging in sexual activity, taking baths, and adhering to his routines.
And certain experienced people said that when a wound occurs within the intestine and is small: then it should be bound in this manner. And it is like taking ants of great heads. Then bring together the two edges of the wound and place an ant on one of them that has an open mouth over the two edges of the wound. When it bites into it, and clenches its mouth, the head is severed because it sticks and does not come loose.
Then place another ant near the first, and continue doing this until all wounds are filled with ants in a sequential manner according to the quantity of the wound.
Then close it tightly and heal the wound. For indeed, there remain certain parts attached to the intestine which, as long as the intestine is fed and healed, do not cause any harm to the patient.
And indeed, it is now possible to tie the intestine even with a fine thread: which is extracted from the intestine of an animal, and attached to it after being introduced into the stomach.
And it is also possible to take the end of this thread from the intestine, and it becomes much fleshless. Then, tie the end of this thread with a fine linen thread. Then introduce that thread into the stomach, and use it with the intestine. Then reduce the lump into a disorder.
And this method of stitching with worms and intestines is nothing but according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is great, wide, especially if it is in one of the intestines' grat vessels: then there is no skill but to narrow it, and its healing is not at all.
**Of care for fistulas and abscesses**
You should know that every wound, or fistula when it becomes old and hardened, forms an ulcer, and does not heal, and always emits pus, and is not severed. Then it is universally called in any part of the body where it may be (fistula), and we call it "zuchemen".
Indeed, a fistula is a true hardened, white, filleted knot: without pain, but with a feeling of pricking; and for that reason some call it "risati", which means "penne".
And it is in some cases humid, which emits pus. And perhaps the humidity is severed at certain times. And sometimes this humidity is abundant, and sometimes it is scarce; and it is thick, or thin.
And I have already explained in division, when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor any flesh grow in it, is not that unless for one of nine causes.
Of which one is either for lack of blood in the body, or because of evil (disorder); or because there is hard flesh between it and its lips, preventing the birth of flesh; or because there are many abscesses and scabs; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter sent to it is of bad quality; or because medicine is unsuitable for its cure; or because corruption falls in the region, a kind of pestilence, or by reason of the property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, where digestion of ailments becomes difficult, and healing of fistulas is delayed due to nature; or because there is one opening, or a certain number of bones.
And I have now set forth all the causes and signs of these conditions, as well as their treatments with medicines in division; and here it is necessary that I make you know the treatment of fistulas and incisions, which are made through a surgical cut with the hand.
Know therefore that a fistula occurs in all parts of the body. For from these [fistulas] there are some that affect the great veins and arteries, or nerves, or passages; or they affect the intestines, or the ureters, or any of the ribs; or they affect any part of the spine or the connections of joints in composition, such as the hand and foot; and from them there are some that discharge near the primary member and similar ones.
Therefore, when fistulas occur in this way, they are difficult to heal from the illnesses and do not receive treatment, and dealing with them involves labour and ignorance.
If there is some fistula whose depth does not reach the places I mentioned, and you hope for and trust in its healing, and wish to learn about it, then accept attempts with air or iron if the fistula tends straight according to rectitude, and examine it with itself.
If, however, there is a curvature in the fistula, then investigate it with a delicate lead probe. Since lead, due to the softness of its body, is relieved and healed around its curvature.
If there is a fistula with many openings, it is not possible for you to point to it with your finger. Therefore, wash it from those openings through its own openings.
Since the moisture through which it is washed, flows towards other openings, and runs out of them.
Then, inspect it carefully according to what is possible for you: to recognize if there is a bone or a nerve, and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or close, or if there is one fistula with many openings that covers the entire area with your greatest strength. When asking about the patient's illness, and when finding pain at the compression point with your hand over the place, and similar signs and causes of accidents.
So when you have considered all these things and are standing at the right place, then proceed with treatment according to your confidence. If there is a fistula close by that appears, or in a healthy place far from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I told you about; then dry the fistula as mentioned earlier in my narration and remove what is inside it from leakage, and corrupt flesh, which arises at its opening from meat or something similar, and care for it until it heals.
If there is a fistula of great depth and it runs straight, then you must cut it as deeply as possible. Then cleanse that part from all corrupted flesh.
Then apply a clean, sharp knife to the wound and insert it into the depth of the fistula that you have reached with the iron, doing this several times until the acute medicine has corroded all that remains in the fistula's cavity from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that promote healthy flesh until it is healed.
If it does not heal from that treatment, then cauterize it as we have described.
If the cause of the fistula is a bone and you have verified this, then examine it and dry it as I have told you; if nothing prevents you from tying off an artery, or nerve, or a major limb, as we said.
If you therefore detect a bone that has something of decay and blackness in it, then cut through it until all the corruption is removed. Then treat it with what causes it to heal until it is cured.
If it does not heal and discharges pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved complete removal of the corruption.
Therefore, examine it a second time and make a thorough examination of its condition, and purification with all your remaining strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is deep and far within the body, which you have not achieved. You do not have any skill in this unless you let nature discharge it.
If the affected part is soft and it is possible for you, draw it out with delicate forceps suitable for that purpose.
However, if there are many bones, then carefully extract them all, and do not leave any behind with your strength.
If something causes you contrary feelings due to humors, and there is no fear or error in making the incisions: then cut away those humors and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If you do not have the preparation to remove the bones at this time, then place a poultice on the wound that is causing it to rot; and leave it until it has rotted away from around the bones due to the flesh and humors.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And the incision should be constricted, as you have made it during your operation. In my opinion, place over it, if necessary, a cloth soaked in triple-distilled sulfur and oil, or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or some kind of radiant ointment.
For when those putrefactions, that is to say moistures, have separated the bones from them, the sloughing of them should be relieved and their attraction removed.
However, if it is a single large bone, such as the shank bone or the hip bone, and similar bones, and that which has been corrupted from it is a significant part, then remove it with the last reason, until that blackness and corruption are gone. Then restore the wound.
If indeed what is corrupted in it is a significant part and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it needs to be tightened and cut entirely back to where the corruption begins; then care for it until it heals.
And I tell you of a case that occurred with a man on his leg: so that you may use this as an example and assistance in your operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years old who suffered pain in his leg due to a condition that had arisen inside his body, extending upward towards it from within, until the affected material reached the leg, and it became a great abscess. And there was no external cause for this.
Therefore, much time has been spent on him with errors of doctors until the abscess was opened and many materials came together with it. And the treatment of his condition was postponed until the leg became a fistula, and many openings were made in it, which all discharged pus and bodily humors.
So it was treated by many physicians for about two years, and there was no skilled hand in the art among them, until I was called upon, and I examined his leg and saw the materials that were flowing out of those openings, with a great flow, and even all over his body had already become covered, and his color was yellowish.
So I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached towards the bone.
I then examined all the openings, and I found them connecting to each other in all parts of the leg.
So I probed and followed over one of those openings until I found the part of the bone, and I found it corrupted: already rotted, blackened, putrefied, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I therefore cut out what I have discovered and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I marked that there was no corruption in the bone other than what I cut away and prepared. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied the restoration of the wound with long spaces filled with medicinal consolidants. And it was not consolidated.
Then I attempted it, and I found, above the first discovery, a second bone with continuous corruption; and I cut away what appeared to me even from that corruption.
Then I attempted the restoration of it. And it was not restored.
I therefore examined it again, and I did not stop striking the bone separately, and I tried to restore it; but it was not restored until I cut away about one palm's breadth of bone with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicine, and it was quickly consolidated and healed; and this repetition of the operation and cutting away was not necessary for its restoration, except because of the weakness of the illness, and the lack of endurance of the patient, and my fear of losing him to death, since he would have fainted at all hours from excessive vomiting.
Health is therefore complete healing, and flesh hardens naturally in the place of the bone, and a good arrangement is made within its body, and it regains its strength, and it changes to its dispositions, and causes no harm at all to its walking.
However, if a prominent bone in the body has already fractured, then it is necessary to secure it in this way, and so it is: take the ligament and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and instruct someone to gently pull it upwards, and place another ligament made from thicker wool around the first. Then bind it over the flesh that is against the bone, and extend the extremity to pull the flesh downwards, and you will uncover the flesh over the place where you want to secure it, so as not to harm the cut.
Place a wood or board under the bone correctly, because when you do this, it will not prevent the rotten flesh from being cut.
And it is necessary that there be a secure fastening over the rotten place for a little while, so that there is no gap between the bones, and it does not appear in its appearance to the eye, and it is compelled to attach itself in its own way.
If the bone is rotten and not prominent, then its part continues with another part, and the corruption is in the middle or in a part of it, then uncover the flesh from all parts completely. Then place the wooden splint below.
Next, cut it from the affected side using a saw, until the fracture is completely joined on both sides, and the splint extends according to the length of the fracture, as we mentioned earlier.
If the fracture is at the joint, cut through the affected joint itself, and saw through the bone where it continues on the other side.
If indeed the fracture is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no skill involved except for the sawing.
If the fracture is in the palm of the hand or in the toe joint: then the case is very difficult. Indeed, it is necessary that you inquire about the fracture as it appears to you. And you should cut it and clean it according to any arrangement possible for you, and with every skill that is rectified for you, as long as it does not cause opposition from a vein or nerve.
And know that cutting and sawing these bones require much skill, according to the position of the bone, and the preparation for cutting them, and their thickness, and size, and thinness, and rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare an instrument suitable for every kind of operation.
And know that these operations signify to you the kind of instrument you need when you have a long study with it. And the knowledge of these ways of this art is a testimony to the modes of sickness; for then you will indeed find within yourself what suits him from among the instruments for every illness.
And I am firming up for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will set examples over which you will incur trial and proof on others.
