When it becomes apparent, then perform phlebotomy on it as best you can, either directly or through diversion; and it is better to cut it with an incision or remove it with a section. Its location is indeed the healthier one, and it is hidden from many people.
Its location is indeed the healthier one, and it is hidden from many people. If you cannot find it and it does not appear to the senses at all, then perform phlebotomy on one of its branches, and that is the one that appears on the back of the foot, towards the ear; and beware of the nerves, and cause a quantity of blood to flow out as desired.
Then release the tension, place a covering over the site of phlebotomy, and tighten the area. It heals immediately.
**On Drainage and the Nature of its Use**
They become tubes from horns, and from metal, and from other materials.
And drainage is performed according to two methods. With scarification and extraction of blood: and another is venesection without scarification.
And this venesection, which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the venesections that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen places on the body. Of which one is venesection of the alnocratis, and is last on the head; and alkehel, and is in the middle of the collarbone; and venesections of the algadhahim, and there are two plates on the collarbone at the same time; and venesection of the mind, and it is under the jaw in the lower mouth; and venesections of the two spatulas; and venesection of the alachosos above the end of the tail; and venesections of the two forks, and they are in the middle of both arms; and venesections of the two tendons; and venesections of the two cavities.
Indeed, venesection does not extract blood from the minute veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, the strength does not leave them, as is the case with phlebotomy. It is not necessary to administer ventilation either with fire or without fire in any illness resulting from congestion, until the entire body is evacuated.
If, due to illness or habit, ventilation is required, we administer it at every hour, at the beginning and end of the month, and at any time in between.
This is because there are people who, when the blood becomes concentrated in them, need ventilation until it is extracted, and they find a heaviness and soreness in their head.
And from them, some experience congestion and redness in their face, head, and neck. And from them, some experience itching on their face and forehead, darkness, and drowsiness in their eyes.
And from them, some scratch the area of their ventilation. And from them, some experience an increase in laughter. And from them, some experience a taste of blood in their mouth; either their tongue becomes detached and they spit out blood. And from them comes one whose sleep is multiplied. And from them is he who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and those who are wounded, and sores, and things similar to them.
When therefore we see something of them, and especially if it is in the third part of the month: then we order the wind to be raised, after two or three hours have passed from the day.
The wind treatment of the nape is: because it conveys weight to the head, and what flows down to the eyes. Indeed, it is necessary that this be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this, indeed, is the wind instrument that takes the place of phlebotomy in the head.
And it is not permitted for one who has a cold complexion or catarrh to use this. For it inflicts great harm.
And because of that, one should not use them on the elderly, and in heads that have cold ailments.
However one persists in attending to it, oblivion overtakes him.
And for that reason, you must urge the windbag to place his hand gently downward for a moment, fearing the generation of oblivion.
Bloodletting using the windbag (Ventosatio or Alkehel) is indicated for cases of black blood (Fuscae phlebotomia), basic bloodletting (Basilicae phlebotomia), and it thereby brings relief from symptoms such as asthma, constricted breathing, instrument-induced fissures, coughing, and fullness.
And the windbag must be gently urged downward, for if it is placed too low, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
The windbag (Ventosatio) is indeed effective for sudden pains in the head, eyes, half-headache (hemigraine), perforation, and tooth root pain; it is used for basic bloodletting.
And you must urge the windbag to be careful not to use his hand with too much force during scraping; so as not to cut an artery and cause bleeding.
However, the windbag placed under the chin brings moisture to the mouth and helps with conditions like gingivitis and similar ailments in the mouth; it replaces other bloodletting methods used on both lips.
However, the ventilation of flaps is achieved through a combination of reflection and heat.
The ventilation of the inner parts of both these two vessels provides an aid to phlebotomy of three veins, namely, the Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: for it draws blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh; and these small veins draw from other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three main veins.
And it is necessary to be careful with the ventilation instrument so that it does not proceed too far in the scarification, for the area has been stripped of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
However, the ventosa that is ventilated over glands causes hemorrhoids in the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to instruct the ventilation instrument to be strong and made of air, for the location requires a powerful suction, and the delicate ventosas might break, and thus a significant scarification must be performed.
The ventosas of the veins reduce the swelling significantly: for they draw blood from the whole body and bring relief to old pains in the kidneys, ovaries, and vesica; they induce menstruation and relieve congestion and swelling, and take the place of phlebotomy on two saphenous veins and the varicose veins.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the opening of the windpipe (trachea) is close to the opening of both pleural cavities.
The quality of the position of the windpipes is to place the open end first in the vacuum and apply a tempered suction; do not prolong the position of the windpipes, but release them swiftly, and move them swiftly so that the humours reach the affected area with equal flow; do not cease repeating this process continuously until you perceive the place to be already red and inflated, and the appearance of blood redness; then make an incision and continue the suction gradually. Then consider the condition of the bodies.
For those of tender flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary to make incisions only once, not repeatedly; so as not to ulcerate the site.
And especially the windpipe (trachea), to enhance the incision, make it deeper; and regulate the suction with ease, swiftness, and its own motion.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to make incisions twice. In the first instance, the thin path of blood clarification and absorption opens; and in the second, the precise extraction of thick blood.
If indeed the blood is very foul, then let there be a third incision in order to reach the ultimate result.
And for the ultimate, when we wish to extract blood gradually or little by little, a single incision is sufficient for us. That said, if we wish for extensive blood extraction, we perform multiple incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it needs to be incised with a deep incision, and the depth of the incision is equal to the termination point in the cutting.
What is that which needs to be administered from oils at the position of the Winds, and what does it caution about the flow of air?
In that case where the skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, it is necessary to apply the Wind position places with open oils, soft, smooth: if in summer time, as is the oil of almonds, or whipped oil, or oil from sweet fig seeds, or oil from pumpkin seeds.
And if it is in winter time, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or syrup: and similar ones.
If there is a thick, cold excess, then use amaranth oil or balm of galgal oil or white oil or aniseed oil; and similar ones.
If there is a considerable swelling of soft tissues, then it must be prevented from oil and these places should be washed after venesection with rosy water or cold water or solar water or cucumber water or portulaca water; and similar ones.
He, whose blood is of great moisture, should wash the wounds with vinegar or myrtle water and sumac; and similar ones.
He whose excesses are thick, should wash his wounds with old wine or samach water or anise decoction or chamomile; and similar ones.
And it is necessary to be careful about venesection in a bath or in a bathing place; indeed, after exiting the bath, it is necessary to administer for an hour, or two hours.
And it is not necessary for anyone to sleep after venesection.
§ What is it that must be observed to regulate venesection or phlebotomy before venesecting?
And after that, it is necessary for you to consider first: if he is choleric, either Ventosatus or Phlebotomatus, then sharp acidity and inflammation will attack his blood; then it is necessary to take refrigerants, such as the granatum (pomegranate) peel, and also vinegar, lettuce, and willow bark, and similar things.
And remove his food particles, and the meats he chews, and foods seasoned with herbs; and similar things.
And for one whose condition is cold, it is necessary to take in syrup made from honey, or water, or willow bark powder; and also to consume perfumed wine that is between old and new; and he should be instructed to eat less food. And remove his food particles, and chewing, and birds; that is, small birds; and let the pigeons' chicks leave.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of ventilation and bleeding be more than food; and it is necessary to give certain people in the hours of tyro (cheese) a drink of alfaroch or a remedy of moss, that is, mastic, or asselith before ventilation: and before phlebotomy or after; in order to strengthen the principal members, and purify the blood. And it is not necessary for them to take it in a hot drink.
On chest drains without scarification. The chest drains (ventoses) that are placed without scarification are those put on the epigastrium, spleen, podagra, and over the abdomen, umbilicus, renal regions, and anca; for these parts do not endure scarification.
And they are not sought after except by attraction from a part of the blood to the member, such as we place a chest drain over the nipples in cases of nosebleed. And we use them to remove cold humidity from the member, such as when we place a chest drain over the abdomen and umbilicus. For indeed, it (the chest drain) rarefies the member, warms it up, and removes pain because of that, as it resolves that humidity.
They (chest drains) are also placed over the kidneys when there is an inflammation or a stone in them. By its power of attraction, it may perhaps open an inflammation or remove a stone from its place.
And similarly, when it is placed over the epigastrium and spleen to drain the humidity from them both.
And these wounds are treated with air only when they are cut open. And sometimes they are treated with fire.
And sometimes they are treated with warm water in cases of pleurisy: and that is to fill the wound with a great quantity of warm water alone, or water in which herbs that are suitable have been boiled.
§ Form of the treatment with fire and air.
It should be of such a width as the two fingers can cover, and in depth half a palm, with a small hole on its side, about the middle, through which the instrument may be inserted; it must be made of iron, steel, or brass, smooth and even, so as not to injure the surrounding parts, and in the middle of it let there be a cross-piece of iron or steel, where the candle is placed with fire.
And this air becomes now great, greater than we have described, and sometimes smaller, according to the illness and age of him who uses it.
For the air of boys and emaciated people is different from that of men and robust bodies.
The quality of placing this powerful flame over the member is such that it ignites the linen with a moderate fire, or a small candle from wax; and place it over the middle of the sword which is in the center of the flame, so that the fire is ignited around the lower part of the flame, so as not to burn the body below.
Then place it over the member, and the finger should be on the opening that I mentioned, until we hold the flame; when we no longer need it, remove the finger, and the vapor will exit through that opening, and the flow of air will be immediately resolved.
Then use the flame according to its mode, and repeat it if you need it.
However, in the case of the ventosa used to treat pleurisy with water, there is no crosswise sword or opening; and it is not applied unless the water is filled, and placed only over the member.
And this is its form.
And the ventosa is better the larger it is, as it can hold more water.
## On the Suspension of Sugar-Plums.
Sanguisugae should not be used on many parts of the body unless their small size allows for the placement of veins, such as in the lips, gums, and similar areas. Or in a bare member, such as the finger or nose, and similar areas.
And the quality of their use is as follows: one should come from sanguisuges (leeches) that are in sweet water, clean from putrefaction.
They should then be left in sweet water during the day and night until they become hungry, and nothing remains in their stomachs. Then the body is drained first with phlebotomy and with veins. Then the infected member is cleaned until it becomes red. Then they are placed back on top of it.
When they are inserted and fall off, and it is possible to suck the place with veins, then this is the greatest relief; and if not, then withdraw the place with vinegar. Then with much water, it is rubbed and squeezed.
If the flow of blood continues after the operation (amputation), and that blood is oozing out, then immerse a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top, until the oozing stops.
If the amount of blood is excessive, then apply pressure with a clean cloth or similar materials from the wound dressing until the bleeding stops.
Or they can be placed over the wound with half-boiled fabrics, and then removed until the bandages adhere to the place where the blood has stopped flowing.
And if it is necessary to repeat the amputation, as possible, the wound should be changed.
However, if the amputation cannot be suspended, then the wound should be cleaned with fresh blood. Or fix the bandages at the wound until something comes out of the blood. Then they should be applied, for they adhere immediately when they feel anything from the blood.
When therefore they (bandages) begin to fall off, apply to them something of crushed aloe or salt or ashes, and they will fall off immediately.

**And for this reason, this method is kept secret from the sciences in our land.** **And I have never found it at all good, and I have not acquired that from it unless through the length of reading ancient books and studying above their understanding, until I extracted that science from them.**
**Then I adhered to experience and study through the length of my life.**
And now I have described to you from that in this Chapter all that contains my knowledge: and on top of what has passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and set it free from the occupation of lengthening, and abbreviated it to the end of abbreviation, and shown it to the end of declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of the instruments used in it, with it being through the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary that we say in the Prologue of this Chapter, the main points of the discourse, and the Chapters to which one must attend for understanding the truth of their content: that you, and those who are eager, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when someone experiences a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or some other accident: then it is necessary that the nearest ones attend to him first for bloodletting and cleansing, or both at once: if nothing hinders, like weakness of strength, or he who has suffered the accident is a child, or an old decrepit person, or there is sudden heat, or extreme cold.
Then he should be satisfied with his food, cold odors, and meats of birds and fishes; and abstain from wine, fat meats, and filling up with foods and every kind of food that engorges the veins with blood. Until you are free from the heat of illness and do not suffer from excessive discharge of fluid to the limb; then the patient should return to his usual routine, with which he had previously become accustomed.
When the broken bone begins to heal, then the patient should be fed a nutritious diet with many rich and substantial foods, such as cooked wheat porridge mixed with water, rice, heads of vegetables, feet, entrails, fresh fish, eggs, and similar items. Also, he should drink hearty wine and other beverages like them. With this treatment, the healing of the fracture will occur more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in young men who are still strong, and in the case of the aged who are weak, it is not possible for them to continue and consolidate according to their natural state forever, because of the wasting away of their limbs; but those that are healed at the end of their weakness, such as the bones of children, will consolidate and continue in a similar manner.
However, nature attaches a certain amount of glue-like substance to the broken bone from every side, which increases its thickness and holds it together until they are completely conglutinated and bound together; until they have reached their final strength and firmness, as they were in the beginning, unless something prevents them from performing their functions. And for this reason, it is necessary to provide the patient with nourishing food that contains weight, richness, and thickness, as we have said.
And you should know that the kinds of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of members; for a fracture of the thigh is different from a fracture of the bones of the skull; and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and so too all the other members exist differently one from another. And I come now with a remembrance of all kinds of fractures exposed in its chapter, distinguished from others. And the kinds of fractures of bones themselves also differ.
Because it becomes an arched fracture, besides what happens in it of stars. And sometimes it becomes a fracture according to the length of the bone; and there are fractures of its splinters, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, as they are in a reed when it is bent with a knife for writing. And they do not proceed; and it is a fracture with a notch and disruption at the head: and it is a fracture of a small fissure.
And every kind has its own property, or specific tool for its restoration, according to what comes to mind regarding its remembrance, distinguished in its places.
And from that which is known as the fracture of a bone, there results its twist and eminence; and its appearance to the sense, and the sound it makes when pressed by your hand.
When there is no apparent twist in the place, nor does a sound occur when you touch it with your hand as if there were movement of the bone, and the patient does not experience intense pain: then there is no fracture there, but rather it may be a twist or a small fissure; therefore, it is not necessary to move it with your hand and compression completely, but instead place on it some medicine from what has been recalled, after allowing time for that which has occurred in the place to take effect. Then the tightness will be tightened gently.
And you should know that when the place arches and splits into two, beyond what happens there because of tension, nevertheless every part moves from its comparison; then you must begin from your moment to straighten it, equalizing it before it experiences a hot delay.
However, if it experiences a hot delay, then allow it several days until the delay has ceased. Then equalize it according to any manner that is possible through gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and equalization is easier than the bones where tension occurs.
Then tighten it according to what comes to your recall of it.
If indeed there are tensions in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured member from its two parts, if it is a hand or a foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two cords, or with a cord and a hand.
And thus it is your position to place the member over an equal surface according to its natural form until when the parts of the fractured member are extended; then try to return those additions by every means possible and in such a way as you can in their places, and be careful, according to your strength, so that no pain occurs to the weak through your operation, nor any harm. And try, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to better forms.
And at that hour, you must try and touch it with your own hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it and equalize it with the measure of your strength.
And be careful of excessive stretching and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For often they cause a hot apostema or contraction in the member through their own operation, as we have seen many times from their operations.
Then connect after equalizing, and applying a proper operation, and tightening that member, and ensuring rest, and tranquility: and warn the weak about it. And make it a priority that it does not move in the hours of its own will, and at its own rotation, agitation, and at its own movement, and all its motions with its utmost strength. And to prevent this, let not the position of the member be such that it causes pain when moved. For this reason, when it feels pain or injury in the position of a member, then it is necessary to change it to another position where it does not feel pain.
And also be careful that its position is equal and straight, so that there is no curvature in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the member's fracture is according to what I have described.
Know that fractured members differ in their weakness, magnitude, and shape.
Therefore, for those that are weak from them, such as the arm, finger, foot, and similar members, then it is necessary to use a soft, thin piece of cloth for their bandage. But for those that are thick from them, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary to have wide, hard bandages on them.
Because the wide ligament connects the great member and tightens it from all sides of equal constriction so that no spot enters into it.
Then, after you have evacuated the wound from the equilibrium, apply a poultice of suitable consistency over the site of the fracture. Then, begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture three or four times, according to the needs of the member, and slightly tighten your hand with the bandage.
Then, move with it to the upper part from the site of the fracture, and tighten less than your previous tightness, which you applied to the fractured place. Then stretch yourself with the bandage at the site of the fracture slightly, and soften the tightness gradually, until you obtain from the healthy place a great relief.
Then take another bandage, and wrap it again over the fractured place with times of wrapping. Then, move with the bandage to the lower part from the fracture. And let your work be in the tightening and softening of the bandage, according to what we said in the first superior wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings a soft pad or cloth, to adjust the curve of the fracture if there is any, and do not put anything on it if there is no curve.
Then insert another stitch above it. Next, equalise the stitches made from the centre, if there is no swelling or heat in that place.
However, if there is swelling or heat in it, then place a stitch above the spot where the swelling or heat sits, and remove the swelling; leave it for a few days. Then tie the thread above those stitches.
And the stitches should be made from the middle of a wide, shaved, and skillfully-formed cane, or from wood where sieves are made, from pine or palm branches, or from a calving hook; and the width of every stitch, according to this form and figure, should be the same.
However, the stitches placed above the track should be somewhat thicker and wider than the rest of the stitches; and the length of the stitch should be according to what is allowed by the member's size and condition.
Then tie the stitches together with another thread, according to the same tension as before. Then bind it from above with threads carefully, according to what I said about tension; and it should be that your tension over the fracture site is greater; and as much as it is elongated from the fracture, so the tension should be less.
And it is necessary that they [the threads] be of medium size in regard to thickness and fineness, and made of soft linen.
For if the threads are too thick, as is often seen in the clumsy work of those who use twisted linen cords, it is a great error, for the tightness does not suit them outside their natural qualities.
And very fine threads do not work either, for you will not achieve with them what you see through tightness.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's width between one thread and another.
If the thread is weakened at its ends by the tightness of the stitches in healthy places, then place under them a piece of soft cloth or dyed wool until nothing is damaged from that.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and there occurs a disruption in the skin, I come to recall it in Chapter Two according to its particularity.
And know that not every fractured member needs to be bound with threads from the first day. This is because when a member is large, you do not need to put stitches over it until after five or seven or more days, according to your safety from the event of heat removal.
And this is the method of using plasters by which the ancients restored what was broken and dislocated.
The common method of the plaster, restoring a fracture, consisting of many plasters, particularly for women and children, since it is applicable to what neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And take some flour from the mill, and there is a core of flour that hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the millstone; and make it as it is, except that sifting it with alum makes it better. And do not make its thickness too thick or too thin. Then use it.
Another method of the plaster, restoring a fracture, dislocation, and torsion. Take almonds, laurel, acacia, enele, and mugath; and honey, of all these in equal parts as myrrh, and aloe half as much; and all these are sifted and mixed with water or alum, if the plaster for the sick is warmed. Then use this plaster. Indeed, it is a good composition, restoring broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another method of applying plaster, which also reinforces fractures and twists. Prepare a mixture of equal parts of myrrh, aloe, and Armenian earth (terra sigillata), all finely crushed and mixed with water or albus (calcium hydroxide). Administer as needed.
A method for applying plaster to joints and separated bones, soothing the pain of their injury, and healing cracks in bones and fractures. Take sucking wool and immerse it in vinegar and boiled oil, then place it on the affected area.
And this plaster is not a power of restoration, but it is good for the alleviation of the burning of blisters and it expels pain properly.
A method of plastering that restores a broken bone. Take dried fig leaves and paper from the mulberry tree, mix them well, and make a plaster from both.
A shortened method of plastering, administered during the restoration of a broken bone, and when you see the remaining scar diminishing. Prepare equal parts of the root of ginger, chamomile, flowers of marigold, and herb flour of all kinds; mix them together, and add wine if the member is not already heated.
If the member is indeed heated, then prepare it with moist coriander water or simply with water, and use it accordingly.
Another method for making a stronger plaster for resolving apoplexy, more powerful than the first, when there is a hard fistula near the restoration of the member. Prepare equal parts of the root of ginger, flaxseed, ballist (possibly a type of plant), melilot, samas (a possible spice), marigold flowers, and chamomile, mix them together, and prepare them with willow water, sweet water, or wine. All of it should be according to the warmth of the member and the calmness of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a broken member during its restoration, except for a bandage made of lint and oil; nothing else. He told that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
Galen, however, observed that they should place things on a broken member during its restoration to reduce the heat, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, and similar substances that we have mentioned.
Method of applying a plaster that relieves the injury and pain. Mix equal parts of meal, chickpeas, human hair clippings, or bird feathers and alum, grind them, sift them, and prepare them: and thus an emplastrum is made.
The amount, however, should be such that the bandage remains intact, and then it is dissolved when you consider that if the patient does not experience pain, itching, or the broken bone does not move from its place, then it is not necessary to dissolve it for many days. If it happens that he feels an unbearable itching, restless pain, or swelling: then remove and dissolve the emplastrum at once from that place, and take a soft cloth or a wet sponge, dip it in warm water, and wash the affected area with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides.
If it happens that he feels an unbearable itching, restless pain, or swelling: then remove and dissolve the emplastrum at once from that place, and take a soft cloth or a wet sponge, dip it in warm water, and wash the affected area with it until the itching stops and the pain subsides. Then allow the member to rest for one hour. Then place a cloth soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, on top of it and bandage it for one night, until you are free from the heat's discomfort, and the swelling in the member subsides, and its pain is relieved.
Then repeat the process, applying a tighter but milder bandage and a lighter emplastrum, without applying excessive tension with your initial strong bandage, until it is healed.
If you observe that the ulcer, redness, pain, swelling and all other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you now need only a plaster and compression, repeat the process as you did initially.
If none of the aforementioned conditions occur in a member after three, four, five or seven days, then do not drain it unless absolutely necessary. And when after twenty days the entire condition of the member has improved as you observed from its drainage, according to what we said, until the fracture heals and the coagulation of the flesh over it approaches, then at that time add even more compression than your initial compression, and also add in increased intake of low-nutrient food, according to what was mentioned before.
However, if you observe that the site of the fracture is already dried out and contracted more than necessary, then know that nutrition will be prevented from reaching that part. Therefore, drain the member with warm water for all three days of cleaning, and apply relief compression briefly every day.
By this procedure, the member receives nutrition, and healing takes place quickly.
However, what the ignorant healers do by draining another part of the member's fracture, when it does not restore according to what is necessary, and it restores according to its distortion, is an error from their operation, and a great fear; and if it had been right, they would have said so in their old books, and acted accordingly.
And I have not seen their mark anywhere on that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
**On the Handling of Accidental Wounds in the Head.**
**The kinds of fractures are diverse, and their appearances numerous: their causes are distinct.**
**For there is a fracture that occurs from the blow of an axe: the blow removes the bone entirely until it reaches the sinew beneath, just as an ax cuts through wood; and because of this, this type of fracture is called axial.**
**Or, an axe may strike something of the bone itself, and it strikes its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to its end; and this is called a black fracture, that is, complete destruction.**
**And it is a wound of two fractures, either great or small.**
**And there is a fracture that is a crushing or grinding, and its cause is the blow against a stone, or falling upon a stone, or something similar.**
**And this fracture is also one that penetrates to the membrane beneath the bone, or is on the surface of the bone, and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or large.**
And from this fracture is one that is hidden within the bone, subtle as a hair; and it is a small fissure, hence this type of fracture is called Capillary Fracture.
And from these there arises a fracture due to an accident or the striking of a stone, and similar conditions which cause the penetration of a slice of bone into its insides, creating a concavity; as happens with caldrons of air when struck, and part of them penetrates inward.
And indeed this most commonly occurs in the heads of humid bones, as are the skulls of children.
And all these types of fractures become separated, and I return with the recollection of them all in their proper place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures through the detection upon them, and inquiry with touch, and the removal of corrupted flesh which is upon them.
The true Capillary fracture is recognized by the fact that it is detected upon the bone, cleaned, and bandaged over it with wax; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fracture, however, consider first the condition of the patient; for if you see that from the accidents there is a clear indication of fear, as in the case of cholera, prostration, loss of intellect, loss of speech, fainting, acute fever, discharge of the eyes, redness thereof, and similar accidents in many positions, then undoubtedly he will not recover; and if you do not find that the accidents cause you fear, but hope for recovery, then begin his treatment.
What is it if a wounded person comes to you in the initial stages of their injury: and that is, during the colder days, then it is necessary that you should study the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all arrangements.
And if it is during the warmer days, then you should aim to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the tissue beneath the bone degenerates and that condition we described occurs.
If the fracture of a bone has already reached the point of touching the brain, and it is with minute pieces and attraction: then you should cut into the broken and crushed part, as I have told you.
And you should also clean the head of the wounded person: and remove the tissue above the bone as best as you can; and according to the shape of the wound, and what is weak on top.
If this happens to you during the examination of the bone, such as bleeding or a hot discharge: then oppose it with what is necessary. And you should fill the space with cloth soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest until the discharge stops, and be secure from bleeding. Then begin the cure of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
The first method is to cut the bone with a sharp blade of straight edge, and this is its form.
Then, apply another blade (more wide) shortly after the first cut, and this is its form.
Next, apply another blade even wider than the second one.
And the second method is to have many blades at hand, some wider and others narrower, all with their tips sharp, made of iron or tempered; and use them with ease by striking upon the blade, so as not to hurt the head or damage itself.
But if the bone is hard and dense, then it is necessary to drill holes around it before applying the blades, using what are called non-drilling chisels; these are not so named unless they do not penetrate beyond the surface of the bone into the softer part beneath. This is because the rounded end of the chisel resembles a margin, and a very small circle prevents it from sinking or penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate for you to have many of these chisels, each suited to the thickness of the bone, so that there is a chisel suitable for every skull; and their acute ends should be in length and brevity according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of terrors, or trepans.
The quality of drilling around a broken bone is as follows: you place the trepan over the bone and use your fingers to turn it until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the trepan to another place and place what is between every hole and the thickness of the radius, or about that.
Then cut with the incision tools what is between every hole from the bone, and make it thinner than you can from the fine edge, as we said, until the bone is removed, either by hand or with some other tool you have prepared for this purpose, such as pliers or thin saws.
And it is necessary to be careful not to let the trepan or incision tool touch anything that could harm them.
When you have surrounded the bone with drilling and separated it from the surrounding tissue, if it is still attached to it, you should clean and even out its edge that remains in the bone with another similar incision tool. However, it should be thin and subtle more than other incision tools.
If there remains anything of small bones and soft tissue, then take them with ease, as you have at hand from your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with lint and ointments as we have said.
And another way of operating is easy, quite distant from fear: as Galen said and praised it greatly, and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin detaching the bone part from the place where the fracture is more severe and apparent, until when you detect that part, and position under it the extremity of this lens-shaped incisor, whose form is this.
And the lens-shaped part does not cut anything when it is soft; and its sharp edge on both sides cuts along the length as you can see, so that the lens-shaped part is inserted towards the chisel, with its sharp face cutting into the bone.
Then strike the chisel with a small mallet from one side until you cut through the bone easily, as is desired, and be careful from the cloth to prevent any accident that you might fear.
And if the physician is not more foolish than other men and less ignorant of it, and if he is not sleeping, then if there remains something attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it off with the edge of his lens-shaped incisor and free it easily. It is indeed freed from him without harm and without fear.
If a bone fracture does not penetrate to the flesh, and what is detached from the bone is its face, leaving in it roughness and subtle adhesions, then you should smooth that roughness and remove those adhesions with the fine rasors you have prepared in various quantities, so that it is possible for you to apply them in any place more suitable, according to what the operation leads you to, and the shape of the fractured bone itself. And it is necessary that you administer according to your judgment, how much of those rasors you first used on the bone. Then use the finer ones after that.
And it is necessary that you administer according to your judgment, how much of those rasors you first used on the bone. Then use the finer ones after that. And do not cease to do that according to continuity until you reach the point of administering the thinnest and subtlest of them all.
In the case of any remaining small cracks or subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that should not be hidden from anyone who has any study in this art, and it stands over what we have written and demonstrated regarding large fractures.
If, however, the skin is detected and retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth, making it as comfortable as possible so that it does not hurt.
Then apply a broad ligature above it, but do not tighten it unless necessary to hold the wound; release it after one or two days of this treatment until you are free from fever's departure. Then loosen it and apply medications such as lily roots, herb flour, turd silk, aristolochia, and similar substances to remove the dryness; make a powder from these drugs that can be applied to the wound like a dry crust.
And universally apply any medicine that has the property of removing it without causing pain or irritation. And be careful according to your ability so that the wound is clean during your treatment; do not allow it to become dirty with ointment or filth.
And be careful according to your ability so that the wound is clean during your treatment; do not allow it to become dirty with ointment or filth. Do not let pus form on it at all; for when pus collects over the skin of the brain, it corrupts and putrefies it, causing great harm afterward; this has already happened in a patient's head when the bone is cleaned by him, especially when his care is neglected, resulting in discoloration on its surface.
Therefore, consider then if an unfortunate accident occurs, as we have described, you should know that it is certainly harmful and likely to cause great damage.
And if blackening does not occur, except due to the medicine placed on it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackening, then you should take one part honey, three parts rose oil, and a cloth with them. Then place it on the wound; afterward, care for the wound with suitable curative agents until it is healed.
§ On the restoration of our body when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except at both its upper parts simultaneously, or at least one of them; for it consists of two bones, and the lower part is cartilaginous, so it does not break, but only suffers from friction, distortion, and compression.
If one of the two parts of the nose breaks, then you should insert a thin finger into the nasal hole and equalize the fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and it should be done skillfully and easily. And moreover, so as not to cause that pain in the patient through your operation.
If there is a fracture in the upper part of the nose and it does not reach the tip of the finger, then it should be adjusted so that it aligns with the end of the radius, where there is a small thickness.
If indeed there is a fracture in both parts, then do it like this: and nearby with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the time of the patient's comfort from heat.
Then insert a thin strip of linen into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts, and let the thickness of the linen be such that it fills the nasal hole.
And some ancient restorers have said that linen should be dipped in butter and changed every day. That does not seem right to me; rather, linen should be soaked in a paste made with ground almonds. Then remove the linen until the bone hardens and the cartilage solidifies.
And sometimes a quill of a duck's wing is introduced into the nose where a thin cloth has been inserted, and it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly, so that the patient is not hindered from breathing.
And this is not a necessary thing. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do the latter.
If, while operating on the nose again, the wound should become infected, apply a warm poultice to the nose with lint or cotton dipped in rose oil or a small amount of ointment.
However, if there is no sign of a hot infection, then it is necessary to apply a poultice with sesame flour and dust from the husk of a date palm. Then place a soft pad over it, and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are indeed broken or crushed, then you must cut them apart and extract the pieces with the instruments that are suitable for this purpose. Then use the appropriate cutting tool, and treat it with whatever helps it to heal and solidify from the suitable poultices or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose and eye, then it is necessary to treat it with liniments, and insert a lead cannula until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then note if there is merely an external fracture, not splitting into two parts, and it heals from the inside, then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if there is a fracture in the right part of it (the jaw), insert your left index finger into the lower left tooth; likewise, if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the right hand index finger; and push with it the deformity of the fracture inwardly towards the exterior with your other hand from the inner side of the bone correctly and evenly.
If the lower jaw is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer an extension to both parts according to their correct alignment; until it becomes possible to equalize them.
However, if it has already happened that there is a disturbance or dislocation of the teeth: then bind them with what you hope will remain, using gold, silver, or silk thread. Then place a piece of cloth over the broken mandible. Then place over it a doubled cloth, and on top of that a large, neatly made pad: or a piece of skin from the sole of the foot equal in length to the mandible.
Then bind it firmly from above, according to the preparation of its ligature, and let its application continue until it is not destroyed; and especially instruct the patient to use tranquility and quietude, and give him light food.
If, however, you estimate that any change of form has already occurred in some way, then approach it and loosen it on the third day. Then straighten what has been altered from it, and apply a plaster made of ground flour, with albut or sesame flour after you have removed the cloth; and place a soft woolen cloth on top of the plaster.
So long as that plaster remains attached and the arrangement of the bones is not altered, do not loosen it, until healing has occurred; or tighten the fracture. This fracture is often tightened in three weeks.
If, however, an abscess should occur during this time, then administer what we have said many times for its relief, until the abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider it. If a fragment has become separated from the bone, either a small piece or a large one, then be delicate in this regard, so as to remove those fragments of the bone with the instruments at your disposal.
If the wound opening is tight, then widen it with a phlebotom according to the amount needed by you.
Then, when you have removed those fragments and nothing remains of them, open the wound again if it is wide, and if not, place over it the appropriate dressings and leave it to heal.
§ On the restoration of the furcula (fork) when it is broken.
Indeed, the furcula is most often broken from its anterior part near the shoulder, and the fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Or it may break and separate into two parts, other than when fractures occur within the bone, and then its restoration is easier.
Or so that fractures occur within the bone: and then its restoration is more difficult.
Thus, in the case of a fracture with an open wound.
And the operation in such a case when there is no open wound is as follows: you must have two assistants, one holding the fractured bone in place while the other extends the neck towards the affected part.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, so that there is neither a protrusion nor a depression.
If you need further extension, then you must place a small ball of cloth or wool under the fracture and adjust its size according to your needs, then extend and lift the fractured bone with your hand until it is equalized.
If you cannot pull the end of the bone towards the exterior because it has gone deep inside, then the patient must lie on his side and place a medium-sized pebble under his elbow, and have an assistant press down on his arm until the opening of the fracture moves up to the surface, at which point you can equalize the fracture with your fingers.
However, if you feel that there is a lodged piece of bone from the fracture, which moves, then you must locate it and carefully extract it.
If it is retained in the bone, then use a cutting tool that you have prepared for that purpose, after placing under the furca an instrument that preserves safety; and the instrument is of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It is shaped like a snail shell, without any indentation.
And its width is according to the need of that part from the magnitude of the bones and the thickness of it. However, its length is also according to what is possible for you during the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you can see: one end is wide, and the other is narrow.
If, however, the wound that you made when extracting the bone shard is wide, and you are secure from further damage by heat, then sew together two edges of the wound with stitches.
And if the wound is small or there is no further damage by heat, then fill the wound with cloth and small stitches according to the amount of the wounded cut.
If there is an infection due to heat, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine and place it on top.
The true stiffness of the bone when it is without a fracture or incision is like placing over the bone a plaster made from ground flour and alum. And place a soft pad over it. Then place a sphere under its swelling, if necessary. Then take a bandage, that is, the long cloth used by the Saracens, which should be at least one palm wide, or somewhat less. Then place a doubled cloth over the swelling and plaster. Then take a star-shaped piece of thin wood, whose width and length are each the size of three fingers. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the star-shaped piece inside, in the appropriate place over the fracture and its area. Then place the plaster over the fracture as desired, and wrap it around the collarbone and under the healthy and weak swellings, and bring it down over all parts, and as you can see, the stiffness holds the fracture firmly in place; and it is not hidden above you, and all together, so that the star-shaped piece does not move from the fractured bone. And for this reason, you must look at the patient every day. Whenever the swelling subsides and you see the joint start to move again, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the patient's sleep on his back and put under his pillow a little of warm water by his side at night, so that when he turns, help will come to him from his own side. Thus, the fracture of the fork will be reduced by raising the arm.
Or tie the arm to the collarbone; and do not loosen the ligature unless some accident happens from itching or dislocation, until after twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see it coming off from his face towards that. And add to the compression, and leave it alone until it is restored and coagulated.
And indeed, the fracture of the fork is compressed and healed within twenty-eight days; and it happens in some men more quickly.
# On the Restoration of the Fork Fracture.
The spatula rarely breaks, and only at its wide end; it does not break off from that part unless the edge gives way.
When therefore the part breaks off from that point and breaks in the middle of it, and is not recognized except by touch, then according to the form of the fracture attempt its preparation. And reduce it according to the natural form of every possible skill to you.
Then place a layer of flour on the millstone with alum and a soft cloth, and put a small piece of doubled cloth on top. Then place on top of it a broad lattice made of thin wood according to the size of the spatula or slightly larger.
If indeed there is a hollow in some parts of the spatula, then equalize that hollow with the soft cloth until the lattice descends according to its equality.
Then gently pull it with a long and tight string, until the lattice does not move from its place.
And consider the string every day. Whenever the ligament softens, tighten it and restore it to its original position if it has moved; and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the spatula will be restored in twenty days, or in thirty-five days.
When this number is reached, then loosen the ligament; and you will be in safety. For it is made of bones whose joints are not to be feared, nor their dislocation.
However, if it proceeds from a fractured bone and irritates under the skin, then cut above it and remove it. And do as we said regarding the fracture of the fork from the heat of the fire if something happens from it.
**On the restoration of the fractured chest**
The chest sometimes fractures in the middle of itself, and it rarely happens. Its extremities break most easily and become fractured from its accidents.
When the middle part of his leg, which bends towards the lower part, is broken and causes unbearable pain, as well as difficulty in breathing and coughing, and perhaps he loses blood, and the fragility of the broken bone becomes apparent to the senses, its restoration is such that the patient can rest on his back again, and place a poultice between his legs.
Then press his shoulders and join the ribs to both parts or sides with the hands, and adjust it delicately until its shape returns to the bone's natural form as required.
Then place a plaster and a compress on top of it, and put a lattice made of thin wood from willow or chalk, and similar light materials over it after you wrap it in cloth. Then gently wrap it around the broken bone to prevent movement, and tie it with the bandage according to its curvature towards the back several times, and tighten the knot securely.
Then regularly check the bandage and tighten it whenever it loosens, and if its need prompts its dissolution near the itching or corrosion that occurs at the site, or pain, or swelling, then remove it, loosen the bandage, and correct what has occurred during the course of its cure.
Then reduce the plaster if you see it is bulging, and attach a new bandage to it until it heals.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
You should know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur unless there are significant areas following the spine and extending from their ends towards the front part. And they do not suffer friction unless because they are cartilage-like. And the knowledge of itself is not hidden from sense upon inquiry with fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the fracture is restored with fingers according to the possible manner until it achieves the form, as it should be. Then apply poultices, and tighten the broken bone with strings if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclining towards the interior, then a severe pain, as well as a piercing, occurs, similar to that piercing which happens to those who have pleurisy: for the bone pierces the membrane, and also there occurs difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of much blood: and this is a difficult healing.
And the ancients have already devised many ingenious methods for it: and among them are those who say that you should give to the patient foods that produce inflammation and venting, so as to inflame the bowels, and extend them, and drive the fracture out towards the exterior.
We should indeed avoid it, so that a rapid cause for making a hot fistula does not occur, if it is not necessary. However, if it has already occurred, then add to it.
And some of them said: A flow should be placed over the place, then it should be drawn out with force, and it is more in line with reason. However, there is fear that the flow will attract too much to the place due to the weakness of arrangement.
And some of them said: The place should be cooperating with the wool which is already submerged in hot oil: and small pieces should be placed between the bones, until it is filled; so that there is an equal ligature when they insert it according to the curvature.
Then the patient cares for the healing of the pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the patient is in great distress, to such an extent that he does not tolerate it, and the bone pierces the membrane with painful stabbing, and we fear for the patient's condition: then we must break open the place above, and remove the fractured rib. Then we place an instrument that maintains the cure, whose memory preceded: and we cut the bone with ease, and we cut it itself.
Then we will bring together the two edges of the wound if it is large and requires stitching: and we will treat it with bandages until it heals.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess develops, then apply it with suitable remedies and insert small pieces of rose-colored oil into it, and place a covering over it: and the patient will be cured by what is appropriate for the abscess.
Also, the patient's body should be turned over onto their side where they sleep until they heal.
§ On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Indeed, a fracture of the bones of the neck often occurs and rarely does friction happen to them: likewise, the bones of the spine too. When this happens to someone, and you want to know whether they will heal or not, then consider. For if you see that their hand is already softening, and numb, and dead; and they cannot move it, nor stretch it out, nor contract it; and when you press it with your fingers or prick it with a point, they feel no sensation or pain: then know, that they will not heal, and in many cases it is fatal.
If, when moved, they feel a tightness with the fingers and a puncture, then know that the back of the bone is sound, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If it affects the spine in a similar way, and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider his feet: if you see that they have become soft, and this happens to them as it does to his hands, then when he lies on his back, breathlessness and straining occur without willing; and when he turns over on his belly, the straining occurs without willing; and when he turns over on his back again and wants to move, but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not despair in his treatment.
If indeed nothing of that happens to him, the condition is lighter.
And the treatment for what happens from that is as follows: to apply a poultice of cooled rose oil only on the affected spine; or when the pain has subsided, use a poultice of herbs.
Apply this several times a day until the heat of the apostema subsides. Then place over it one of the emplasters that soothe, draw out, and tighten, and secure it with a bandage; and especially instruct the patient to use rest and tranquility. He should not sleep over the affected part until the pain has subsided.
If this occurs near the friction of a broken bone, or something that has already separated from it: then one should, as it were, cut through the skin and remove that part. Then close the two edges of the wound together with stitches if it is large. Then care for the wound with some medicinal ointment until it heals.
If indeed the last part of a bone breaks and it is at the end of a limb: then one should, with the thumb of the left hand, insert into the opening, and with the other hand, equalize the broken bone as best as possible.
Then place something over it and remove the part that was removed: and care for the wound with the cure that preceded, until it heals.
§ On the restoration of an anchor.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the anchor rarely break. If they are broken, then their fracture is not, except for a comminution at the extremities; and they split along their length, and bend towards the interior, and there occurs pain in the affected area and a puncture, and the leg that breaks becomes stunned.
And its restoration is such that you extend your hand over it until you rest on the fracture as it is shown in the figure.
If the fracture is only at the extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you from the equation until its natural shape is assimilated.
However, if the fracture is along its length, or it has already bent towards the interior, then make the affected person lie on their side until a restoration for that fracture is prepared. When you have equalized it, place a plaster over it, then place a frame made of wood or skin on top of it, and tighten the bandage above it so that you do not fear any change in the fracture, nor the removal of the frame.
And it should be equalized with what fills it, until the bandage takes hold according to its equality; and especially instruct the affected person to sleep on their back or their healthy side.
However, if there occurs a hot apthous ulcer: then stop the extension and restoration of it until the hot apthous ulcer is seated; and place over it what sits on it according to what has been said previously, then return to its restoration, and tighten it as appropriate.
If a fracture occurs in a bone, or it is broken, extract it.
**On the Restoration of Fractures (with the Aid of God)**
That aid is between the elbow and the head of the spatula.
If therefore it (the aid) is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two ways.
Of these two, one is that you take a flat wood of medium thickness, according to this form that follows.
And two ligaments are bound at both ends.
Then it is suspended from the elevated position, and the weak (part) sits on a support. Then he projects his broken arm over the wood until it reaches its (the wood's) thickness, where a curvature of the wood is attached in the middle.
Then he suspends something heavy from above, so that the helping hand extends down below. Then the physician should reduce the fracture with his hands until it is healed as it ought to be.
There is another method to raise the patient onto his own head: and suspend his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then command two assistants to hold one of them above the fracture with his hands, and let the other hold it below, extending each to their own side.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then tie below the fracture with one bandage and above it with another, and each of the two assistants should extend to their respective sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the humerus, then it is necessary to place one bandage below the joint and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary to place a bandage over that place and above the very elbow.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without strain until it is healed as it ought to be and applied properly: then tie it if there is no risk of fever.
If indeed there is a fever, then release the bandage until the seventh day. And place over it a linen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until the wound rests; then bind it, and the manner of binding it is such that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a new cloth over the plaster. Then bind the arm over itself, and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm over itself, so that the arm remains in its place as if it were the stars; if nothing prevents you from doing this, or something does not disturb it above the broken bone.
If, however, anything hinders you from doing this, or something disturbs it above the broken bone, then use supports; and these are such that you place over the fracture itself a support which is broader and stronger than the others. And place between every support the breadth of a finger; and the length of the supports according to the fracture, with the addition of three fingers from every side.
Then bind over the supports the tightening as I said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is such that your tightening over the place of the fracture is tighter. And the more a fracture is elongated, the less is the contraction.
If therefore you see the position of the stars and the contraction, as we said, at the hour when you restore the member immediately, then do this: and if you fear a hot apposition, then release the contraction and let the stars remain until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the ligature for all three days, so that there is no itching, or swelling, or prevention of food reaching the member due to the excess contraction. Therefore correct that whole thing according to what we said.
If indeed you have great confidence that nothing will happen from it: then do not release the ligature, unless after three days or more.
And let the nurse lie on her back, and her hand on her stomach: and place under her a pillow filled with equal wool.
And consider at every hour of the night or day, so that the form of the broken member is not released, or the ligature becomes soft. Therefore correct that whole thing with your strength.
And place food for the patient according to the order we have mentioned, so that it is a subtle food at first, until the mouth begins to tighten; then it is necessary to make the food larger.
For the purpose of aiding and strengthening the leg, it should be tightened for forty days; therefore, it is necessary to loosen it, apply a bath, and treat it with ointments that correct the condition.
If the fracture is severe and dislocated, do not release the ligature from it and do not reduce it until after fifty days, that is, until two months have passed.
**On the Restoration of a Fractured Arm**
The arm is composed of two bones, called the humerus and the radius.
Of which one is smaller: and it follows the thumb. And the other is larger, and it is that which is placed under the smaller one at the lower part.
Therefore, perhaps only the larger bone is broken, or perhaps only the smaller one; and perhaps both are broken at the same time.
When therefore the upper part of the parenchyma is ruptured, its restoration is easy and its healing is more so.
And when the lower part of the parenchyma is ruptured, it is a severe fracture, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are broken at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is a lower parenchyma, then it is fitting for the physician, in its restoration, to apply an extension of the part with ease, until it recovers on its own.
If indeed the large parenchyma is that which is broken: then it is necessary to apply a stronger constriction with force.
And it is necessary to apply the hand's form in its restoration, and the extended extension above the pulse. And the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the other fingers; and the joints lower than all the other fingers, or remaining: and the weakened one sitting square on itself, and the pulse facing it, or straight in front of it, at an altitude so as not to incur weak labor.
Then the assistant should extend the arm from its lower part, or with their own hand, or with a bandage. And another assistant should spread it similarly above.
Then the surgeon should adjust the opening, until it reduces to a form more suitable according to his ability.
If there are jagged edges in the fracture of a bone, then try to reduce all the fragment to its place by your strength.
If indeed it appears separated with a jagged edge that pierces the skin and you have no hope of restoring it: then find above it and remove it according to the manner we have mentioned regarding those things that have been preceded.
However, if the fracture is with a wound, I have already dealt with that particular chapter, so take care of it from there.
If, during the beginning of your restoration, a fever occurs, then prepare a cloth with rose-colored oil and white wax, in the middle between thickness and thinness: and wrap the cloth tightly over it until the swelling is subsided; then remove the wax and apply a prepared plaster from ground meal with egg white. Then place the staples, and let the staple that is placed over the fracture be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of staples in various dispositions is six; either there is a fracture in one groove or in two grooves at once.
Then place your bandage over the stronger and more firm part of the fracture, and a little higher or lower than the site of the fracture, or place a softer bandage over it for a short time, as mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft, wet cloths and not hard or rough. And the thread used to tie it should be made of linen, of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we have mentioned.
And consider the member and ligament after days. If something happens that requires correction, such as a itching sensation in the member: then it is necessary to immerse the member in warm water until the itching subsides, and release the tight bandage until it is relieved, then repeat the bandaging.
If the bandage has already become softer, and the bone has already been removed, and similar things have occurred: then correct it all with your strength, and consider again.
For if the nourishment is blocked from reaching the member due to excess bandaging, then it is necessary to soften it a little for some time, and release it for a few days until it receives its nourishment, then tighten it again.
If, however, nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary for it to be resolved after twenty days or so.
Then the hand of the patient should be suspended over his neck. And his neck should be steady, and held firm against agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture will heal in thirty days, or perhaps in thirty-two days, and possibly even earlier, within twenty-eight days. All of this is according to the arrangements of the humors and the dispositions of strength.
## On the Healing of Fractures of the Hand
Know that a fracture of the hand's joints or the rare separation of the fingers' knots happens. And it does not happen to him unless there has been friction many times.
When therefore there occurs a fracture or contusion of the plantar part, then it is necessary that the patient be seated with his leg raised and in a position parallel to the ground. Then place his hand over it, extended.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones and adjust them until they are healed properly.
Then it is necessary for him to apply a plaster and bandage, unless there is a risk of a hot ulcer. Then place a splint over it according to the amount of space needed, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
However, if the fracture is around the lower part of the foot, then make a sphere-like shape out of cloth and especially instruct the patient to wrap it with his own fractured foot, then tie it tightly with a long piece of cloth.
And the splint should be made from skin, which has flexibility so that it can be applied with the skin of the foot, and tighten it as necessary.
However, if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place a splint above and another below the foot, and keep the hand open and straight. Then he applies a bandage as required by the hand, and extends it between the fingers with a ligament.
If any of the joints of the fingers should break, then if it is the thumb, it should be set as necessary. Then it is bound with the foot, and if necessary, a splint should be placed straight and firm so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
However, if the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or little finger, then it should be set and bound with the healthy finger that follows it, or all of them according to their position, and this is better. Or place a firm splint on top of it as we said regarding the thumb.
And consider in your recovery, and after this arrangement of the heat treatment, oppose it as necessary when something happens from it, according to the course of its narrative.
## On the Recovery of Hip Fractures.
The hipbone often breaks, and this is perceived by the senses because it turns towards the inside and rear part.
And its restoration is such that one ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another below the fracture. And the patient lies stretched out on his face.
Then he stretches every supporting ligament to its own side according to equal measure; and this is when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
However, if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then one ligament should be pulled towards the pelvis so that the extension goes upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if there is a fracture near the knee, then the ligament close to the knee should be adjusted so that the extension goes downwards.
Then the surgeon aligns the bone with both hands until it is reduced to its natural form; and the bone is joined together with a good union.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and tighten it, if there is no loss of member. If there is such a loss: then leave it for a few days until the wound becomes heated (or infected), after which resume your treatment.
The bandage should be adjusted as follows: You must cover the hard fracture with a bandage; you should wrap it twice or three times and leave excess. Then bandage the leg until you reach the heel near the root of the toe.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts, so that the ends of the thread come out from the upper part of both parts. Then roll up the excess from over the leg and hip.
Then place over the hip at the site of the fracture stars, and from them make one star on top of the leg bone.
Then fill the space between the leg and hip with soft cloth to ensure tightness.
Then begin with strong bandaging from the middle over the site of the fracture using three or four turns. And the more it is elongated with the ligament from the site of the fracture, the tighter and softer your bandage will be, and gentler.
Then turn towards the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the hip and leg. Therefore, tie it with both of the three stars that are above. Then insert with both ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the root of the foot. And tie both strings, too, at the ends of the stars from the other side, so that the ligature does not move from its place.
Then leave the ligature over that, as long as it remains, so that there is no corruption, nor ulceration, nor swelling, or similar things to them. If something of that happens. Therefore, with the removal of that, and straightening what happened from it, according to what we have said many times.
If there is indeed a fragment of bone that pierces through the bone, then you should, if possible, remove it completely. And if not, find it above and remove it, and take care of him who preceded in the remembrance until he is healed.
And when this fracture of the hip is constrained, besides adding stars to it as we said in the aid and in the arm.
However, in the restoration of such a severely broken limb, it does not cause the patient to limp. But if it is restored alone, apart from applying another crutch, then it is necessary for the one whose leg it is, to limp forever.
And know that the hip is tightened for fifty days, or added slightly, or reduced slightly; all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and the remaining dispositions.
**On the restoration of a broken knee joint.**
Know that the breaking of the knee joint rarely occurs, but it does experience friction many times.
If indeed it experiences a break, then not only is there a crack, but also a hole in its parts. And it is that, with or without a wound. And it stands over that whole with sensation.
And its restoration is such that it should be adjusted what has been disrupted from its parts with the fingers, until it is gathered and joined according to what is possible from balance and ease, and construction.
Then apply a plaster made of sesame flour or from a mill, mixed with egg white; and place over it a round pad if necessary, and tighten a corresponding ligature above it.
Then consider all the arrangements which we have described for the rest of the fractures, such as a hot apposition and similar ones, so that you oppose to every occurrence what is appropriate for it until healing takes place.
§ On the restoration of a fracture of the thigh.
Know that the thighs are two bones, of which one is thick and is called the thigh bone, and the other is thin and is called the femur.
And it happens that both kinds of fractures occur in each of the bones of the thigh, and because of this their restoration is done like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If both bones break simultaneously, the thigh turns into an invalid, affecting all parts.
And if the thinner bone breaks, the thigh turns into an invalid towards the front part.
And if the thicker bone breaks, you will find it below, and they are among those that will not be hidden from you.
Then he administers the extension, equalization, and ligation of the stars according to that equality.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a severe one, having many pieces, then there should be less extension and a gentler relief in your treatment.
And indeed, in the leg, there is something like a lever on the thigh, and it is just as when you equalize the stars and complete all your work, then take two strips of pine wood, which are used in making sun dials that are placed under tables: they are made from the palm of a hand, and choose those with a moderate thickness, not too small, and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee down.
Then wrap over each cloth with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other part on the other side, so that they extend from the knee down to the foot.
