Then tie the two strips at three places from both ends and the middle. For when the tightness is forbidden, the leg bends to the right and left, and stands steadily in place.
And indeed, a cradle should be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and the leg placed in it so that it preserves itself from movement.
And indeed, it is most necessary that you do this in a fracture when there is a proper wound therein.
Then consider the wound every day, and study with it with utmost diligence, from ulceration, or swelling, or any other conditions. If therefore something happens from these, then oppose it with what is necessary until it heals, if God wills.
On the fracture of bones of the foot and toes.
A calcaneal fracture does not occur completely.
To the bones of the foot indeed occurs a fracture, and to the toes a fracture occurs rarely: and it does not occur to them unless there is friction in many positions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs, and you see these bones rising up above each other again, then the patient should put his foot on prepared ground as if walking.
Then stand up, and put your foot on whatever raises these bones; then press, or tread on it; and equalize it, until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster and a poultice on top of it. Place a thin board under the sole of the foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it tightly with the foot wrapped neatly, after you have covered it with linen and other things it needs.
So, when they have passed three or four days, then untie the ligature. For when you find the bones are either equal [in length], or broken, or out of joint. Therefore, the operation on them is as you see.
However, if some of the fingers are broken, then restore them, and make them equal according to what I have told you about restoring the fingers of the hand. Then place your finger as an asterisk according to the length of the finger, and make it a little wider; then place this board under the foot, which I have told you about, and tie it neatly with a strap.
If indeed it is that two, three, or more bones are broken, then place over every finger an asterisk of its amount, wrapped in linen cloth; and tie the board to the lower part of the foot, and let it stand on one end of the table's cap outside the foot's vent, so as to hold good retention.
And it comes to pass that you do not forget, but consider all that I have told you about the other fractures from accidents that we mentioned, and oppose every accident with what is necessary.
On the fracture of the woman's vulva, the ribs, and the man's penis.
When the woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit with her legs apart. Then lean her slightly towards the back part of her own body, and hold her rearward.
Then the midwife should fill her vulva with cotton until it is full, and let it be like a sphere.
Then the woman should be made to cough, and slowly raise her back. That cotton will emerge, until the vulva is made to look like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns.
Then place a small piece of cloth on her back. When she feels the need to defecate, remove the cloth with ease, then let her do her business, and bring it out according to the manner in which you first inserted it; then return to her original position.
He should do that in seven days, or approximately, for the bone itself is restored.
And if it is still alive, take a wax-covered thread and tie it tightly over the opening of its wound, and insert the entire needle into its flesh. Then blow into the needle with strength until the thread straightens inside the flesh; for indeed, the fracture is healed.
Then it is wrapped with cloth and left for several days, as we have said, until it heals.
And when a man's chest bone, or a woman's, is broken, then apply for its restoration, and use the same method we have described for the bone of the anchor.
And let the straightening of these close fractures not be hidden from one who is learned or skilled.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself shows you the way to a straight path in restoring and tightening it. Therefore, understand.
But when a man's staff is broken, take the neck of a goose, and insert the staff into it. Then place a poultice on top of it and leave it to absorb for three or four days, and it will heal if God wills.
On the restoration of a bone fracture when it is accompanied by a wound.
It is advisable for anyone who experiences a fracture with a wound, especially if it is a large bone such as the hipbone or the femur or similar bones, to immediately seek help from phlebotomists. If you are familiar with the procedures of phlebotomy, as we have mentioned, then follow these steps:
If the wound bleeds, then it is necessary to have someone cut it open, so that the pus accumulated on top of it can be drained; if you do not have another option.
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day; do not delay it unless there is no risk of complications.
If indeed there is a fever, then postpone the restoration of the fracture until the ninth day, unless the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, lest you cause it to take a turn for the worse.
If, however, a broken bone protrudes above the skin, you must then strive to reduce it and equalize it with your hands easily and with slight extension.
If, however, sufficient extension cannot be prepared and the bone cannot be equalized with your hands, then reduce it using this instrument. And there is an instrument made of iron that is seven or eight fingers in length and as wide as the wound. And for that reason, the physician must take three or four of them, according to the amount needed from each kind of treatment in every type of fracture.
And it should be round, and have a slight thickness so that it does not double up at the time of compression during surgery, and its ends should be sharp with a margin at their extremes. And it should be, from the middle of its thickness up to its lower part, in a slanting manner.
And this is its form.
In this operation, it is necessary to place the sharp end against the prominent extremity of the bone, and push it with one blow until the bone returns and is equalized by itself.
Therefore, strive to equalize and align the ends of the fracture.
If the ends of the fracture are fine and do not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary to cut the end of the bone with a chisel until the instrument has a firm grip.
If you cannot reduce them at all with what we have mentioned, then cut it with the instrument that is suitable for cutting, as we have said; or saw it with any saw that is possible for you to use.
Then smooth the remaining part that is on the bone, due to its roughness and fine chips.
When the bone is reduced and experiences a sudden severe pain after its reduction, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
Do that. Indeed, you provide great assistance to the weak when your restoration of the bone is complete.
Then immerse the cloth in black, astringent wine. And use it appropriately in the summer. And do not place any yellow substance over a wound, nor anything that contains oil, so as not to cause putrefaction and corruption within it.
Then administer stars at the appropriate time for the restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed: so that you may cut with scissors a hole into the inflammation according to the amount of the wound.
And take every care not to strain the wound with a fracture. Many times foolish doctors have done this: and they have caused harm to their patients or death, or putrefaction, or gangrene, that is, loss of blood. And let your traction be soft, pliable, unlike the hard tractions of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and there is some bad event as we described, and you feel pain at the suspended place, then it is not necessary to put stars over it. And make incisions on them with hard bands at the place of the stars, and tie them tightly to it.
However, if it has been one day or two, and you see that the wound is beginning to form pus, then remove the cloth you placed over it with wine. Then apply poultices and ointments that we usually use to treat wounds, such as the four-ingredient ointment and others like it.
And it often happens that you need to loosen the ligature. And consider the wound every day at morning and evening, until it hardens and heals.
And it is necessary that the member be prepared so that pus can drain from it easily downward.
If many days have passed since the wound, and it does not harden nor does pus drain from it, then know that there are seriously small bones there. Therefore, it is necessary that you examine the wound with care. Indeed, as many of those small bones become separated, remove them, and extract the entire one. And those which are not separated from the others and pierce the member, causing pain, try to cut them off, and remove them in every way possible for you to do so.
If a wound becomes infected or gangrenous, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction arises, then it is necessary to oppose each of these with what is appropriate for its cure, which has been previously described in its chapter.
And from this, where it is necessary for my speech to stand firm, and when you make your present mind when a bone breaks and protrudes beyond the member, such as the hip joint, and similar parts of large members, do not presume its attraction, for often death results from it, indeed, let go of it until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will disappear of itself after twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, cure the wound if it seems suitable to you, otherwise, let it be.
**On the Cure of the Nodule at the Site of a Fracture.**
This nodule often occurs at the site of healing a fracture. Especially, when it occurs near joints, and it corrupts the form of the member. And perhaps it may prohibit the member from its natural operation.
Therefore, at that time, if the swelling is recent, administer medications that have a drying effect, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar substances.
So, take some of these, or all of them, and mix them with astringent wine, or with albumen, or with vinegar, and apply them to the swelling in a poultice, and bind them tightly over the affected area.
And do not remove the bandage for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another one, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind a sheet of lead tightly over it. Lead, indeed, has the property of drawing out whatever is feared from the member.
If however the swelling has already turned into a stone, and it is hard and tight, and urgent need requires its removal: then find a way to remove it from its upper part, and cut off the protruding excess; or rasp it off with rasors until it is removed, and take care of it until it heals.
On healing when a member is restored and remains after that in a subtle state, not according to its own nature.
When a fracture of a bone is restored and the member remains after that in a subtle, weak state, then it does not become healed except because of many causes. One of these is the excessive solution and binding of the ligament, not according to what is needed; either because of an excess of tightness in the ligaments, preventing nutrition from reaching the member; or because of too many applications at the wrong time; or due to a lack of blood in a weak body, and its debilitation.
And the treatment for this is to feed the weak patient and make his body strong, until blood increases in it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness over him.
Then place a poultice on the member, so that it attracts the poultice to itself, drawing much nutrition to it. And he should continue to apply the hot water bath until nutrition flows and it returns to its natural form.
On the cure of fractured bones when they are healed in a twisted manner.
When a member, having been already restored after its healing, presents itself in a twisted manner or with prominent fractured bones or some deformity, and because of this form the member is disfigured, yet it does not prevent the member from performing its natural functions; then it is not necessary to accept his claim, who considers that the member should be broken again.
And indeed, there have been many foolish doctors and healers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed reprehensible, leading to death or a great fear, as it is mortal.
Indeed, if the twisting and deformity are recent: then it is necessary to immerse it in warm water with herbs that soften, such as almond leaves, and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And let a plaster be made with a softening paste, such as a properly made poultice.
Or take the mucus of the almond root, and mix it with chicken fat, or chickpea oil, and wrap it up with that.
They may be enriched with fat-soluble figs and rubbed with columbine lard or other similar preparations that reduce scarring.
And sometimes the knot is loosened by persistent friction, old and gentle, which is done with moist hands. And the movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
However, if deformity has already occurred and it has become strong and hardened into a stone, and the need for treatment arises with a knife: then it is necessary to cut off its upper part and dissolve the continuity of the bone and cut away what exceeds from the knot or bone using delicate scissors: and ease should be provided in that place with care and diligence.
**On the Discussion of Dislocation**
Dislocation is the separation of any joint from its position. Therefore it is forbidden from its own motion, and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes painful accidents and severe injuries to the weak.
When someone experiences a dislocation, it is necessary to hurry to restore it; nor should there be any delay at all.
For if there is delay, the place should be changed, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
For this reason, it is not necessary for him to be moved, nor should he be extended in the time of his change of place. Because it often causes spasms to occur, and painful troubles.
Indeed, when that happens, then it is necessary to prepare for bloodletting by the nurse. Then he is released until the dislocation has healed.
Then the member is bathed with warm water and oil. Then it is reduced with ease.
And all the members should be treated as soon as the memory of their condition returns to its place.
I have indeed already outlined the chapters on dislocations, even according to what has come before in the fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower parts.
On the treatment of mandible dislocation.
The two mandibles rarely dislocate unless, occasionally, they do.
And the dislocation of both [mandibles] occurs according to one of two methods. Or they are indeed removed from their places by a slight dislocation, and they become soft for a little while. Or they may dislocate completely and entirely, until they become soft towards the chest, until the saliva is released, and it cannot hold itself firm, nor can it close its own jaw, and its tongue is drawn in with speech.
When, however, the dislocation is slight, then it returns to many positions on its own with little effort.
If it is indeed a complete, entire dislocation: then it is necessary that its reduction be done quickly, and not delayed thoroughly: and it is as if it should hold the minister's finger below the chin: and the physician should insert one of his hands into the root of the mandible dislocation between the jaw and the tooth, if the dislocation is from one side. If a dislocation occurs in both parts, he should insert both his thumbs simultaneously. And the rest of his hand's fingers should be outside as he requires.
And especially instruct the patient to clean his jaws, and bring them back to normal for chewing on both sides. And the doctor should adjust the jaw, and gently push it back to its normal position until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce it, especially if both jaws are involved simultaneously, then apply a hot water and oil poultice until the reduction becomes easy; never delay reducing the jaws altogether, as we have said.
When they return and align, and the patient's mouth is closed, and they do not open: then place small cloth poultices on them, coated with a paste made from beeswax and rose oil.
Then it should be tied gently with soft ligatures.
And the patient should sleep on his back, with his head elevated between two pillows to prevent him from moving it to the right or left, and he should not try to chew anything or even think about chewing; instead, a soft liquid diet should be provided until the pain subsides and the jaw is flexible. Then he may eat what he likes and swallow it easily.
It should not be difficult to perform surgery on one's own mouth while eating and drinking, and chewing, until the jawbone is detached and healed.
If the reduction of the two jawbones becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some point and do not return to their places, then often fever occurs from there, and persistent vomiting; and perhaps the internal organs are damaged, and perhaps pure cholera is expelled.
So if you see that (the condition) becomes like this, then know that it is a loss, and often he who experiences that dies within ten days.
**On the Reduction of Shoulder Dislocation and the End of the Humerus**
The shoulder blade (furcula) does not dislocate inwardly from its inner side due to its connection with the chest. And it (the shoulder blade) dislocates outward sometimes, and that is indicated to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lies on his back and extends his arms. Then it (the shoulder blade) shortens the space with your strong contraction of the hand, for it will return on its own.
Then place a plaster, and some wool, and bind it.
The extremities indeed, which follow the cubitus, and continue with it, are not dislocated unless rarely so. If it becomes dislocated at any time, then it must be reduced and set in the position that we have said, and what you have prepared.
Then place a plaster, and some wool, and bind it. And especially instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with the same treatment, the end of the arm is reduced when it has been separated from its place as well.
## On the Reduction of Shoulder Dislocation
Know that the shoulder (Humerus) is not dislocated unless according to three modes.
Of which one is that it becomes dislocated towards the lower part of the side (titillicus). The second [method] is to dislocate it towards a part of the chest. And perhaps it will dislocate towards the upper part of the humerus, and that becomes rare.
And it does not dislocate towards the rear, because of the spatula. And it does not dislocate towards the front, because of the nerves.
And indeed, it dislocates and emerges most towards the lower part of the thigh, especially in those whose flesh is scarce. Because it emerges swiftly in those [parts], and enters swiftly.
However, in those whose flesh is abundant, the opposite occurs; that is, it emerges with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men through a blow or accident, and the humerus is dislocated at the joint with heat, and one thinks that's because it's already dislocated.
Therefore, one must experience that, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And a dislocation is known when it affects the lower joints; and you distinguish between the dislocated humerus and the healthy humerus. For you perceive a clear difference between them, and you notice a weakness in the head of the humerus, and near the joint, the head of the humerus feels as if it is another bone; and the infirm person cannot bring their hand back to its own side, nor move it through all its motions.
And similarly, if the joint of the chest or the upper part is dislocated, then you will notice a clear sensation, and it will not be hidden.
And indeed, this dislocation is easily reduced when it is recent or if the patient is a young child.
And its reduction is such that it lifts the patient's hand towards the upper part. Then place your thumbs under the joint and lift the connection with strength towards its proper place; and the patient will raise their hand and extend it upwards. Then place it downwards; for indeed it returns quickly.
If, however, it does not return as we said, and there have been many days since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an application made which softens and relaxes, such as when roots of high quality, fennel, or melilot are steeped in water and applied.
Next, the patient should be placed face up on his back, and under his armpit is placed a sphere and wool that are equal between relaxation and tension.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere and raise the head of the humerus with force, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if he struggles, bring him back in this manner: and it is as if you make the present man who is stronger than the patient, and stands on his side, and inserts his elbow under the patient's armpit; then lift the armpit towards the upper part until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand towards the lower part of his chest. If however the patient is weak, then something else should be suspended with him in order to make him heavier; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is brought down in another manner as well; and a long wooden beam should be fixed in the ground, whose head is round like the handle of a mortar; not too thick nor too thin. Then a cloth should be placed under the patient after it is laid over his head, and the patient should stand according to the length of the wood.
Then he should extend his hand towards the lower part on the other side, and extend his body from the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If his reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this care: and it is as if you take a wood whose length is the amount of two arms, and its width is the amount of four fingers, and its thickness is the amount of two fingers; which has a round head to facilitate its entry into the deep part of the wound, in this form.
Then cloth should be laid over the round head so as not to harm the wooden patient.
Then place the patient under the wound, so that he extends his hand or arm over the wood until below; and bind the wood over the helper and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then place the arm over the step according to its width, and extend the hand below, and let go of the remaining body suspended on the other side. The joint enters immediately.
Thus, when the opening is completed in any way that is possible, it is then necessary to place beneath it a sphere of an equal amount made from wool. Then apply the prepared plaster from ground wool mixed with myrrh and egg white over the entire upper arm as required.
Then tighten the bandage properly; and the ligament is wrapped over the plaster above; and the hand is attached to the neck and released, so that the hand does not move for seven days.
And it is necessary to give light food to the weak patient until the member is strengthened, for this aids its healing.
Then a larger bandage is applied after seven or five days, and it is removed with movement. If it is firmly established and does not relax, then it is already healed.
If the dislocation occurs repeatedly due to moisture or for another reason, then cautery with three incisions should be administered according to what is mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not secured, and the plaster and bandage are removed several times, but do not become firm, and fall off, and cannot raise itself up to its original position; then know that the nerves of the shoulder have been cut, or stretched, or relaxed; and know that the joint will never be restored to its place.
However, when dislocation occurs against the chest and breast, or when it is reduced to the rear, this is done with a push and extension using the hands; until it returns, and the remaining tension and care are administered, until healing occurs.
If, after healing, a severe condition occurs in the member and there is delay in its motion, then the patient should use baths frequently until that severity is alleviated and it returns to its original natural state.
**On the healing of elbow dislocation.**
You should know that the elbow joint can become dislocated with difficulty, and likewise, it returns to its place with difficulty; indeed, it dislocates towards all parts, particularly to the front or rear.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it falls under pressure and touch, according to whatever form it takes. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, that becomes clearly evident. The joint is then secured, and the arm cannot be doubled or touched with its own humerus.
And it is necessary that you move towards reducing the dislocation immediately, before a hot apoplessia occurs to it.
For if a hot apoplessia occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not be cured at all, especially when it affects the posterior parts. It is indeed the worst of all types of dislocations, and causes more severe pain; and often leads to death with it.
And its restoration when it is possible to reduce it is, as he extends his hand with both hands: and his arm is expanded, and the doctor's hand is above his elbow and under it, and he pushes the joint with his thumbs simultaneously, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is to the front, then it returns so that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes the elbow which is dislocated with the root of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey to be reduced, then apply a strong, steady extension; and it is as if both arms of the assistants are extended: and even two assistants hold it, so that it does not move during the extension.
Then the arm is moved to all parts after it has been covered with a doubled cloth or a wide ligament.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should grease it with oil so that it becomes from those things that aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should push the joint with a firm impulse until it returns to its place.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place a plaster on top of it, which has a constricting and drying effect with alum, and the tightness should be adjusted appropriately. And the arm should be suspended from the neck of the patient, and leave it for several days.
Then it is removed. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then loosen the ligature from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not tightly bound, then repeat the plaster and ligature, and leave it for several days until it tightens. Then remove it.
However, if there is a serious problem after the reduction of the joint and its position is in motion, then apply moisture to the bath, and light friction, and compression until it is lined or place a hot compress on the affected joint, that is, a pinged arrow poultice. Then it is bound, and left upon her day and night. Then remove it and insert the same into a bath. When, therefore, it is sweating, then fear joins friction with moderate pressure. Then repeat over the same the application of the aloe once, and twice, and three times with the entrance into the bath, until it becomes soft.
And if it is necessary, place upon it a hot, moist, greasy compress and tighten it over the same.
Do that several times. It becomes soft and returns to its original position.
§ On the treatment of dislocation according to Al-Mahashem.
Al-Mahashem frequently dislocates the hand, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. In contrast, the remaining joints are connected. However, it is necessary that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation at the time when it becomes dislocated, before the place leaves; or else a hot ulcer occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation is such that you place your weaker hand on the table and have the assistant stretch out his hand, and the doctor places his palm on top of the joint and pushes it until it returns to its place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already retracted into the hand; then the patient should place the back of his hand on the table at the time of extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand should be such that the inner part of his hand is placed on the table, so that the doctor's hand can fall upon the very top of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster to prevent the ulcer from spreading, and release it, without repeating this.
For if it does not endure, nor can you reduce it further after the passage of days, but the joint remains according to its deformity, and it causes the patient no pain except that the hand becomes soft, and he cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed or rubbed raw.
There is no skill in it other than to bind with a ligature near the case (wound). And perhaps it does not contribute anything.
If, therefore, it does not return as Almahassem described, then place an ointment (dressing) over it as we have mentioned. Then it should be bound and left for five days; after that, it should be opened and left, and the hand (or wound) should be exercised.
If, however, its (the wound's) movement is restricted and something grave happens: then it should be relaxed with warm water and friction several times until it becomes relaxed.
**On the treatment of finger dislocation.**
When a finger is dislocated from its joint in any direction, towards the back of the hand or between its joints, then extend (the finger) and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to its place.
Then support the finger's head, and hang it towards the side where it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it becomes straight in its own time.
When evening comes, bind it according to that very description; and do not cease to loosen it during the day; and exercise it with motion; and bind it at night.
Do that for several days until it is tightened.
And do likewise with it if it becomes dislocated at the wrist; bind it to that very part, and perform your first operation with it until it is healed.
And do likewise with it when it becomes dislocated at other parts.
**On the healing of spinal joint dislocation.**
When anyone suffers from a complete dislocation of the spine or the vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death is approaching the sick person.
And the sign of it is that the sick person's strength departs without will, and he cannot hold it; and often some of its members become weak, or both legs, or both arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is removed from its place.
Many times that which is removed happens to be separated from those parts four times.
Therefore, that which is removed towards the rear is called gibbousness.
The treatment of it is that you consider at that time if gibbousness occurs from childhood: then there is no cure for it, nor is there any recovery from it at all.
For those that occur due to accident or impact, or similar causes, the ancient methods of treatment have been discussed at length. Much of it does not return with success.
I will now briefly depart from that topic, which requires little excuse given its proximity to my intent; and with the exposition I have provided regarding it, and the various ways in which the instrument was shaped, as well as the demonstration of itself, and its application.
Therefore, I say that in the gibbousness that occurs from the anterior part, there is no skill, nor is there recovery from it.
And similarly, for that which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not treated from them, unless what happens in his own back, because of what I am telling: and it is as if the sick is extended over his face on an equal level with the floor near the wall, and a humid cloth is spread under him, so that his chest is not harmed.
Then a straight board is placed fixed in the fire on the floor at the foot of his head and another board around his feet at the other end of the foot, and the attendant holds the board, and it is not fastened in the fire. And another attendant holds the other.
Then he wraps over the head of the sick person a soft cloth, and fastens it under his armpits firmly, and extends the end of the cloth to the board that is near his head, and ties him to it.
Then he tightens another cloth over his thighs, and above his knees, and near his feet. Then he combines all the cloths, and ties them to the other board that is near his feet.
Then all the attendants stretch out towards the part of the board with the cloths, and do not move the two boards from their places where they are fixed; however, they are not fastened, as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with strength until it returns, or place a board on top of it. Then he attaches his feet over the board until it returns.
If, however, it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms' breadth. And he should choose the place on the wall, as we said, that is near the weak point, where the end of the board will enter. Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And the physician should place his feet on the other end. And he should tie it well until the spine is tightened, and return to its place.
Then, if you wish, use a drill that is inserted with your hand: and it is as if you fix two wooden sticks in the ground near the head of the weak foot, whose length is one arm's breadth, and two more near its head.
And the length between all the sticks is one palm; and now a hole has been made at every place where the drill runs: and every stick is firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And a round stick should be inserted; and it is a turn in which the ligament is revolved through two holes of the two sticks.
And at the end of it there is a hole, in which the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm with which it revolves; and similarly in the other two sticks.
Then tighten the ligaments which you have tightened in the lower chest around the lower turn which is near its head, and the ligaments which you have tightened in its legs around the turn which is near its feet.
Then stand beside each turn that moves the hand with a tie, with which the turn revolves; and the physician adjusts the spine, as we have said.
This is the form of the turn that follows.
Then after the spine returns and the place is adjusted, then you should put a dry plaster with alum and eggwhite. Then apply the poultice. Afterwards, place above the plaster a star-shaped pad from wood, whose width is three fingers, or approximately those dimensions. And its length is the amount that takes up the space of the hernia, and beyond the healthy part of the spine.
Then it is tied with the ligament that we have mentioned. And it should be nourished with delicate food until it is healed.
If there remains something of prominence in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that remedies that soften and relax be administered, along with the application of a poultice as we described in detail during a long time. And now a sheet of lead is administered in that place.
And sometimes there occurs a prominence in the final part of the spine, and it is thought to be due to dislocation, and that bone which already protrudes. Therefore it is not necessary to make such an assumption with this medicine; perhaps indeed it will cause death to occur.
## On the Treatment of a Dislocated Ankle
Know that the ankle and shoulder joints do not become dislocated except due to displacement alone; nor does it happen to them what happens to other joints from a slight removal and pressure.
Indeed, the joint of the anchor is dislocated according to four parts. This is because it is dislocated towards the front and back.
And it is most dislocated towards the interior, and slightly dislocated towards the front and back.
And the treatment for the dislocation towards the interior is as follows: because when you join the healthy leg to the injured one, it is longer; and the knee of the injured leg stands higher than that of the healthy one, and the injured person cannot bend their foot at the groin; and there is a place that follows the groin after the limb is straightened due to heat, because the head of the thigh has already reached there.
And the sign of one who experiences a dislocation towards the exterior is that it is contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of one who experiences a dislocation towards the front is that they stretch their healthy leg to its limit; however, they cannot bend it without pain in the knee; and if they try to walk, they cannot go beyond this point; and they retain urine, and their groin becomes stiff, and during walking, they place weight on their heel.
And the sign of one who experiences a dislocation towards the back is that they do not stretch their leg, and cannot bend it until their groin doubles in size; and their leg is also shorter on the affected side; and the groin is relaxed, and the head of the thigh is at the location mentioned.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is such that you must consider it at that time, if the dislocation is old and has become worn with the passage of time. Then, if you perform the reduction of it and it remains according to its own arrangement, then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary for a complete assumption to be made about it.
However, in the case of a recent dislocation, which belongs to one of the four types of dislocations, you should act appropriately to restore the joint, and stretch it towards the interior and exterior, and move it with your right and left hand: perhaps it will return on its own and no other treatment will be needed.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong helper, and stretch the leg from its lower part: either with their hands or with a bandage that is tied above the knee: and another assistant extends their hand from above, so as to insert their hands beneath his armpits. Then, tie a loose bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And the extension should be either from the front part of the groin to the part of the femur, or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let their extension be with one motion until the patient is lifted from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is the common type among the four kinds of species.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we said, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary for each kind to use that which I will say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it, indeed, when the dislocation affects internal parts, it is necessary that the patient be placed on his healthy side. Then he should place the ligament over the root of the hip, where it is between the hip joint and the space under the groin. Then extend the ligament upwards from the groin area towards the higher part of the body, towards the part of the fork (of the hip bone).
Then have another assistant take the patient's strong arm and narrow the large space of the weak hip joint, and extend it with a steady motion towards the external extension. For I return it to its place.
And this kind is the easiest among the rest of the cure kinds, through which this member is reduced.
If, however, it turns away from you and does not obey you for entry with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary that both weak feet be bound simultaneously with a strong ligature, loose over the two calcaneuses and over the two knees; and the length of each one should be from its own comparison to the quantity of four fingers; and the weak leg should be extended more than the other by the amount of two fingers.
Then the weak one should be suspended over the head of the log, which is in the house; and its length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
Then command the strong young one to strengthen the head of the thigh; and hang another weak one with the strong one; and push the strong one, strengthening the thigh with force, for the connection returns to its place quickly.
In its return, however, when the dislocation goes to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lies on a bed, according to what we have mentioned in the case of hunchback. And the ligature should be tied over its weak leg, properly, and also over its chest.
Then two logs should be placed, one at his feet and another at the head of him. Then the additional log should be placed in the middle of the foot, fixed well; over which wet cloths should be laid to not harm the weak one; and the log should be between his thighs so as not to be pulled down towards the lower part during extension.
Then each instrument should be extended to its own part, and the surgeon should equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place a board over it and press it equally, as we said regarding the hump. However, it is necessary for the sick person to lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary to quickly extend the sick person's leg, and according to this same description on the bed: and the surgeon should place his right hand's palm over the sick person's inguinal area. Then it tightens itself with the other hand, and with that tightening, place the extended hip towards the lower part of the leg.
And if the dislocation is towards the back, then it is not necessary for the sick person to be extended downwards and lifted up, but rather he should be placed on a hard surface, as it is also done when the wide dislocation is towards the exterior, according to what we said about his position on two surfaces: and it lies on its face, with its ligaments extended, according to what we just said.
And it is necessary that a compression be administered with a board over the place to which the operation extends, when the reduction is completed according as it should be.
And you must not overlook the sign of its return: and it is as if you stretch out the two weak legs; and when you see that they are equal, and the weak one tightens its leg and expands itself without straining, then you know that the member has returned according to what was needed; then join the two thighs, and apply a plaster, and bind with a bandage, so as not to move widely to any part, and let the patient adhere to rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligature, and remove the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; and when you see that they are equal in position, then you know that the dislocation is now fixed. Therefore release the patient to walk.
And if you see any sign of softening: then return, and apply that plaster, and bind it according to your first tightening, and release it after three days as well.
Then loosen it and make the healing take place slowly in walking on each for several days until it is well strengthened.
§ On the dislocation of the knee.
The knee dislocates in three ways. It dislocates outwardly, inwardly, and downward, that is, to the rear.
And it does not dislocate toward the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is that it causes the patient to bend their leg towards their chest. If, however, it does not adhere to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all three kinds of dislocation is to make the patient sit upright, so that they can extend their leg as much as possible towards it and attach the back of their thigh to the front of yours; and place their foot between your feet.
Then make the knee adhere to your palms, and place them straight between your fingers above the knee. Then apply your palm to both sides of the knee with force. And have another assistant stretch their leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of healing is that the foot adheres to the thigh gently, not painfully.
Then apply a hot poultice, and make the foot adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with bandages for three or four days. Then loosen them.
And the invalid should not walk for several days until he is healed.
If, however, you do not achieve the return of the limb, then administer a strong extension with ligatures, whose method was previously described in the treatment of the ankle, until it returns.
**On the treatment of calcaneal dislocation**
The calcaneus is sometimes removed by a slight displacement, and sometimes it is dislocated in its full range. And its dislocation occurs either towards the interior or towards the exterior.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you will see the calcaneum (heel bone) inflated, extending towards the part to which it is dislocated.
However, the treatment for its reduction is easy: it should be extended with ease using the hands and then adjusted until it returns to its proper place.
However, in its treatment when it is dislocated towards the second position, you should make the infirm sit upright, and have a strong assistant hold them firmly from the rear part of their back, in the middle.
Then hold the foot with your right hand from its upper part, and with your left hand from its lower part, at the level of the toe joint.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with your left hand, using two fingers.
Then extend your left hand and push the sole of the foot with your right hand towards the shin without effort.
Do that twice as we have described.
Then push the sole of the foot towards the shin in the third position, and pull the toe joint.
If it returns to its original state once or twice in this manner and you see the foot is even, that is good. If not, then repeat the operation on it, for it recovers.
However, if you are prevented from reducing it in the manner described, then lay the patient down on his back on the ground and fix a very firm pillow on the ground between his thighs so that he falls into a comfortable position; and you have already covered him with a cloth to avoid hurting him.
Then the assistant should take hold of the patient's thigh; afterwards, another assistant extends the leg, either with their hands or using a bandage that is tied around the collar of the foot. Then every assistant should extend it against the extension of his counterpart: and a pillow should stand between the two thighs of the patient, holding him in place so that his body does not tilt downwards during the stretch.
Then the physician should reduce the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy leg down towards the ground. The dislocation returns with speed.
When the dislocation returns and its restoration is declared to you, then apply a plaster and bandage, tighten it firmly with bands, and tie the leg with a bandage to the shin bone.
And it is necessary that you be careful or fear of the nerves that are above the rear ankle, so that no ligament over them prevails and injures them.
Then allow it to rest for two or three days. If the ligament becomes relaxed, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day and forbid the disabled person from walking for forty days. If indeed he tries to walk before that space is filled, there is no security unless the dislocation dissolves and heals, and it does not receive treatment afterwards.
If a fever occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to his sedative that we mentioned earlier in another place concerning treatment, and embrocation until the fever disappears.
**On the Dislocation of the Toe Joints, and Treatment.**
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them with a slight extension without effort be corrected; and this is not difficult but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in any of the knots of the foot, then it is necessary that the disabled person sit on his foot over an equal place to the ground or on a board and stands as if walking.
Then stand you, and place your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it firmly with your foot until it returns to its original position, and you will see that the swelling has gone down and no longer appears prominent.
Then place under the foot a board which will take the whole foot, having two ends. Then tighten this restriction for three days. Then loosen it, and make it rest from walking for many days until it is tightened and secured by the reduction.
§ On the kinds of dislocation with the ulna or fracture.
When something of this sort occurs and you attempt its cure and restoration, death often follows many times over: and for that reason one should not presume to undertake such care unless one is skilled in the art, merciful, pious, slow to act, not rash, nor presumptuous: and use only initial medicines which provide merely temporary relief from heat. And let the sick be committed to divine judgment. Unless you have hope, or expect salvation from destruction through the gentleness of illness, and where something of hope appears. Therefore, try to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the member returns to its original state, according to what we wish, then administer treatment to soothe the abscess: and care for the wound with what is appropriate for it from dried ointments.
If there is a dislocation with a fracture, and these occur in separated bones of the leg, then try to remove them, and do in that what we said about simple illnesses from what preceded in those places.
And be careful according to your strength, and guard yourself from the entrance of fear: as my previous prediction to you has said.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more beneficial to your body among them during your time.
